Messengers Establish Divine Speech
Argues that establishing divine speech depends on the possibility of messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of sending messengers establishes divine speech, command, and prohibition.

Core Idea
Messengers prove that God speaks, commands, and prohibits.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology: the very possibility of prophethood necessitates that God possesses speech. The author links the concepts of messenger and divine command, showing that denying God's speech leads to denying prophethood. Let's explore how he builds this connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and its English translation carefully. Notice how the author presents a conditional argument linking speech, messengers, and commands.
2
Identify the key conditional link
The passage states that establishing speech, command, and prohibition is built on the possibility of sending messengers. Identify how denying speech leads to denying the sending of messengers.
3
Understand the refutation of the alternative view
Some opponents interpret divine commands as mere acts indicating will. The author argues that such an act still implies requirement and judgment, not mere wishful thinking, thus conceding the point.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The meaning conveyed by the messenger's act is what is called speech, command, and prohibition according to Islamic law. Reflect on how this argument ties prophethood to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إثبات الكلام والأمر والنهي يبني على جواز انبعاث الرسل، فإن من أحال كونه متكلما يلزمه أن ينكر (4) كونه مرسلا ،رسولا ومن جوز على الله تعالى أن يبعث رسولا فذلك الرسول يبلغ رسالات الله لا محالة، ورسالاته أوامره ونواهيه، وحملها على فعل يفعله في محل بحيث يفهم من ذلك الفعل أنه يريد من المكلف فعلا ويكره فعلا تسليم المسألة، فإن تلك الإرادة التي تتعلق بفعل الغير حتى يفعله إرادة تضمنت اقتضاء وحكماً، وإلا كانت تمنيًا وتشهيّا، وذلك الذي يسمى أمرا ونهيًا، وسميتموه إرادة وكراهية ) ، فإذا مدلول ذلك الفعل الذي أشاروا إليه هو الذي نسميه الشرع كلاما وأمرًا ونهيًا.
We say: The establishment of speech (كلام), command (أمر), and prohibition (نهي) is based on the possibility of sending messengers. For whoever considers it impossible that He be speaking, it follows that he denies that He is a sender of messengers. And whoever considers it possible for God, exalted, to send a messenger, then that messenger conveys the messages of God without doubt, and His messages are His commands and prohibitions. To interpret them as an act He does in a place such that one understands from that act that He wills the obliged to do an action and dislikes an action is to concede the issue. For that will that relates to the action of another until he does it is a will that includes requirement and judgment; otherwise it would be wishful thinking and desire. And that is what is called command and prohibition, and you have called it will and dislike. Thus, the meaning of that act to which they refer is what we call according to the law (شرع) speech, command, and prohibition.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage argues that divine speech, command, and prohibition are established by the very fact that God can send messengers. If one denies that God speaks, one must also deny that He sends messengers. But if one accepts the possibility of sending messengers, then those messengers convey God's messages, which are commands and prohibitions. Some opponents interpret these as mere acts that convey will or dislike. The author argues that such an interpretation still concedes the point because that will includes requirement and judgment, not mere wish. What is conveyed is precisely what the author calls speech, command, and prohibition.

Key Terms

انبعاث الرسل
مرسل

رسالات
إرادة
اقتضاء
حكم
تمني
تشهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine commands are reducible to God's will without being distinct speech acts.
Correction: The author shows that the act understood as indicating will actually includes requirement and judgment, which is what we call command and prohibition—thus it is not mere will but speech.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for establishing divine speech, command, and prohibition?
A) The possibility of sending messengers
B) The occurrence of miracles
C) The consensus of scholars
D) Logical necessity

Key Takeaway
The possibility of sending messengers proves that God speaks, commands, and prohibits, and these are not reducible to mere will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God's commands and prohibitions are personal speech addressed to you, requiring a real response, or do you treat them as impersonal guidelines you can negotiate? What would need to change in your daily decisions if you took them as the very words of God?
?
When you face a command from the Qur'an or Sunnah, do you sometimes rationalize it away as merely 'God's general will' rather than a direct, binding order? How can you train yourself to hear His speech in each command, not just an abstract wish?
?
If you believed that every command from God is a real act of speech from Him, not just a reflection of His will, would you be more attentive and immediate in your obedience? What is one command you have been delaying or ignoring because you treated it as less than speech?

The Inability of Mutakallimun to Explain the Spirit's Manifestation
The author argues that the mutakallim cannot coherently explain the spirit's manifestation in human form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun's metaphysical framework cannot coherently explain the miraculous manifestation of spiritual beings in physical form, and how this failure extends to understanding divine communication in human languages.

Core Idea
The mutakallim's explanations for spiritual manifestation are either annihilation or reincarnation—neither is genuine manifestation.

The author challenges the mutakallimun to account for the miracle of the spirit appearing as a human, as in the story of Maryam. He argues that on their principles, the two possible explanations—annihilation or using an existing body—both fail to qualify as true manifestation. This exposes a fundamental weakness in their metaphysical system, which also undermines their ability to explain how the eternal divine command can be expressed in different human languages.

Guided Path
1
Encounter the Quranic verse and the problem
Read the Quranic verse cited (19:17) and note how the author uses it to pose a challenge to the mutakallimun: how can a spirit appear as a physical human?
The verse describes the angel Gabriel appearing to Mary as a well-proportioned man.
2
Identify the two failed explanations
The author presents two possible explanations a mutakallim might offer: either the spirit is annihilated and a new body is created, or the spirit uses an existing human body. Understand why each fails to be genuine manifestation.
The first is not manifestation but replacement; the second is reincarnation (tanasukh), not manifestation.
3
Grasp the broader implication for divine speech
The author extends the critique: if mutakallimun cannot explain spiritual-physical manifestation, how can they conceive of the eternal command manifesting in different human languages (Arabic, Syriac)?
This highlights a deeper inconsistency in their framework regarding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنى لهم تصور نزول الروحانيات وتشخصها بالجسمانيات، كما أخبر التنزيل عنه : فَأَرْسَلْنَا إِلَيْهَا رُوحَنَا فَتَمَثَّلَ لَهَا بَشَرًا سَوِيًّا [مريم : ١٧]. وكيف يستقيم على مذهب المتكلم تمثل الروح بالشخص البشري أبأن تعدم الروح ويوجد الشخص، وليس ذلك من التمثل في شيء، أو بأن يستعمل الروح شخصاً موجوداً بشريا ولم يكن ذلك تمثلا أيضاً، بل تناسخا، وإذا لم يمكنه تقرير التمثل والتشكل ؛ أعني : تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني كيف يمكنه تصور الأمر الأزلي يتمثل اللسان العربي تارة واللسان السرياني طورًا حتى يجب أن يقال
How can they conceive of the descent of spiritual beings and their assuming bodily forms, as the Revelation informed about it: 'So We sent to her Our Spirit, and he appeared to her as a well-proportioned man' (19:17). And how can it be sound according to the school of the mutakallim (theologian) that the spirit manifests as a human person? Either by the spirit being annihilated and the person coming into existence, and that is not manifestation at all; or by the spirit using an existing human person, and that would also not be manifestation, but rather reincarnation (tanasukh). And if he cannot establish manifestation and formation (tamaththul wa tashakkul), I mean: the manifestation of the spiritual by the physical, how can he conceive of the eternal command manifesting in the Arabic tongue one time and the Syriac tongue another time, so that it must be said...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the mutakallimun's inability to account for the manifestation of spiritual beings in physical form, using the Quranic story of the angel Gabriel's appearing to Mary as a man (19:17). The author presents two possible explanations on the mutakallim's principles, both of which fail. First, the spirit could be annihilated and a new physical body created, but that is not 'manifestation' (tamaththul) but complete replacement. Second, the spirit could take over an existing human body, but that is not manifestation either; it is reincarnation (tanasukh). Since neither is tenable, the mutakallim cannot explain this miracle. The author then draws an analogy: if they cannot explain the spiritual-physical manifestation, how can they conceive of the eternal command (al-amr al-azali) manifesting in different human languages (Arabic, Syriac) over time? The passage ends mid-sentence, but the implication is that the mutakallim's framework is inadequate for both phenomena.

Key Terms
الروحانيات
الجسمانيات
التمثل
التشكل
التناسخ
الأمر الأزلي
اللسان العربي
اللسان السرياني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the mutakallimun's framework can easily explain the spirit's manifestation by appealing to God's power to create a new body or use an existing one.
Correction: The author argues that both options fail: the first is not manifestation but annihilation and recreation, and the second is reincarnation, which is rejected. Thus, the mutakallim cannot provide a coherent account that preserves the concept of manifestation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the two problematic explanations a mutakallim might offer for the spirit's manifestation in human form?
A) Reincarnation and creation of a new body.
B) Annihilation of the spirit and creation of a person, or the spirit using an existing body.
C) Transformation of spirit into body and vice versa.
D) The spirit's appearance as a dream.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun cannot explain the spirit's manifestation without falling into reincarnation or annihilation, revealing a flaw in their system that also affects their account of divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on a logical explanation for something that ultimately requires faith, like the manifestation of the angel? Are there areas in your life where you demand that God's actions fit your intellectual framework? Will you confess that and submit to His revelation as the ultimate authority?
?
The mutakallim could only think in terms of annihilation or reincarnation. What are some 'explanations' you have given for divine matters that actually reduce or distort the reality of what God has done? Have you ever tried to force a miracle into your limited categories? What would it cost you to simply accept what God has said, even if it defies human reasoning?
?
The passage ends by asking if the mutakallim can conceive of the eternal command manifesting in different languages. How does your own expectation that God must speak in a certain way (like only in Arabic, or only through 'reasonable' means) limit your understanding of how He reveals Himself to people? Will you surrender your preconceptions to accept His choices, even when they surprise you?

Two Interpretations of Animal Speech in the Quran
The author explains two ways to understand Quranic attributions of speech to animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways Muslim theologians interpret Quranic verses that attribute speech to animals, preserving the miraculous nature while affirming that animals do not naturally speak.

Core Idea
Quranic animal speech is a miracle, not a natural ability.

The Quran mentions ants, birds, and even a sheep speaking. How can this be reconciled with the fact that animals do not possess rational speech? The author presents two acceptable interpretations that treat these events as miracles for prophets rather than as natural occurrences. This lesson examines both views and the example of the speaking sheep's leg.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the two interpretations. Notice the author's structure: first interpretation then second, with an example.
2
Identify the two interpretations
List the two ways the author explains animal speech. Note the key difference: one involves God giving the animal intellect and actual speech, the other involves God causing words to be heard from the animal without its awareness.
3
Reflect on the miracle aspect
Consider why both interpretations are considered miracles. How do they uphold the Quranic text while respecting natural laws? The example of the sheep's leg is especially telling.
The sheep's leg incident is a well-known miracle in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ورد في التنزيل من نسبة الكلام إلى أمثالهم فهو محمول على أحد وجهين: أحدهما: أنه أعطاهم عقلا وأنطقهم حقيقة بحرف وصوت، وجعل ذلك معجزة للنبي الذي هو في زمانه. والثاني: أنه أجرى على لسانهم وهم لا يعرفون كلاما ففهمه ذلك الزمان من غير أن يشعر به المتكلم من الوحش والطير، كما أجرى على ذراع الشاة: لا تأكل مني فإني مسمومة.
And what is mentioned in the Revelation of attributing speech to their likes is to be interpreted in one of two ways: One: that He gave them intellect and actually made them speak with letter and sound, and made that a miracle for the Prophet who was in his time. The second: that He made speech flow on their tongues while they did not know it as speech, and the person of that time understood it without the speaker (the animal or bird) being aware of it, just as He made flow on the leg of the sheep: 'Do not eat from me, for I am poisoned.'
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage deals with Quranic verses that attribute speech to animals (e.g., the ant and birds). The author provides two acceptable interpretations to reconcile these verses with the principle that animals do not possess real speech. The first interpretation is that God caused a miracle by giving the animal intellect and making it actually speak with letters and sounds, for the benefit of a prophet. The second is that God caused the words to be heard from the animal without the animal's knowledge or intention, similar to the well-known incident of the roasted sheep's leg that spoke to the Prophet Muhammad and informed him it was poisoned. Both interpretations preserve the miraculous nature while maintaining that animals do not inherently have speech.

Key Terms
التنزيل
معجزة
الذراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals have the natural ability to speak like humans.
Correction: The Quranic attributions are interpreted as miracles, either by God giving them intellect temporarily or causing speech to be heard from them without their awareness, not as a natural faculty.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two interpretations given for animals speaking in the Quran?
A) Animals have natural speech like humans.
B) It is a metaphor for their sounds.
C) God gave them intellect and made them speak as a miracle.
D) The prophets imagined it.

Key Takeaway
Quranic accounts of animals speaking are understood as miracles for prophets, not as evidence of natural animal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses about animals speaking, did you previously doubt the possibility? How does knowing that these are miracles, not natural abilities, deepen your trust in Allah's absolute power? Will you pause before questioning such verses in the future?
?
If Allah can make an animal speak without its own awareness (as in the second interpretation), what does that imply about the words you speak? Are you always conscious of how Allah might use your tongue for purposes beyond your intention? Tonight, will you ask Allah to purify your speech and make it a means of good, even when you are unaware?
?
The first interpretation says Allah gives intellect to an animal as a miracle. Your intellect is a constant gift. Do you treat it as such? In what area of your life have you been using your intellect to disobey Allah? Commit today to using your mind to seek knowledge and obedience, not to justify sin.

The Soul's Power to Actualize Others
The author describes the soul's unique ability to teach and actualize others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique property of the human soul to bring other souls from potentiality to actuality through teaching, and how this distinguishes humans from animals.

Core Idea
Teaching is a unique power of the human soul to actualize others.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam explores a special characteristic of the human soul: its ability not only to develop itself but also to develop other souls. The author provides concrete examples of teaching to illustrate this causal property. He then contrasts this with animal instinct, showing what makes humans unique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's description of the soul's property sink in.
2
Identify the special property
Notice that the author states: when the soul itself exits from potentiality to actuality, it can bring other souls from potentiality to actuality. This is the core idea.
3
Examine the three examples
The author gives examples of a teacher of reading, a teacher of wisdom, and a teacher of crafts. In each case, the teacher's soul causes the student's soul to actualize the same skill.
Think about how each example shows the same principle: the teacher's actualized state becomes the student's actuality.
4
Contrast with animals
The author then says this property is not for any animal, because animals only have what is by nature and instinct. Humans can teach, animals cannot.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
The author cites the verse about Adam learning the names as a proof. Then he notes humans have both the power to receive and to perform knowledge and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن خواص أفعالها أنها إذا خرجت من القوة إلى الفعل عقلا وعلما أو خلقاً وعملا أخرجت غيره من النفوس الإنسانية من القوة إلى الفعل حتى تصير صورتها مشابهة الصورة المخرج ؛ كالمعلم للقراءة يخرج نفس الصبي من قوة الاستعداد إلى فعل القراءة حتى يصير قارئًا مثله، وكالمعلم للحكمة يخرج نفس المتعلم من قوة الصلاحية إلى فعل الحكمة حتى يصير حكيمًا مثله، وكالمعلم للصناعات البشرية صانعا الخاصة بنوع الإنسان يخرج نفوس المتعلمين من القوة إلى الفعل حتى يصير مثله، وليست هذه الخاصية أيضا لنوع من الحيوانات ؛ لأن ما لها بالطبع والفطرة فهي لمواليدها كذلك، فهذا المعنى فضيلة النفس الإنسانية وانفصلت، وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى : ( وَعَلَّمَ ءَادَمَ الْأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ) [البقرة : ٣١] الآية، وكان له قوة القبول وقوة الأداء في طرفي العلم والعمل
And among the special properties of its actions: when it exits from potentiality to actuality in intellect and knowledge or creation and action, it brings other human souls from potentiality to actuality until its form becomes similar to the form of the one who brought it out — like the teacher of reading who brings the soul of the boy from the potentiality of readiness to the act of reading until he becomes a reader like him, and like the teacher of wisdom who brings the soul of the learner from the potentiality of aptitude to the act of wisdom until he becomes a sage like him, and like the teacher of human crafts who brings the souls of learners from potentiality to actuality until they become like him. And this property is not for any type of animal, because what they have by nature and instinct is also for their offspring. So this meaning is the virtue of the human soul and it is distinguished. And to this points His saying, the Exalted: 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them' [Qur'an 2:31] — the verse. And he had the power of reception and the power of performance in both ends of knowledge and action.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage lists the first special property of the human soul: its ability to actualize other souls. When the soul itself becomes actualized in intellect, knowledge, or action, it can bring other human souls from potentiality to actuality. The author gives three examples: a teacher of reading, a teacher of wisdom, and a teacher of crafts. In each case, the teacher's soul causes the student's soul to actualize the same skill. This property is unique to humans; animals only convey what they have by instinct, not through teaching. This distinguishes the human soul. The author cites the Qur'anic verse about Adam learning the names as evidence. Finally, he notes that humans have both the power to receive and the power to perform in knowledge and action.

Key Terms
قوة (potentiality)
فعل (actuality)
تعليم (teaching)
فضيلة (virtue)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the soul's power to teach is just a metaphor or a social phenomenon, not a real causal property.
Correction: The author treats teaching as an actual causal process: the teacher's actualized soul brings the student's soul from potentiality to actuality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what example is NOT given for the soul's power to actualize others?
A) Teacher of reading
B) Teacher of wisdom
C) Teacher of crafts
D) Teacher of music

Key Takeaway
The human soul has the unique ability to bring other souls from potentiality to actuality through teaching, which is a real causal property not found in animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who in your life has acted as the 'teacher' that brought you from potentiality to actuality in a meaningful way? How has that shaped who you are today, and are you grateful enough?
?
The passage says teaching is a distinctive property of the human soul. If you have been actualized in some area, who are you deliberately helping to bring from potentiality to actuality right now? If the answer is no one, what is holding you back?
?
When you teach something, do you see it as a causal process of actualizing another soul, or just as transferring information? How might that shift your intention and approach starting tomorrow?

The Basis of Hearing and the Reality of Divine Speech
The author discusses the basis for hearing and explains how God's speech is heard without means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological explanation of how God's speech is heard without physical means, and the distinction between the modes of revelation.

Core Idea
God's speech is heard directly by the soul, without letters, sounds, or intermediaries.

This passage by the author addresses a key theological question: how can God's speech be heard if it is not composed of letters and sounds? The author begins by noting different opinions on the basis for hearing, then builds an analogy from human experience to explain how divine speech is perceived. The discussion is crucial for understanding the nature of revelation and the distinction between the modes by which prophets receive God's words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once without overthinking. Then read the literal English translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the author's use of the Shura verse and the analogy from internal speech.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the three modes of revelation
The author cites Quran 42:51 to enumerate three ways Allah communicates with humans: (1) direct revelation without means, (2) from behind a veil, (3) through an angel. Find these in the passage and note the examples given.
Each mode corresponds to different prophets' experiences.
3
Understand the analogy from human internal speech
The author argues that just as we have internal speech (كلام نفسي) that we express with the tongue and hear with the ear, we can conceive of a direct communication from soul to soul. This analogy makes sense of how Moses heard Allah's speech without physical sound.
The key is that internal speech is known to the person without external senses.
4
Apply the analogy to Moses and to ordinary believers
The author uses the example of Moses hearing Allah from behind a veil (without means) and then distinguishes that from how we hear the Quran. The passage ends by noting the distinction between recitation and the recited.
This distinction is important for avoiding anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف رأي العقلاء في أن المصحح للسمع ما هو؟ فمنهم من قال: هو الوجود كالمصحح للرؤية، ومنهم من قال المصحح كونه موجوداً باعتبار كون المسموع كلامًا، والكلام قد يكون عبارة لفظية، وقد يكون نطقا نفسيا، وكلاهما مسموعان، ونحن كما أثبتنا كلاما في النفس ليس بحرف ولا صوت، كذلك نثبت سماعًا في النفس ليس بحرف ولا صوت ثم ذلك السماع قد يكون بواسطة وحجاب، وقد يكون بغير واسطة وحجاب ما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحيا أو من وراء حجاب أو يرسل رسولا [الشورى: ٥١]، فالوحي ما يكون بغير واسطة وحجاب كما قال تعالى: فأوحى إلى عبده ما أوحى، وكما قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: نفث في روعي، وقد يكون من وراء حجاب كما كان لموسى عليه السلام وكلمه ربه، وقد يكون بواسطة رسول وحجاب كما قال تعالى: أو يرسل رسولا. وبالجملة نحن نعقل في الشاهد كلامًا في النفس ونجده من أنفسنا كما أثبتناه، ثم اللسان عنه بالعبارة الدالة عليه، ثم يسمع السامع فيصل بعد الاستماع إلى فقد وصل الكلام إلى النفس بهذه الوسائط، فلو قدرنا ارتفاع هذه الوسائط كلها من البين حتى تدرك النفس ما كانت النفس الناطقة المتكلمة مشتملة عليه، فتحقق كلام في جانب المتكلم، وسماع في جانب المستمع ولا حرف ولا صوت ولا لسان ولا صماخ، فإذا تصور مثل ذلك في الشاهد حمل استماع موسى كلام الله على ذلك، وعن هذا قال: إني اصطفيتك على الناس برسالتي وبكلامي [الأعراف: ١٤٤]، فالرسالات بواسطة الرسل والكلام من غير واسطة، لكنه من وراء حجاب، وأما في حقنا فكلام الله تعالى مسموع بأسماعنا، مقروء بألسنتنا، محفوظ في صدورنا، وقد تبين الفرق بين القراءة والمقروء في مسألة الكلام.
Then the intellectuals differed concerning what is the basis for hearing. Some said it is existence, like the basis for vision. Some said the basis is its being existent, considering that what is heard is speech. Speech may be a verbal expression, and it may be an internal utterance, and both are heard. And we, just as we have affirmed speech in the self (nafs) that is not a letter nor a sound, likewise we affirm hearing in the self that is not a letter nor a sound. Then that hearing may be by means and a veil, and it may be without means and a veil. 'It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation, or from behind a veil, or by sending a messenger' [Shura 42:51]. So revelation is that which is without means and veil, as Allah said: 'So He revealed to His servant what He revealed' [53:10], and as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'He breathed into my heart.' And it may be from behind a veil, as it was for Moses (peace be upon him) when his Lord spoke to him, and it may be by a messenger and a veil, as Allah said: 'or send a messenger.' In summary, we understand in the witnessed world (shahid) speech in the self and find it in ourselves as we have affirmed. Then the tongue expresses it by the expression indicating it. Then the hearer hears, and after listening it reaches that the speech has reached the self by these means. So if we suppose the removal of all these means from between, so that the self perceives what the speaking rational self was containing, then speech is realized on the part of the speaker, and hearing on the part of the listener, with no letter, no sound, no tongue, and no ear canal. So when such a thing is imagined in the witnessed world, the hearing of Moses' speech of Allah is interpreted accordingly. And from this He said: 'Indeed, I have chosen you over the people with My messages and My speech' [Al-A'raf 7:144]. So the messages are by means of messengers, and the speech is without means, but it is from behind a veil. As for our case, the speech of Allah the Exalted is heard by our ears, read by our tongues, preserved in our hearts. And the distinction between the recitation and what is recited has been clarified in the issue of speech.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the basis of hearing and the nature of divine speech. The author notes disagreement among intellectuals: some say hearing is based on existence like vision, others say it is based on existence considering that what is heard is speech. Speech can be verbal or internal, both audible. The author then affirms, as with internal speech, an internal hearing without letters or sounds. The modes of revelation are then enumerated based on Quran 42:51: (1) revelation without means or veil, (2) from behind a veil, (3) through a messenger. The author then uses an analogy from the witnessed world: we experience internal speech, which is expressed by the tongue, heard by a listener, and then reaches the soul through these means. If we suppose the removal of all means (tongue, ear, etc.), the pure communication of the speaker's internal speech to the hearer's soul is conceivable. This is used to explain how Moses heard God's speech without such means, as indicated in Quran 7:144. The author concludes by clarifying that for us, God's speech is heard through our ears etc., but the distinction between the recitation and what is recited is a separate issue.

Key Terms
السماع
الكلام النفسي
الوحي
الحجاب
المصحح
الواسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hearing God's speech requires physical sound waves or that God's speech is like human speech composed of letters and sounds.
Correction: The author argues that divine speech is heard without letters or sounds, as internal speech is known directly. The hearing of Moses was a direct spiritual perception, not through physical ears.

Checkpoint
According to Quran 42:51, which mode of revelation is exemplified by Moses (peace be upon him)?
A) Direct revelation without veil
B) From behind a veil
C) Through a messenger
D) Through a dream

Key Takeaway
God's speech is heard by the soul directly, without need for physical sound or intermediary, as exemplified by Moses and explained through the analogy of internal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever pause to consider that the words you recite are not the ultimate reality of God's speech? How does the distinction between recited words and internal divine speech affect your relationship with the Quran? Will you make space today to reflect on the meaning behind the words, rather than just the sounds?
?
The passage shows that Moses heard Allah's speech without physical sound. If you could hear God's speech directly, what would you want to hear? Are you preparing your heart to be receptive to divine guidance, even if it comes without dramatic signs? What practical step can you take this week to listen more deeply to God's messages in your life?
?
Consider the analogy: internal speech is known to you, expressed outwardly. How often do you examine your own internal speech—your thoughts and intentions—as they relate to God? Are they aligned with what you profess with your tongue? What thought today needs to be purified so that your inner speech reflects true devotion?

Prophethood

Revelation Confirms Reason and the Necessity of a Prophet
Revelation reinforces rational truths and a prophet is needed due to human limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complementary roles of reason and revelation in guiding humanity to true knowledge and good deeds.

Core Idea
Revelation confirms reason but also supplements its limitations.

This passage from al-Shahrastani explains why divine revelation is not opposed to reason but rather reinforces what reason already knows. However, individual human intellects cannot grasp all that is needed for universal guidance, so a prophet is necessary to provide detailed direction for both worldly and spiritual well-being.

Guided Path
1
Define human perfection
Read the first sentence: human perfection involves attaining true knowledge and good works in imitation of higher beings.
Notice that this sets the goal which revelation serves.
2
Understand the role of laws
The next part states that divine laws establish what is already settled in intellects, not change them.
This shows harmony between reason and revelation.
3
Identify the limitation of intellects
Individual intellects are deficient in acquiring all intelligibles and cannot guide themselves to universal interests.
This is the key premise for the necessity of prophecy.
4
See the necessity of a lawgiver
Because of that limitation, wisdom requires a prescribed law from a lawgiver who guides people to belief and action.
The lawgiver combines knowledge and action according to reason.
5
Reflect on the outcome
The purpose is to lead to pure good, avoid pure evil, preserve the species, and maintain world order.
This shows the comprehensive benefit of prophecy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكمال حال الإنسان أن تستكمل النفس قوتي العلم الحق والعمل الخير تشبيها بالإله تعالى والروحانيات العلوية بحسب الطاقة والشرائع إنما ترد بتمهيد ما تقرر في العقول لا بتغييرها لكن العقول الجزئية لما كانت قاصرة عن اكتساب المعقولات بأسرها عاجزة عن الاهتداء إلى المصالح الكلية الشاملة لنوع الإنسان وجب من حيث الحكمة أن يكون بين الإنسان شرع يفرضه شارع يحملهم على الإيمان بالغيب جملة ويهديهم إلى مصالح معاشهم ومعادهم تفصيلا فيكون قد جمع لهم بين خصلتي العلم والعمل على مقتضى العقل وحملهم على التوجه إلى الخير المحض والإعراض عن الشر المحض استبقاء لنوعهم واستدامة لنظام العالم.
And the perfection of the human state is that the soul attains both the power of true knowledge and good work, in imitation of Allah Ta'ala and the higher spiritual beings according to capacity. And the laws are only brought to establish what is already settled in the intellects, not to change them. But because individual intellects are deficient in acquiring all intelligibles and incapable of guiding themselves to the universal interests that encompass the entire human species, it was necessary from the standpoint of wisdom that there be a law prescribed by a lawgiver among humans, who makes them believe in the unseen in general, and guides them to the interests of their livelihood and afterlife in detail, such that he brings together for them the two qualities of knowledge and action according to the dictate of reason, and carries them to focus on pure good and turn away from pure evil, for the preservation of their species and the continuation of the world's order.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage explains the purpose of revelation in relation to reason. Human perfection lies in combining true knowledge ('ilm haqq) and good deeds ('amal khair), emulating God and higher spiritual beings. Revelation (shara'i') does not contradict or change reason; rather, it establishes and reinforces what is already determined by the intellects ('uqul). However, individual human intellects ('uqul juz'iyya) are limited: they cannot grasp all rational truths or discern all universal benefits (masalih kulliyya). Therefore, divine wisdom requires sending a lawgiver (shari') who prescribes a law, guides people to believe in the unseen (ghayb), and directs them to their worldly and religious interests. This lawgiver combines for them knowledge and action in accordance with reason, leading them to pure good (khair mahd) and away from pure evil (sharr mahd), thus preserving the species and the world order.

Key Terms
العلم الحق
العمل الخير
العقول الجزئية
المصالح الكلية
الإيمان بالغيب
الخير المحض
الشر المحض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation is unnecessary for those with strong reason; reason alone can fully guide.
Correction: The passage states that even the strongest individual intellects are deficient in grasping all universal interests, so revelation is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why is a lawgiver (prophet) necessary according to this passage?
A) Because reason is completely unreliable
B) Because individual intellects are deficient in grasping all universal interests
C) Because revelation changes what reason dictates
D) Because humans are naturally disobedient

Key Takeaway
Revelation confirms reason but is necessary because individual intellects cannot fully grasp universal interests, so a prophet guides to true knowledge and good deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you relied on your own reasoning to make a decision, ignoring the guidance of revelation? What was the outcome, and what does that reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
This passage says human perfection is to imitate Allah in knowledge and action. What is one area of your life where you are actively seeking to grow in true knowledge and good work? If none, why not?
?
The lawgiver (prophet) guides to the unseen and to interests of both worlds. Are you living with a constant awareness of the unseen and the afterlife, or have you become consumed by this world? What specific step will you take today to realign your priorities?

Distinguishing the Prophet by Miracles
The prophet must be marked by signs proving his divine commission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a prophet must be distinguished by miracles as signs of divine commission and must also excel in intellect, opinion, speech, and intuition.

Core Idea
Miracles are signs, but the prophet's personal excellence also testifies to his truth.

This passage from al-Baqillani introduces a crucial requirement for the prophet: he must be clearly distinguished from others. While miracles are the primary sign, the prophet also must excel in intellectual and moral qualities. This lesson explores that dual requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence aloud. Notice the terms used: آيات for signs, and the list of excellences.
The passage is incomplete, but the key points are clear.
2
Identify the two main requirements
The passage first says the lawgiver must be distinguished by signs (miracles). Then it mentions he must outweigh others in four qualities: intellect, opinion, speech, intuition.
These are two distinct aspects of his distinction.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would a prophet need both miracles and personal excellence? How do they complement each other?
This connects to the broader topic of prophetic proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذلك الشارع يجب أن يكون من بينهم مميزا بآيات تدل على أنها من عند ربه راجحا عليهم بعقله الرزين ورأيه المتين ولفظه المبين وحدسه
Then that lawgiver must be distinguished among them by signs indicating that they are from his Lord, outweighing them with his solid intellect, his strong opinion, his clear speech, and his intuition...
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage states a crucial requirement for the prophet (shari'): he must be clearly distinguished from others by miracles (ayat) that prove his message is from God. Additionally, he must excel in intellect, opinion, speech, and intuition. The text is incomplete but introduces the rationale for the need for prophetic miracles as signs of divine authorization, and also emphasizes the prophet's intellectual and moral superiority.

Correction: The passage shows that in addition to miracles, the prophet must also possess superior intellect, sound judgment, clear speech, and intuition, which are part of his distinction.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek guidance in your life, do you look only for dramatic 'signs' while ignoring the quiet excellence of intellect and character that God has placed before you? How will you change that starting today?
?
The prophet is described as having 'solid intellect, strong judgment, clear speech, and intuition.' Which of these attributes do you most lack when trying to discern truth from falsehood in your daily decisions? Commit to one practical step to cultivate it this week.
?
If you were to be a witness for the truth in your community, would people recognize you by miraculous signs or by your personal excellence? Be honest: which of the four attributes above are you neglecting, and what will you sacrifice to strengthen it?

The Necessity of a Divinely-Sent Lawgiver
Argument for necessity of a lawgiver and his attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why limited human reason necessitates a divinely-sent lawgiver and what qualities that lawgiver must possess according to classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Limited reason needs a divine guide to achieve universal good.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's Nihayat al-Aqdam presents a rational argument for the necessity of prophethood. It begins by acknowledging the limitations of human intellect in grasping all truths and universal interests. Then it argues that divine wisdom requires a lawgiver to guide humanity, and describes the signs and virtues that distinguish a true prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the flow from problem to solution.
2
Identify the problem
The author starts by stating that individual minds are incapable of acquiring all intelligibles and universal interests. What is the key phrase?
Look for 'العقول الجزئية قاصرة'.
3
Understand the solution
The solution is a lawgiver who compels belief in the unseen and guides to worldly and religious interests.
The lawgiver is necessary 'من حيث الحكمة' (from the standpoint of wisdom).
4
List the lawgiver's qualities
The lawgiver must be distinguished by signs (آيات) and possess superior intellect, opinion, speech, intuition, insight, character, and conduct.
The list includes 'عقل رزين' and 'خلق حسن'.
5
Reflect on the Quranic support
The author quotes Quran 16:125 to show that the lawgiver should call with wisdom and good instruction.
This ties the argument to revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن العقول الجزئية لما كانت قاصرة عن اكتساب المعقولات بأسرها عاجزة عن الاهتداء إلى المصالح الكلية الشاملة لنوع الإنسان، وجب من حيث الحكمة أن يكون بين الإنسان شرع يفرضه شارع يحملهم على الإيمان بالغيب جملة، ويهديهم إلى مصالح معاشهم ومعادهم تفصيلا، فيكون قد جمع لهم بين خصلتي العلم والعمل على مقتضى العقل وحملهم على التوجه إلى الخير المحض والإعراض عن الشر المحض استبقاء لنوعهم واستدامة لنظام العالم. ثم ذلك الشارع يجب أن يكون من بينهم مميزا بآيات تدل على أنها من عند ربه، راجحا عليهم بعقله الرزين، ورأيه المتين، ولفظه المبين، وحدسه النافذ، وبصره الناقد، وخلقه الحسن، وسمته الأرصن، يلين لهم في القول ويشاورهم في الأمر، ويكلمهم على مقادير عقولهم، ويكلفهم بحسب طاقتهم كما ورد في الكتاب: (ادْعُ إِلَى سَبِيلِ رَبِّكَ بِالْحِكْمَةِ وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ وَجَادِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ).
However, since individual minds are incapable of acquiring all intelligibles and unable to attain the universal interests that encompass the human species, it became necessary from the standpoint of wisdom that there be among humans a law prescribed by a lawgiver who compels them to believe in the unseen as a whole and guides them to the interests of their livelihood and afterlife in detail, so that he thus combines for them the two qualities of knowledge and action according to reason, and compels them to turn to pure good and turn away from pure evil, for the preservation of their species and the continuity of the world order. Then that lawgiver must be distinguished among them by signs indicating that they are from his Lord, outweighing them with his sound intellect, solid opinion, clear speech, penetrating intuition, critical insight, good character, and most solid conduct, being gentle in speech, consulting them in matters, speaking to them according to the capacity of their minds, and charging them according to their ability, as mentioned in the Book: 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good instruction, and argue with them in a way that is best.'
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the necessity of a divinely-sent lawgiver (shāri‘). The argument begins with a premise: individual minds (al-‘uqūl al-juz’iyya) are limited—they cannot acquire all intelligibles nor discern the universal interests that benefit the entire human species. Therefore, wisdom (ḥikma) necessitates the existence of a law (shar‘) prescribed by a lawgiver. This lawgiver must cause people to believe in the unseen collectively and guide them to both worldly and religious interests in detail, thereby combining knowledge and action according to reason. The goal is to direct humanity toward pure good and away from pure evil, preserving the species and maintaining world order. The author then lists the necessary qualities of that lawgiver: he must be distinguished by miracles (āyāt) proving his divine mission; he must surpass others in reason, opinion, speech, intuition, insight, character, and conduct. He should be gentle, consultative, speak according to people’s intellects, and charge them within their capacity, citing the Quranic verse (16:125). This lesson establishes the rational necessity of prophecy and the criteria for identifying a true prophet.

المصالح الكلية
الشارع
الإيمان بالغيب
مميزا بآيات
عقل رزين
رأي متين
لفظ مبين
حدس نافذ
بصر ناقد
خلق حسن
سماة أرصن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reason alone can achieve all moral guidance.
Correction: The author argues that individual minds are incapable of grasping universal interests, thus a divinely-guided lawgiver is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say a lawgiver is necessary?
A) Because reason is completely evil
B) Because individual minds cannot grasp universal interests alone
C) Because people refuse to follow reason
D) Because God cannot guide directly

Key Takeaway
Human reason's limitations necessitate a divinely-sent lawgiver who combines guidance and virtue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You acknowledge that your mind is limited in grasping what is truly good for you. In what specific area of your life are you still trusting your own judgment over the guidance of the lawgiver? What step will you take today to submit that area to divine law?
?
The lawgiver is described as gentle, consultative, and speaking according to people's capacity. How does this challenge your pride when you receive religious instruction? Are you willing to be taught by someone who may not match your intellectual standards?
?
If you truly believed that a divinely-sent lawgiver combines knowledge and action for your preservation, what would you change about how you prioritize learning and practicing your faith this week?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Rational Good and Evil
Shahrastani refutes Mu'tazila on rational self-evidence of moral judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's claim that moral judgments are rationally self-evident is flawed, and how self-interest influences such judgments.

Core Idea
Moral judgments are not self-evident like math; they depend on motive and expectation.

The Mu'tazila argued that good and evil are known by reason alone, independent of revelation. But the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) counter that moral judgments are not like necessary truths. In this passage, Shahrastani explains that even rational approval of saving the drowning is rooted in seeking praise, not pure reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Barahima argument
Read the opening lines where the Barahima claim that prophethood is unnecessary because either commands are rational (reason suffices) or irrational (unacceptable). This sets up the issue of rational judgment.
The Barahima's argument is a challenge that the response indirectly addresses through refuting Mu'tazila.
2
Focus on the response to Mu'tazila
The author says 'the people of truth responded to each sect and said to the Mu'tazila...'. Pay attention to how the response distinguishes between necessary knowledge (2>1) and moral judgment (lying ugly).
3
Understand the difference between judgments
Necessary knowledge is forced upon you – you can't deny 2>1. Moral judgment, however, is not necessary: a person in need sees truth and lying as equal and chooses based on self-interest.
This is the core of the refutation: moral judgments are not self-evident.
4
Analyze the drowning example
The Mu'tazila might point to common approval of saving drowning as evidence of rational goodness. But the author explains that such approval is based on seeking praise, not on intrinsic rational necessity.
The author concedes such judgments exist but says they are motivated by expected reward, not pure reason.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on how this refutation upholds the necessity of revelation for moral guidance, since reason alone is insufficient to determine good and evil.
This connects to the broader theme of prophethood being indispensable.

Source Text

وزادت البراهمة على التناسخية بأن قالوا: نحن لا نحتاج إلى شريعة وشارع، فإن ما يأمر به النبي لا يخلو إما أن يكون معقولا أو لا يكون معقولا، فإن كان معقولا فقد استغنى بالعقل عن النبي، وإن لم يكن معقولا لم يكن مقبولا. أجاب أهل الحق عن مقالة كل فرقة فقالوا للمعتزلة: المعارضة غير صحيحة، فإن ما ذكرناه من الفرق بين العلم بأن الاثنين أكثر من الواحد، والحكم بأن الكذب قبيح ظاهر لا مراء فيه، وما ذكرتموه غير مسلم فإنه يستوي عند صاحب الحاجة طرفا الصدق والكذب، وإن اختار الصدق لم يكن اختياره أمرًا ضروريًا ولا يقارنه العلم بوجوب اختياره ضرورة، ولو استروح إليه فلداع أو اعتياد أو غرض يحمله على ذلك، ومن استوى عنده الصدق والكذب في الملام في الحال والعقاب في ثاني الحال لم يرجح أحدهما على الثاني لأمر في ذاته. وأما استحسان العقلاء إنقاذ الغرقى واستقباحهم للعدوان فلطلب ثناء يتوقع منهم على ذلك الفعل وذم على الفعل الثاني، ومثل هذا قد سلمناه.
And the Brahmins added to the transmigrationists by saying: we have no need for a law or a lawgiver, for what the prophet commands is either rational or not rational. If it is rational, then reason suffices for the prophet; and if it is not rational, then it is not acceptable. The people of truth responded to the statement of each sect and said to the Mu'tazila: the objection is not correct, for what we have mentioned concerning the difference between the knowledge that two is more than one and the judgment that lying is ugly is apparent, no doubt about it, and what you have mentioned is not granted, for the person in need considers both sides of truthfulness and lying equal, and if he chooses truthfulness, his choice is not necessary nor does he have knowledge of the necessity of his choice necessarily, and if he inclines to it, it is due to a motive or habit or an interest that impels him to that, and for whom truthfulness and lying are equal in blame in the present and punishment in the future, he does not prefer one over the other for something in its essence. As for the rational approval of saving the drowning and their disapproval of aggression, it is for seeking praise they expect from them for that act and blame for the second act, and this we have conceded.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations. First, the Brahmins (Barahima) argue that prophethood is unnecessary because either a prophet's commands are rational (so reason suffices) or irrational (so unacceptable). The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) respond indirectly by first addressing the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila claim that good and evil are known rationally and that reason necessitates reward or punishment. The refutation points out that rational knowledge (e.g., two is more than one) is distinct from moral judgment (e.g., lying is ugly). The latter is not self-evident: a person in need considers truth and lying equal and chooses based on self-interest. The approval of saving the drowning is explained by desire for praise, not rational necessity. The author concludes by conceding that such judgments are based on expectation of reward, not intrinsic goodness.

Key Terms
البراهمة
المعتزلة
الحسن والقبح العقليان
أهل الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that moral judgments like 'lying is ugly' are rationally self-evident and necessary, just like mathematical truths.
Correction: The passage shows that such judgments are not necessary; a person in need may see truth and lying as equal, and choice depends on motives, not rational necessity. Even the approval of saving the drowning is based on seeking praise, not pure reason.

Checkpoint
What is the central flaw the author identifies in the Mu'tazila's claim that the judgment 'lying is ugly' is rationally self-evident?
A) The judgment is actually revealed, not rational.
B) A person in need may see truth and lying as equal due to self-interest.
C) The Mu'tazila confuse moral judgments with scientific knowledge.
D) The judgment is not universally agreed upon.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's claim that moral judgments are rationally self-evident fails because such judgments are not inevitable and are influenced by self-interest and social expectations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If social approval is not a reliable guide to moral truth, what criteria do you use to decide right from wrong? Will you commit to measuring your actions against revelation, even when it goes against what others praise?
?
The person in need sees truth and lying as equal. In a moment of personal crisis, what keeps you from lying? Is it a rational conviction or fear of punishment? How can you strengthen your reliance on divine guidance rather than your own motives?

The Necessity of Prophethood from Philosophical Principles
Author deduces the necessity of prophethood from the philosophers' own principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari argues that even the philosophers' own system—where knowledge comes from the Active Intellect—necessarily requires a prophet to convey that knowledge to all people.

Core Idea
Even if knowledge comes from an Active Intellect, not everyone can access it—hence a prophet is needed.

Al-Ash'ari engages the philosophers on their own ground. He first summarizes their view that the Active Intellect actualizes potential knowledge in human minds, then demonstrates that this very system implies the necessity of a prophetic intermediary. This passage is a powerful example of internal critique, turning philosophical principles against themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فقد اعترفتم' and 'فذلك' which signal the argument's structure.
2
Identify the philosophers' principles
Notice how the author restates the philosophers' view: the Necessary of Existence, the Active Intellect, and the role of particular intellects. This sets the foundation for the critique.
The author does not reject these premises; he uses them.
3
Analyze the limitation of knowledge
Focus on the statement that not every individual has preparedness to derive from the Active Intellect. This is the key pivot point in the argument.
The phrase 'ليس لكل واحد' is crucial.
4
Trace the conclusion
See how the author concludes that a prophet is necessary, and then makes a broader claim: knowledge is known by reason but only made obligatory by revelation.
The final sentence 'فلزمكم من حكم قاعدتكم هذه ما أثبتنا' shows the argument is a necessary implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الحقيقة ما انتفى عنه وجوه القوة كالمرجح الجانب الوجود على العدم في الممكنات هو واجب الوجود بذاته، والمخرج من القوى إلى الفعل هو العقل الفعال، فقد اعترفتم بأن لا خالق إلا الله ولا هادي إلى المعارف، ولا موجب للتكاليف المستدعية للجزاء إلا هو بتوسط العقل الأول الفعال، والعقول الجزئية بحكم مناسبتها العقل الأول ربما تشتمل منها صور المعارف فيعرف الأوائل فطرة وبديهة، ثم يرد إليها الثواني والثوالث نظرًا وتفكرًا حتى يخرج إلى الفعل، وهذا على طريق الجواز والإمكان لا على طريق الوجوب والضرورة، لكن لا يعرف إيجابه وتكليفه على العموم؛ إذ ليس لكل واحد من نوع الإنسان استعداد الاستمداد منه من كل وجه بل واحد بعد واحد، فذلك هو النبي عندنا، فلا تعرف المعارف إلا بالعقل ولا تجب المعارف إلا بالسمع، فلزمكم من حكم قاعدتكم هذه ما أثبتنا وهو ضرورة.
In reality, what is devoid of aspects of potentiality, like the preponderator of the side of existence over non-existence in possible things, is the Necessary of Existence in Its essence. The bringer from potentialities to act is the Active Intellect. So you have admitted that there is no creator but God, no guide to knowledge, and no obligator of duties that require recompense except He, through the mediation of the first Active Intellect. And the particular intellects, by virtue of their suitability to the first intellect, may include from it the forms of knowledge, so they know the first principles by innate disposition and intuition. Then they refer to them secondary and tertiary matters by consideration and reflection until they emerge into act. This is by way of permissibility and possibility, not by way of necessity and obligation. But one does not know His obliging and duty in general, for not every individual of the human species has the preparedness to derive from it in every respect, but one after another. So that is the prophet according to us. Thus, knowledge is only known by reason, and knowledge is only obligatory by revelation. So your principle necessitates what we have affirmed, and it is necessary.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a deductive refutation aimed at the philosophers who posit an Active Intellect as the intermediary source of knowledge. The author first restates their position: the Necessary of Existence (God) causes existence, but the actualization of potential knowledge comes through the Active Intellect. Then he draws an implication: the particular intellects (human souls) derive knowledge from the Active Intellect by innate disposition, but this derivation is only possible for some individuals, not all. Since not everyone can access this knowledge directly, a prophet is needed to convey it universally. The conclusion is that even on the philosophers' own grounds, prophethood is necessary. The argument builds step by step: admission of the system, limitation of access, then the need for a prophetic intermediary.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال
النبي
المعارف
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari rejects reason entirely.
Correction: He affirms reason's role in knowing ('knowledge is only known by reason') but limits its obligating force to revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the philosophers' system necessitate a prophet?
A) Because the Active Intellect is insufficient.
B) Because not every human can derive knowledge directly from the Active Intellect.
C) Because revelation is superior to reason.
D) Because the philosophers denied prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' own system logically requires the existence of a prophet because direct intellectual access to the Active Intellect is not available to everyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you rely on your own intellect alone to decide what God requires of you, how do you answer the passage's claim that obligation only comes from revelation? Which duties might you be ignoring because they are not clear to your reason? Commit to studying revelation for guidance on one such duty this week.
?
The passage says not everyone has the preparedness to receive direct knowledge—are you humble enough to admit you need a prophet and that your reason may not be sufficient? What is one area where you have trusted your own judgment over the prophet's teaching? Will you submit to that teaching today?
?
You know the truth by reason, but do you feel the weight of its obligation? The passage separates knowledge from duty—when was a moment you knew something was true but did not act on it because you felt no obligation? Will you now let revelation turn your knowledge into a commanded act?

Refuting Sabian Claims on Moral Knowledge and Experience
Author refutes Sabian claims on planetary influence and moral knowledge by reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the Sabian positions that planetary influences determine moral good and evil and that beneficial properties of things are known by reason or experience alone.

Core Idea
Reason and experience cannot yield certainty in moral commands or hidden properties; revelation is essential.

This passage tackles a sophisticated challenge from the Sabians, who argued that planetary influences determine moral good and evil and that human reason can discover beneficial properties through experience. The author first grants a limited point about natural good and evil, then sharply delimits the scope of reason, showing why neither reason nor experience suffices for moral commands or hidden properties. This leads to the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Observe the author's argument structure.
Notice the shift from conceding a minor point to a full refutation.
2
Identify the two main issues
The passage addresses two distinct Sabian claims: that planetary influences determine moral good and evil, and that beneficial properties are known by reason/experience. Identify where each is discussed.
The first part deals with moral judgment (ḥusn/qubḥ), the second with experiential knowledge.
3
Understand the critique of experience
The author argues that experience alone cannot yield certain knowledge because exact repetition is impossible due to variations in temperament, air, soil, and hidden properties (khawāṣṣ). Reflect on why this matters.
This critique undermines the Sabian reliance on empirical observation.
4
Connect to the need for a prophet
The author concludes that only a person with insight into both visible and hidden realms (a prophet) can establish true benefits and harms. Trace how the argument leads to this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن مقالة الصابئة نقول: أنتم منازعون في إثبات تأثيرات الكواكب في الأجسام السفلية كل المنازعة ولنسلم لكم ذلك تسليم المساهلة، فالسعد والنحس لم يؤثر إلا في الحسن والقبح من حيث الخلقة، وذلك غير متنازع فيه، وإنما النزاع بيننا في الحسن والقبح من حيث الأمر، ولا شك أن الله تعالى حكم في أفعال العباد بافعل ولا تفعل، وعلى ذلك الحكم جزى في الدار الآخرة. فنقول: ذلك الحكم غير معلوم ضرورة ولا استدلالا من حيث العقل، فوجب التوقف إلى أن يرد به سمع وما قالوه من نفي النبوة في الصورة البشرية فتقررت الحجة عليه في إثبات النبوة، وأما المنافع والمضار في الأشياء وكونها معلومة بالعقل فغير مسلم على الإطلاق، فإن طريقتها عند القوم وعند الطبيعيين والأطباء هو التجربة، والتجربة ما لم تتكرر لم تفد العلم والتكرار فيه غير ممكن لاختلاف طبائع الأشياء بالنسبة إلى مزاج ومزاج، وهواء وهواء، وتربة وتربة، وقط لا تحصل التجربة في شيء واحد باعتبار واحد وأيضًا فإن الخواص التي هي وراء كيفيات الأشياء وطبائعها ربما تخالف ما يحصل بالتجربة من الطبائع، والخواص عندهم من فيض النفس الكلية على ماهيات الأشياء، وربما تحصل آثار كبيرة من مجرد عدد يتركب من أعداد مخصوصة، وربما تحصل آثار مختلفة من مجرد أمزجة تتركب من عناصر مخصوصة، والمطلع على الماهيات غير الباحث عن الكيفيات والكميات فلا بد إذا من إثبات شخص له اطلاع على الكواكب وعلى ما وراء الكواكب؛ حتى يكون الكل عنده كصورة واحدة فتقرر الخاصية منافع الأشياء ومضارها من جهة الخواص، ويحمل العامة على التسليم ذلك جملةً، وسيأتي تقرير ذلك.
As for the answer to the article of the Sabians, we say: You dispute entirely in affirming the effects of the planets on sublunary bodies. Let us grant you that by way of leniency. For good fortune and ill fortune only affect good and evil in terms of constitution, and that is not disputed. Rather, the dispute between us is in good and evil in terms of command. There is no doubt that God Almighty has commanded regarding the actions of servants with 'do' and 'do not do'. And upon that command He rewards in the hereafter. We say: That command is not known necessarily nor by inference from the aspect of reason, so it is obligatory to wait until revelation comes with it. And what they said about denying prophethood in human form, the proof against it has been established in affirming prophethood. As for the benefits and harms in things and their being known by reason, it is not granted absolutely. For their method according to these people and according to the naturalists and physicians is experience. And experience, if not repeated, does not yield knowledge. Repetition in it is not possible due to the differing natures of things relative to temperament after temperament, air after air, soil after soil. Never does experience occur in one thing in one respect. Also, the properties that are beyond the qualities of things and their natures may contradict what is obtained through experience from natures. According to them, properties come from the emanation of the universal soul upon the essences of things. Great effects may arise from mere number composed of specific numbers, and different effects may arise from mere mixtures composed of specific elements. The one who has insight into essences is not the same as the researcher into qualities and quantities. Therefore, it is necessary to establish a person who has insight into the planets and what is beyond the planets, so that everything is like a single picture for him. Then the property of benefits and harms of things is established in terms of properties, and the common people are made to accept that in general. And its elaboration will come.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sabians, who held that planetary influences determine moral good and evil and that beneficial properties of things are known by reason. The author first concedes a limited point: planetary effects on natural good and evil (like health and disease) are not disputed, but the real issue is moral good and evil judged by divine command (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ min ḥaythu al-amr). He argues that such commands are not known by reason alone, so one must wait for revelation. He then attacks the claim that benefits and harms are known by reason: their method is experience, but experience is unreliable due to the impossibility of exact repetition given variations in temperament, air, soil, etc. Moreover, the hidden properties (al-khawāṣṣ) beyond those qualities may contradict experiential generalizations; these properties derive from the universal soul and can produce effects based purely on number or mixture. Thus, a person with insight into both the visible and hidden realms is needed—this is the prophet—to establish which things are truly beneficial or harmful. The passage ends by noting that further elaboration will follow.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الحسن والقبح
التجربة
الخواص
النفس الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies all experiential knowledge of benefits and harms.
Correction: The author does not deny all experiential knowledge; he denies its certainty in moral and metaphysical matters without prophetic guidance.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that experiential knowledge of benefits and harms is insufficient?
A) Because experience is always false.
B) Because repetition is impossible due to constant variation in conditions.
C) Because the Sabians reject experience.
D) Because revelation contradicts experience.

Key Takeaway
Moral commands and hidden properties cannot be known by reason or experience alone; prophetic revelation is indispensable for certainty in these matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you rely more on your own reasoning and experiences to decide what is truly good for you, or do you submit to divine revelation? Reflect on a specific decision you made recently that was based purely on your own judgment. How would that decision change if you saw revelation as the only source of certainty about moral commands and hidden harms?
?
The author argues that experience can never give certainty because conditions are always changing. Have you ever trusted your personal experience in a spiritual matter and later found it misled you? What does this lesson say about the limits of your own experience, and how can you humbly submit to revelation instead?
?
The Sabians believed that planetary influences determined human character. What modern ideologies—such as scientific materialism, psychology, or social media trends—subtly claim to offer complete knowledge of human nature without needing revelation? How does this passage expose the insufficiency of those sources and call you to anchor your worldview in prophethood?

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
Knowledge of Benefits and Harms Requires Revelation
Refutes rational knowledge of benefits and harms, proving need for prophecy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of the benefits and harms of things cannot be obtained by reason alone, and why a prophet (or divine revelation) is necessary for such knowledge.

Core Idea
Reason and experience cannot fully determine benefits and harms; revelation is necessary.

This passage from al-Shahrastani tackles a key objection to the necessity of prophethood: why can't reason alone tell us what is beneficial or harmful? The author argues that reason is insufficient, because the method of experience (tajriba) is unreliable due to variability, and because deeper properties (khawāṣṣ) go beyond observable qualities. This leads to the conclusion that a prophet with insight into the unseen is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and notice that the author begins by denying that the judgment about benefits and harms is known by reason, either necessarily or inferentially. He argues that we must wait for revelation.
The claim is the starting point for the refutation.
2
Understand the problem with experience
The author explains that the method for knowing benefits and harms is experience (tajriba), but experience requires repetition to yield knowledge. Repetition is impossible because conditions (temperament, air, soil) vary, so experience cannot be relied upon for certain knowledge.
Consider why variability makes repetition impossible.
3
Grasp the argument from properties
The author introduces the concept of properties (khawāṣṣ) that lie beyond qualities and natures. These may contradict experiential knowledge and come from the Universal Soul. Effects can arise from numbers or mixtures, not just observable qualities. Therefore, insight into quiddities (māhiyyāt) is different from investigating qualities.
Properties go beyond what is perceptible.
4
See the conclusion: need for a prophet
Since reason and experience are insufficient, a person with insight into celestial and supracelestial realities is needed. This person (a prophet) can unify all knowledge and establish the beneficial and harmful properties of things. The common people must accept this on authority.
This directly proves the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ذلك الحكم غير معلوم ضرورة ولا استدلالا من حيث العقل، فوجب التوقف إلى أن يرد به سمع وما قالوه من نفي النبوة في الصورة البشرية فتقرر الحجة عليه في إثبات النبوان، وأما المنافع والمضار في الأشياء وكونها معلومة بالعقل فغير مسلم على الإطلاق، فإن طريقتها عند القوم وعند الطبيعيين والأطباء هو التجربة، والتجربة ما لم تتكرر لم تفد العلم والتكرار فيه غير ممكن لاختلاف طبائع الأشياء بالنسبة إلى مزاج ومزاج، وهواء وهواء، وتربة وتربة، وقط لا تحصل التجربة في شيء واحد باعتبار واحد وأيضًا فإن الخواص التي هي وراء كيفيات الأشياء وطبائعها ربما تخالف ما يحصل بالتجربة من الطبائع، والخواص عندهم من فيض النفس الكلية على ماهيات الأشياء، وربما تحصل آثار كبيرة من مجرد عدد يتركب من أعداد مخصوصة، وربما تحصل آثار مختلفة من مجرد أمزجة تتركب من عناصر مخصوصة، والمطلع على الماهيات غير الباحث عن الكيفيات والكميات فلا بد إذا من إثبات شخص له اطلاع على الكواكب وعلى ما وراء الكواكب ؛ حتى يكون الكل عنده كصورة واحدة فتقرر الخاصية منافع الأشياء ومضارها من جهة الخواص، ويحمل العامة على التسليم ذلك جملةً، وسيأتي تقرير ذلك
We say: That judgment is not known necessarily nor inferentially from the standpoint of reason, so it is necessary to withhold judgment until a revelation comes concerning it. And what they said about denying prophethood in human form—the argument against it is established in proving prophethood. As for the benefits and harms in things and that they are known by reason, this is not conceded at all. For their method according to the people and the natural philosophers and physicians is experience (tajriba). And experience, if not repeated, does not yield knowledge, and repetition of it is impossible due to the differing natures of things with respect to temperament and temperament, air and air, soil and soil, and experience never occurs for one thing in one respect. Moreover, the properties (khawāṣṣ) that lie beyond the qualities (kayfiyyāt) and natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) of things may contradict what is obtained by experience regarding natures. And properties according to them come from the emanation of the Universal Soul (al-nafs al-kulliyya) upon the quiddities (māhiyyāt) of things. Sometimes great effects arise merely from a number composed of specific numbers, and sometimes different effects arise merely from mixtures composed of specific elements. The one who has insight into quiddities is not the one investigating qualities and quantities. Therefore, it is necessary to affirm a person who has insight into the celestial bodies and what is beyond the celestial bodies, so that everything for him is like a single picture, and thereby the property (khāṣṣiyya) of benefits and harms of things from the aspect of properties is established, and the common people are made to accept that as a whole. And its establishment will come later.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that knowledge of benefits and harms can be obtained by reason alone. It begins by stating that such judgments are not known by necessity or inference, so one must wait for revelation. The author then attacks the idea that benefits and harms are rationally knowable. First, he notes that the method for knowing them is experience (tajriba), which requires repetition to yield knowledge. But repetition is impossible due to the variability of conditions (temperament, air, soil). Second, he argues that properties (khawāṣṣ) beyond qualities and natures may contradict experiential knowledge. These properties are explained by philosophers as emanations from the Universal Soul upon the quiddities of things. Sometimes effects arise from numbers or mixtures of specific elements, not from observable qualities. Therefore, the person who investigates quiddities is different from the one who investigates qualities and quantities. This leads to the conclusion that a person (a prophet) with insight into celestial and supracelestial realities is needed, so that everything is unified for him, and through him the beneficial and harmful properties of things are established. The common people must accept this on authority. The author promises a fuller treatment later.

Key Terms
تجربة
خواص
