The Brahmin Objection and the Rationality of Prophecy
The author responds to the Brahmin objection by distinguishing rational in genus from actual knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Brahmin objection to prophethood by distinguishing between something being rational in its genus and being actually known by every individual.

Core Idea
A truth can be rational in kind yet unknown to many — that is why prophecy is needed.

The Brahmins argue that prophetic claims are either rational (making them unnecessary) or irrational (making them false). The author responds by introducing a key distinction: something can be rational in its nature, yet not automatically known by every person. This passage lays out that distinction and shows how it undermines the Brahmin argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Brahmin objection
The Brahmins claim that prophecy must be either rational (so intellect suffices) or irrational (so it cannot be accepted). Identify this dilemma in the passage.
This is the starting point of the author's response.
2
Grasp the distinction of rationality in genus
The author says the prophet's message is rational in itself (jins). This means its nature is accessible to reason, even if not everyone immediately understands it.
The term 'jins' is key - it refers to the category or kind.
3
Apply the analogy to knowledge
The author compares prophecy to knowledge of the properties of things: such knowledge is rational in kind, but not everyone knows it instantly. This shows the Brahmins' false dichotomy.
This analogy invalidates the sophistry (talbis) of the Brahmins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن مقالة البراهمة سيأتي على استيفاء في مسألة إثبات النبوات والرد عليهم فيما يليق بسؤالهم أن الذي يأتي به النبي معقول أو غير معقول. نقول : ما يأتي به النبي معقول في نفسه ؛ أي جنسه معقول، ويمكن أن يدركه العقل، وليس كل ما هو معقول الجنس يجب أن يعقله الإنسان، فإن العلم بخواص الأشياء وماهيات الموجودات مما هو معقول الجنس، وليس كل إنسان يدركه في الحال، فبطل التلبيس الذي تعلقوا به.
As for the answer to the statement of the Brahmins, it will come fully in the issue of establishing prophethoods and the refutation of them regarding what is appropriate to their question: that what the prophet brings is either rational or irrational. We say: What the prophet brings is rational in itself; that is, its genus is rational, and the intellect can perceive it. But not everything that is rational in genus must be comprehended by a person. For knowledge of the properties of things and the essences of existents is something rational in genus, yet not every person perceives it instantly. Thus the sophistry they clung to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage introduces the answer to the objection of the Brahmins (a Hindu group) against prophethood. The Brahmins argue that either the prophet's message is rational (hence intellect alone suffices) or irrational (hence it cannot be accepted). The author responds by distinguishing between something being rational in genus (jins) and being actually known by every individual. The content of prophecy is rational in its kind—it can be understood by the intellect in principle—but not everyone can immediately grasp it. As an analogy, knowledge of the specific properties and essences of things is rational in kind, yet not everyone knows them at once. Therefore, the Brahmins' dichotomy is false; prophecy provides knowledge that is accessible to reason but not automatically known, so it serves to guide people to truths they might not discover on their own. Thus their sophistry (talbis) is refuted.

Correction: The passage explains that something can be rational in its nature yet not understood by everyone immediately; prophecy provides guidance for such truths.

Checkpoint

C) By denying that intellect can perceive anything.
D) By claiming that all people know the same things.

Key Takeaway
Prophecy is rational in its kind, but not automatically known to all; therefore it provides necessary guidance, refuting the Brahmin objection.

Will you spend a few minutes in du‘ā asking Him to reveal what you cannot see on your own?

Ash'ari's Response to the Brahmins on Rationality of Prophecy
Ash'ari answers Brahmins' objection by distinguishing rational in genus from immediate knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's refutation of the Brahmins' claim that prophetic messages must be either universally evident or irrational.

Core Idea
Rational in genus does not mean instantly known to every mind.

The Brahmins posed a dilemma: if a prophet's message is rational, it should be obvious to all; if irrational, it is false. Ash'ari responds by distinguishing between a message being rational in its genus and being immediately grasped. He uses the example of specialized knowledge—like the properties of things—to show that rational truths often require effort to understand. This passage introduces a key principle for defending prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice that Ash'ari first presents the Brahmins' question, then gives his answer.
2
Identify the Brahmins' objection
The Brahmins ask whether what the prophet brings is rational or irrational. Understand why this question is problematic: it assumes that if rational, it must be known to everyone.
3
Focus on Ash'ari's key distinction
Ash'ari says the prophet's message is rational in its genus (jinsuhu ma'qul) but not necessarily immediately known. The example about properties and essences illustrates this.
Rational in genus means the category is accessible to reason, not that every individual fact is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن مقالة البراهمة سيأتي على استيفاء في مسألة إثبات النبوات والرد عليهم فيما يليق بسؤالهم أن الذي يأتي به النبي معقول أو غير معقول. نقول : ما يأتي به النبي معقول في نفسه ؛ أي جنسه معقول، ويمكن أن يدركه العقل، وليس كل ما هو معقول الجنس يجب أن يعقله الإنسان، فإن العلم بخواص الأشياء وماهيات الموجودات مما هو معقول الجنس، وليس كل إنسان يدركه في الحال، فبطل التلبيس الذي تعلقوا به.
As for the answer to the statement of the Brahmins, it will come in full in the issue of establishing prophethoods and the refutation of them regarding what befits their question: that what the prophet brings is either rational or irrational. We say: What the prophet brings is rational in itself; i.e., its genus is rational, and the intellect can grasp it. But not everything whose genus is rational must be grasped by a person. For knowledge of the properties of things and the quiddities of existents is something whose genus is rational, yet not every person grasps it immediately. So the sophistry they clung to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The author addresses an anticipated question from the Brahmins (a group who reject prophethood) regarding the rationality of prophetic messages. They ask: is what the prophet brings rational or irrational? The author answers that the prophet's message is rational in its genus (jinsuhu ma'qul), meaning its category is accessible to reason. However, not everything that is rational in genus must be actually known by every person. He gives the example of knowledge of the properties and essences of things: these are rational in principle, but not everyone knows them instantly. Thus, the Brahmins' confusion (talbis) is refuted.

Correction: Rational in genus means the category is accessible to reason, but specific truths within that category may require learning or revelation to be known.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari defends prophethood by showing that a message can be rational in its category without being instantly obvious to everyone.

Three Rational Views on Prophethood
Classifies three views on prophethood's rational status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main rational positions on prophethood—impossibility, obligation, and possibility—and how the Ash'arite view affirms prophethood as both possible and actual.

Core Idea
Prophethood is rationally possible, actually occurred, and neither impossible nor strictly necessary.

This passage from al-Ash'ari presents a clear classification of how different theological schools assess the rational status of prophethood. Understanding these three positions—impossibility, obligation, and possibility—helps situate the orthodox Ash'arite view as the balanced middle path. The passage also shows how the Ash'arites ground their belief in both rational possibility and historical actuality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the Arabic and translation to find the three groups and their views. Note the key terms: istiḥāla, wujūb, jawāz.
2
Understand the Ash'arite conclusion
Notice how the passage says that impossibility is negated by actual occurrence. The Ash'arites do not just say prophethood is possible; they affirm it actually happened.
3
Reflect on the importance of this classification
Ask yourself why classifying these views matters for Islamic theology. It shows how different rational judgments affect one's acceptance of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة التاسعة عشرة في إثبات النبوات وتحقيق المعجزات ووجوب عصمة الأنبياء عليهم السلام، صارت البراهمة والصابئة إلى القول باستحالة النبوات عقلا، وصارت المعتزلة وجماعة من الشيعة إلى القول بوجوب وجود النبوات عقلاً من جهة اللطف وصارت الأشعرية وجماعة من أهل السنة إلى القول بجواز وجود النبوات عقلاً ووقوعها في الوجود عيانًا، وتنتفي استحالتها بتحقيق وجودها كما ثبت تصورها بنفي استحالتها.
The nineteenth principle on establishing prophethoods, verifying miracles, and the obligation of the infallibility of the prophets, peace be upon them. The Barāhima and the Ṣābi'a held the view of the rational impossibility of prophethoods, and the Mu'tazila and a group of the Shī'a held the view of the rational obligation of the existence of prophethoods from the aspect of grace, and the Ash'arites and a group of Ahl al-Sunna held the view of the rational possibility of the existence of prophethoods and their actual occurrence in existence, and their impossibility is negated by the verification of their existence just as their conceptualization is established by the negation of their impossibility.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage classifies the theological positions on the rational status of prophethood. It begins by stating the topic: the nineteenth principle concerning prophethood, miracles, and prophetic infallibility. Then it enumerates three schools: (1) The Barāhima (Brahmanists) and Ṣābi'a (star-worshippers) who assert rational impossibility; (2) The Mu'tazila and some Shī'a who assert rational obligation from the perspective of divine grace (luṭf); (3) The Ash'arites and most Ahl al-Sunna who assert rational possibility and actual occurrence. The passage concludes that impossibility is negated by actual occurrence, just as conceptual possibility is established by negating impossibility. The Ash'arite position is the middle path, rejecting both impossibility and strict obligation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophethood must be either impossible or necessary, with no middle ground.
Correction: The Ash'arites affirm it is possible and actual, avoiding both extremes.

?

Objection: How Can a Miracle Prove a Prophet's Truthfulness?
Presents the objection that miracles do not uniquely indicate truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against using miracles as definitive proof of prophethood, and why skeptics argue that miracles are ambiguous.

Core Idea
Miracles alone do not guarantee truthfulness; they require additional criteria.

This passage presents a powerful objection from dialectical reasoning: how can a miracle uniquely indicate a prophet's truthfulness when rational alternatives exist? The objector systematically dismantles the evidential force of miracles, questioning the link between the miraculous act and the claimant's veracity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure of the argument – it proceeds through a series of questions and possibilities.
The objector is presenting a challenge that the author (Sanusi) will later answer.
2
Identify the main lines of attack
The objection attacks the evidential value of miracles from multiple angles: the ambiguity of the connection between miracle and claim, the contingency of miracles on God's will, and the problem of non-occurrence.
List each sub-argument as you find it.
3
Consider the underlying logic
Ask yourself: why does the objector say that even a miraculous act does not necessarily indicate truthfulness? How does the possibility of alternative causes weaken the argument?
This step helps you appreciate the skeptical position.
4
Note the unresolved nature
Remember that this is only the objection; the author's response is not included in this passage. The lesson presents the challenge, not the final word.
Your understanding of the objection prepares you for the subsequent defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقالوا : بم يعرف صدق المدعي وقد شاركناه في النوعية والصورة؟ أبنفس دعواه وقد علم أن الخبر محتمل للصدق والكذب أم بدليل آخر يقترن به؟ وذلك الدليل إن كان مقدورًا له فهو أيضًا مقدورنا ، وإن لم يكن مقدورًا له ولكنه مقدور بقدرة الله تعالى فلا يخلو إما أن يكون فعلا معتادًا ولا يختص ذلك بهذا المدعي فلا يكون دليلا، وإن كان فعلا خارقا فمن أي وجه يدل على صدقه، والفعل من حيث هو فعل لا يدل على الاختصاص بشخص معين سوى اقترانه بنفس دعواه؟ وللاقتران أسباب أخر محتملة كما لخرق العادات أحوال مختلفة، وإذا احتملت أما نفس الوجوه عقلا لم تتعين جهة الدلالة فانحسم باب الدليل من كل وجه، الاقتران فربما لا يتفق في بعض الأحوال؛ إذ الفعل فعل الله تعالى وهو منوط بمشيئته، فكيف يوجب المدعي على الله فعلا يفعله في تلك الحاله)، وليس يدعي النبي على أصلكم هذه الدعوى التي قدرتموها فإنه يحيل خلق الآيات، على مشيئة الله تعالى كما أخبر كتابكم : ( قُلْ إِنَّمَا الْآيَاتُ عِندَ اللَّهِ وَمَا يُشْعِرُكُمْ أَنَّهَا إِذَا جَاءَتْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ ) [الأنعام: 109]، وكم من نبي سئل إظهار الآية فلم يظهر في الحال على يده، فلئن كان ظهور الآية في بعض الأوقات دليلًا على صدقه، فعدم ظهورها في بعض الأوقات يجب أن يكون أيضا دليلا على كذبه، وليس الأمر كذلك على أصلكم فإذا قال واحد من عرض الناس وهو مجهول الحال عند الكل : إني رسول الله إليكم فطولب بالآية وجب عليه التضرع إلى الله في استدعاء الآية الخارقة للعادة على مقتضى الدعوى، ووجب على الله إجابته في الحال إلى ذلك كان الإيجاب أولى على الله ورسوله من الخلق، وهو عكس ما رمتم إثباته.
They said: By what is the truthfulness of the claimant known, when we share with him in species and form? By his own claim, while it is known that speech is susceptible to truth and falsehood, or by another evidence connected to it? That evidence, if it is within his power, is also within our power; and if it is not within his power but is within the power of God Almighty, then it is either a customary action, and it is not specific to this claimant, so it is not evidence; or if it is a miraculous action, then in what respect does it indicate his truthfulness? Action as such does not indicate specificity to a particular person apart from its connection to his very claim. And for connection there are other possible causes, just as for violations of custom there are different states. And if the aspects themselves admit probability rationally, the direction of indication is not determined, so the door of evidence is blocked from every aspect. Connection may not occur in some instances, for the action is God's action and is dependent on His will. So how can the claimant impose upon God an action that He does at that moment? And the prophet, according to your doctrine, does not claim this claim that you have imagined, for he refers the creation of miracles to the will of God Almighty, as your Book informs: 'Say: The signs are only with God, and what will make you perceive that when they come, they will not believe?' [6:109]. And how many a prophet was asked to show a miracle and it did not appear immediately at his hand? So if the appearance of the miracle at some times is evidence of his truthfulness, then its non-appearance at some times must also be evidence of his falsehood. But it is not so according to your doctrine. So when any one of the common people, whose condition is unknown to all, says: I am the messenger of God to you, and is asked for a miracle, he must supplicate to God to request a miracle breaking custom in accordance with the claim, and it is incumbent upon God to answer him immediately to that. Then the obligation would be more incumbent upon God and His messenger than upon the creation, which is the opposite of what you intend to establish.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection from opponents against using miracles as evidence for prophethood. The argument is structured as a series of dialectical questions: How can we distinguish a true prophet from others when they share human nature? Not by mere claim, because claims can be false. If the evidence is a miracle, it must be either within the claimant's power (then others can do it) or God's power. If it's a customary action, it's not specific. If it's a miraculous action, it still does not necessarily indicate truthfulness because the connection between the miracle and the claim could have other causes. Moreover, miracles are subject to God's will; sometimes they are withheld. If appearance is evidence, then non-appearance would be evidence of falsehood, but that is not the case. Finally, if any person could demand a miracle from God and God must respond, then the obligation would be upon God and His messenger, contradicting the opponent's own position. The objection systematically tries to undermine the evidential value of miracles.

Key Terms
صدق المدعي
خرق العادة
اقتران
مشيئة الله
دليل
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any miracle automatically proves prophethood, without additional criteria.
Correction: The objection shows that miracles can be ambiguous; the connection between the miracle and the claim must be established, and other factors like God's will and the possibility of alternative explanations must be considered.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what problem arises if a miracle is a customary action (فعل معتاد)?
A) It is impossible for a prophet to perform
B) It does not specifically belong to the claimant and thus is not evidence
C) It is too easily replicated by others
D) It depends entirely on God's will

Key Takeaway
The objection highlights that miracles alone are insufficient to prove prophethood without resolving ambiguity about their connection to the claimant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a dramatic sign from God as a condition for your faith in a matter? How does this objection expose the weakness of relying on such demands rather than on consistent, rational evidence?
?
This passage shows that even a miracle can be ambiguous. In your own spiritual journey, have you dismissed a truth because it lacked the external validation you expected? What does that reveal about your own heart's posture toward divine guidance?
?
The objectors here use logical arguments to block the door of evidence for prophethood. Are there areas in your life where you similarly resist evidence that demands a change? Where are you playing the role of the skeptic to avoid submitting to a truth you already know?

Objection: The Need for Prolonged Examination Before Accepting a Prophet
Objection against immediate prophetic acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of a kalam objection that argues requiring prior theological proof makes prophetic acceptance impossible.

Core Idea
The demand for prior theological proof would silence prophets.

This passage presents a sharp objection against the prophetic call to immediate faith. The objector reasons that to accept a prophet, one must first know God—His existence, attributes, and the possibility of prophethood—all of which requires time and examination. The prophet's demand for instant belief seems unjust if he does not grant this time. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objector's tone and the chain of reasoning.
The objection aims to challenge the prophet's demand for immediate acceptance.
2
Identify the chain of required knowledge
The objector lists a sequence: knowledge of God's existence, His attributes, then the possibility of prophethood, then the specific miracle. Each step builds on the previous.
This chain is meant to show how much time is needed.
3
Ponder the dilemma
The objector poses a dilemma: if the prophet does not grant time, he wrongs the person; if he does, it delays acceptance. Ask yourself: how might a kalam scholar respond?
The response is not given here, but thinking about it prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مسألة النظر والاستمهال فيه يفضي إلى إفحام الأنبياء، فإنه إذا ادعى الرسالة فقال المدعو : العلم بالرسول يترتب على العلم بالمرسل، والعلم به ليس يقع بديهة وضرورة فلا بد من نظر واستدلال في أفعاله استدلال بتغيرها على حدوثها، وبحدوثها على ثبوت محدثها، ثم النظر في قدمه ثم النظر في وحدانيته ثم النظر في صفاته الواجبة له والجائزة عليه، وما يستحيل في صفته، وبعد ذلك ينظر في جواز انبعاث الرسل ، ثم في معجزة هذا المدعي بعينه. فيقول : أمهلني حتى أنظر في هذه الأبواب، وأقطع هذه المراتب، فهل يجب على النبي إمهاله أم لا ؟ فإن لم يمهل فقد ظلمه ؛ إذ كلفه ما لا يطيق، وذلك أن العلم إذا لم يحصل إلا بالنظر والنظر يستدعي مهلة وزمانًا ولم يمهله فقد ظلمه.
Then the issue of examination and seeking respite leads to silencing the prophets. For when he claims the message, the addressed person says: Knowledge of the messenger follows from knowledge of the sender, and knowledge of Him does not occur intuitively and necessarily, so there must be examination and inference regarding His actions, inferring from their changeability to their temporal origination, and from their temporal origination to the existence of their Originator, then examination of His pre-eternity, then examination of His oneness, then examination of His necessary attributes and those possible for Him, and what is impossible in His attribute. After that, he examines the possibility of sending messengers, then the miracle of this particular claimant. So he says: Give me respite until I examine these topics and traverse these stages. Is it obligatory for the prophet to grant him respite or not? If he does not grant him respite, then he has wronged him, because he charged him with what he cannot bear. That is because knowledge is not obtained except through examination, and examination requires a period of time, so if he did not grant him respite, he has wronged him.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: the need for prolonged theological examination before accepting a prophet. The objector argues that knowledge of God must precede knowledge of the prophet. One must first prove God's existence through the temporal origination of actions, then establish God's attributes, then the possibility of sending messengers, then check the miracle of the claimant. This entire process requires time. The objector then poses a dilemma: if the prophet does not grant time, he wrongs the person by demanding what he cannot immediately know; if he grants time, it leads to stalling. The objection aims to show that the prophet's demand for immediate belief is unjust.

الاستمهال
إفحام الأنبياء
بديهة
ضرورة
حدوث
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what must be known before accepting a prophet's claim?
A) The prophet's lineage
B) The existence and attributes of God
C) The moral character of the prophet
D) The history of previous prophets

Key Takeaway
This objection reveals the assumption that knowledge of God is solely discursive, which the Ash'arite response will challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a claim that demands your immediate submission—whether from a prophet or from Allah's command—do you instinctively seek to postpone your response by demanding more proofs? Which of the stages listed in this objection do you find yourself using as an excuse to delay faith?
?
Have you ever expected Allah to provide you with a complete, step-by-step logical demonstration before you would obey a clear command? In what areas of your life right now are you demanding 'respite' rather than submitting immediately?
?
The objector here insists that knowledge of God requires an elaborate chain of reasoning. But do you truly believe that your knowledge of Allah is merely academic? Or is there a part of you that knows Him directly, yet you still choose to ignore that knowledge? What would change if you acted on that intuitive knowledge today?

The Dilemma of Respite: A Skeptic's Challenge to Prophethood
Argument that requiring time for investigation leads to an impasse for the prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical skeptical argument that requiring rational investigation (nazar) before accepting a prophet creates an inescapable dilemma, and how it challenges the very possibility of prophetic knowledge.

Core Idea
The skeptic's request for investigation leaves the prophet with no good option: either oppress or be ignored.

This passage presents a powerful objection from the skeptic against the claim of prophethood. The skeptic argues that belief in a prophet requires prior knowledge of God, which itself demands time-consuming rational investigation. This creates a dilemma: if the prophet does not grant time, he wrongs the skeptic; if he does, the skeptic may never conclude, rendering the prophetic call ineffective. The author then deepens the challenge by questioning how the prophet himself can be certain of his revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: the skeptic presents a scenario with two outcomes.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find where the passage presents the two possibilities for the prophet: granting or denying respite. Understand the consequences of each.
The dilemma is central to the skeptic's argument.
3
Examine the additional epistemological questions
After the dilemma, the passage raises further questions about how the prophet knows that the message is from God and how the angel knows. Note how these deepen the challenge.
These questions target the foundation of prophetic knowledge itself.
4
Reflect on the goal of the argument
Ask yourself: why does the skeptic call this 'silencing the prophets'? How does the argument aim to make prophetic claims impossible to verify?
This connects to the broader theme of methodology in kalam.
5
Consider a possible response
Think about how a Muslim theologian might respond. The passage itself does not provide the answer, but it sets up the problem that later kalam arguments aim to solve.
This prepares you for subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مسألة النظر والاستمهال فيه يفضي إلى إفحام الأنبياء، فإنه إذا ادعى الرسالة فقال المدعو: العلم بالرسول يترتب على العلم بالمرسل، والعلم به ليس يقع بديهة وضرورة فلا بد من نظر واستدلال في أفعاله استدلال بتغيرها على حدوثها، وبحدوثها على ثبوت محدثها، ثم النظر في قدمه ثم النظر في وحدانيته ثم النظر في صفاته الواجبة له والجائزة عليه، وما يستحيل في صفته، وبعد ذلك ينظر في جواز انبعاث الرسل، ثم في معجزة هذا المدعي بعينه. فيقول: أمهلني حتى أنظر في هذه الأبواب، وأقطع هذه المراتب، فهل يجب على النبي إمهاله أم لا؟ فإن لم يمهل فقد ظلمه؛ إذ كلفه ما لا يطيق، وذلك أن العلم إذا لم يحصل إلا بالنظر والنظر يستدعي مهلة وزمانًا ولم يمهله فقد ظلمه وأمره بمجرد التقليد والتقليد إما كفر وإما قبيح، وإذا أمهله فيلزمه أن لا يعود إليه حتى ينتهي انظر نهايته، ولا يتقرر ذلك بزمان معين، بل يختلف الحال باختلاف الأشخاص وأذهانهم وكياستهم في مدارج النظر، وأيضًا فمن استمهل ووجب إمهاله يعطل النبي عن الدعوة فإن عاد إليهم قالوا: نحن بعد في مهلة النظر فليس لك علينا حكم بعد الإمهال ولم يثبت عندنا صدقك فنتبعك، ثم إذا كان النبي مبلغا أو يسمع من سمع منه، فبم عرف النبي بأن المتكلم هو الله؟ أو بم عرف أن المتوسط ملك إليه؟ وبم عرف ذلك الملك أن الرب هو الأمر المتكلم؟ فما الجواب عن هذه المسألة؟ هذا كلامنا على نفس الدعوى.
Then the issue of investigation and seeking respite therein leads to silencing the prophets. For if someone claims prophethood and the addressee says: Knowledge of the messenger depends on knowledge of the sender, and knowledge of Him does not occur intuitively or necessarily, so there must be investigation and inference regarding his actions, inferring from their changeability their temporal origination, and from their temporal origination the existence of their originator, then investigation into His pre-eternity, then investigation into His oneness, then investigation into His necessary and possible attributes, and what is impossible in His attribute, and after that he investigates the permissibility of sending messengers, then into the miracle of this particular claimant. He says: Give me respite until I investigate these topics and traverse these stages. So is the prophet obliged to grant him respite or not? If he does not grant respite, he has wronged him, because he burdened him with what he cannot bear. This is because knowledge, if it is only attained through investigation, and investigation requires time and duration, and he did not give him respite, then he has wronged him and commanded him mere blind following (taqlid), and blind following is either unbelief or ugly. And if he grants him respite, it follows that he should not return to him until he reaches the end of his investigation, and that is not determined by a fixed time, but rather the situation varies according to the persons, their intellects, and their acumen in the stages of investigation. Also, whoever asks for respite and it is obligatory to grant him respite disables the prophet from his call, for if he returns to them they say: We are still in the respite of investigation, so you have no authority over us after the respite, and your truthfulness has not been established for us that we may follow you. Then, if the prophet is a messenger or hears from one who heard from him, how does the prophet know that the speaker is God? Or how does he know that the intermediary is an angel from Him? And how does that angel know that the Lord is the commanding speaker? What is the answer to this question? This is our speech concerning the claim itself.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection from the skeptic against the prophet. It is a type of argument often used in kalam to challenge the viability of prophetic claims. The skeptic argues that rational investigation (nazar) is necessary before accepting a prophet, but such investigation requires time (istimhal). The author poses a dilemma: if the prophet does not grant time, he oppresses the skeptic by demanding belief without sufficient evidence (taqlid), which is either unbelief or morally wrong. If he grants time, the skeptic will never conclude his investigation because it has no fixed endpoint, varying by individual. Moreover, even after the investigation, the skeptic can always claim he is still in the process. The author then raises further epistemological questions: how does the prophet himself know that the message is from God, and how does the angel know? This extends the objection to the very possibility of prophetic knowledge. The passage concludes by stating that this is their argument concerning the claim itself (i.e., the claim of prophethood).

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
الاستمهال (istimhal)
إفحام الأنبياء (ifham al-anbiya')
التقليد (taqlid)
الرسالة (risala)
المعجزة (mu'jiza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the skeptic is demanding a miracle, but here he demands a prior rational investigation of God's existence and attributes before accepting the miracle.
Correction: The objection is not about the miracle itself but about the rational prerequisites needed to recognize the miracle as evidence.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the skeptic argue the prophet faces if the skeptic requests time for investigation?
A) The prophet must either perform a miracle or admit he is not a prophet.
B) The prophet must either grant time and lose his followers, or deny time and oppress the skeptic.
C) The prophet must either prove God's existence immediately or be silent.
D) The prophet must either accept the skeptic's demands or be declared a liar.

Key Takeaway
The skeptic's demand for investigation creates a logical impasse that threatens the very possibility of prophetic mission, highlighting the need for a different epistemological foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a time when you used the need for 'more proof' or 'more understanding' as a reason to delay obeying a command from Allah? What does this passage reveal about the hidden motive behind such delays?
?
The skeptic's demand for investigation traps the prophet in an impossible situation. Reflect on your own relationship with the Prophet ﷺ: are you approaching his message with a sincere desire to follow, or with conditions that must be met first? What would it cost you to submit unconditionally?
?
This passage shows that even the demand for a rational foundation can become a tool to avoid submission. When you read the Quran or hear a hadith, what is your immediate response? Do you feel the weight of the call, or do you instinctively begin to 'investigate' as a way to keep it at a distance? How can you change that reaction today?

The Skeptic's Denial of Miracles
Skeptic denies miracles as impossible and challenges opponent for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptic's argument that miracles are intrinsically impossible and the challenge he poses to the defender of prophethood.

Core Idea
Miracles are not just improbable — they are claimed to be impossible by the skeptic.

In this passage, the skeptic takes a radical position: he does not merely question the evidence for miracles; he denies their very possibility. He lists several well-known miracles and asserts that such events are impossible. This sets the stage for the response that follows in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the list of miracles and the skeptic's strong language: 'impossible of existence'.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the full argument is not given here.
2
Identify the miracles listed
The skeptic mentions six specific miracles: staff turning into snake, reviving the dead, splitting the sea, she-camel from rock, pebbles glorifying, and splitting the moon. Notice that these are well-known in Islamic tradition.
3
Understand the skeptic's challenge
After denying their possibility, the skeptic asks: 'What is your proof for its occurrence?' This forces the defender to first prove that such events are possible before arguing that they prove prophethood.
Think about how you would respond to this challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام على بيئة الدعوى فنحن أولا ننكر ما حكيتموه عن الأنبياء من قلب العصا حية، وإحياء الموتى، وفلق البحر، وخروج الناقة من الصخرة الصماء، وتسبيح الحصى، وانشقاق القمر، فإن جنس ذلك مستحيل الوجود فنحن أولا نمنع حصولها، فماذا دليلكم على وقوع ذلك؟ ثم بم تنكرون على من يرد الخوارق إلى
As for speech concerning the environment of the claim, we first deny what you have narrated from the prophets: the turning of the staff into a snake, reviving the dead, splitting the sea, the emergence of the she-camel from the solid rock, the glorification of pebbles, and the splitting of the moon. For the entire category of that is impossible of existence, so we first prevent its occurrence. So what is your proof for its occurrence? Then how do you object to one who attributes the miraculous events to...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is the second part of the skeptic's objection. Here, the skeptic attacks the very possibility of miracles (khawariq). He lists several well-known miracles attributed to prophets in Islamic tradition, such as Moses' staff turning into a snake, reviving the dead, splitting the sea, the she-camel emerging from rock, pebbles glorifying God, and the splitting of the moon. The skeptic asserts that such things are intrinsically impossible (mustahil al-wujud), so he denies their occurrence outright. He then challenges the opponent to provide evidence for their occurrence. The passage ends with a question: how can the opponent object to someone who attributes miraculous events to something else (likely natural causes or illusion)? The sentence is cut off, so the full argument is not completed in this window.

المستحيل (mustahil)
بيئة الدعوى (bi'at al-da'wa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the skeptic accepts miracles but questions their evidential value.
Correction: Here the skeptic denies the very possibility of miracles, claiming they are impossible.

Checkpoint

B) He denies that any miracles have occurred because they are impossible.
C) He argues that miracles are possible but not proof of prophethood.
D) He accepts the miracles but questions their transmission.

Key Takeaway
The skeptic's first move is to deny the possibility of miracles, forcing the defender to establish that they are possible before arguing from them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Rational Possibility of Prophecy and the Need for a Proof
Establishes the rational possibility of prophecy and the need for a determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim to prophethood is rationally possible and why a proof is necessary to distinguish true prophets from false claimants.

Core Idea
Prophethood is rationally possible; divinity is not — and proof decides truth.

In this passage, the author distinguishes the claim of prophethood from the claim of divinity. While divinity is rationally impossible, prophethood falls under rational possibilities. However, the mere possibility does not guarantee truth; a proof (dalīl) is required to tip the scale toward truth. This lesson establishes the rational foundation for authenticating prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the opening quotation from the Quran and the logical flow.
2
Identify the two claims compared
Find where the author contrasts prophethood with divinity. Note that the claim of divinity is said to be impossible, while prophethood is not.
The phrase 'لا يضاهي استحالته استحالة دعوى الإلهية' is key.
3
Understand 'rational possibilities'
Look at the term 'jāʾizāt ʿaqliyya' (rational possibilities). The author places prophethood in this category, meaning it is conceptually admissible by reason.
This classification is crucial for the argument.
4
Grasp the need for a proof
The passage concludes that without a proof, the claim remains ambiguous between truth and falsehood. Truth is only preferred over falsehood by a proof.
The word 'دليل' (proof) is the determinant that resolves the ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختيار والاصطفاء) وَرَبُّكَ يَخلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وتختارُ ، فلا استحالة في هذه المراتب، ولا استحالة في نفس الدعوى، ولا يضاهي استحالته استحالة دعوى الإلهية، بل هو من الجائزات العقلية فله الواجبات الحكمية، فبقي كون الخبر والدعوى على احتمال الصدق والكذب، وإنما ترجح الصدق على الكذب بدليل.
And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses, so there is no impossibility in these ranks, and no impossibility in the claim itself, and its impossibility does not resemble the impossibility of the claim of divinity. Rather, it is among the rational possibilities, so it has obligatory [features] from the divine law. Thus, the report and the claim remain subject to the possibility of truth and falsehood, and truth is only preferred over falsehood by a proof.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming the rational possibility of prophethood. The author states that there is no impossibility in the claim of prophethood itself, distinguishing it from the claim of divinity, which is rationally impossible. Prophethood falls under ‘rational possibilities’ (jāʾizāt ʿaqliyya), but once claimed, it must be supported by evidence to determine that it is true rather than false. The key point is that the claim is inherently ambiguous, and a determinant (dalīl) is needed to incline toward truth.

Key Terms
جائزات عقلية
دليل
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is rationally possible, its truth is automatically known without evidence.
Correction: The passage shows that rational possibility leaves the claim in doubt; a proof is required to establish truth.

Checkpoint
How does the author differentiate the claim of prophethood from the claim of divinity?
A) Prophethood is rationally impossible, divinity is possible.
B) Prophethood is rationally possible, divinity is rationally impossible.
C) Both are equally possible.
D) Both are equally impossible.

Key Takeaway
The claim to prophethood is rationally possible, but its truth can only be established through a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says truth is only preferred over falsehood by a proof. What proof do you base your faith on? Is it rational evidence, emotional comfort, or blind following? Are you willing to re-evaluate your belief based on the very standard the passage sets?
?
The author contrasts the impossibility of divinity with the possibility of prophethood. Do you sometimes treat prophethood as if it is as obvious as the existence of God, neglecting to seek the proof? What would change in your faith if you actively sought the evidence that distinguishes truth from falsehood, starting today?

The First Proof: Miracle as Confirmation by Analogy
Presents the first method: miracle as confirmation by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle, when conjoined with a prophetic claim and free from opposition, functions as God's definitive confirmation of the prophet's truthfulness, analogous to a king's act confirming a messenger.

Core Idea
A miracle is God's silent 'yes' to a prophet's claim, proven by analogy with worldly signs.

Theologians need a rational method to establish that a miracle truly confirms a prophet. This passage introduces the first method: a logical analogy that compares a prophetic miracle to a king's action confirming a messenger. By analyzing the circumstances, we can see that a miracle accompanied by a challenge and without counter-evidence yields certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Notice the logical flow from the general principle to the analogy and back to the conclusion.
The analogy is central; try to visualize the scenario.
2
Understand the analogy (king behind curtain)
Follow the story: a commoner claims to be a messenger of a hidden king and requests the king to move a curtain as a sign. The king moves it immediately. This proves the claimant's truthfulness.
The king's action is a miracle in the analogy because it happens contrary to custom and at the request.
3
Identify the three conditions for certainty
The passage explicitly lists three matters that together constitute a definitive indication: the miracle itself (khāriq li-l-ʿāda), its conjunction with the claim (muqārin li-l-daʿwa), and its freedom from opposition (salāma ʿan al-muʿāraḍa).
These conditions mirror the analogy: the movement (miracle) is done at the request (claim) and without anyone else moving the curtain (no opposition).
4
See how they combine to yield certainty
The combination of these three circumstances functions like verbal confirmation. Just as we know the king intended to confirm the messenger by his action, we know God intended to confirm the prophet by the miracle.
This certainty is akin to knowledge obtained from circumstantial and verbal indications (qarāʾin al-ḥāl wa al-maqāl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين طرق في إثبات المرجح؛ أحدها أن اقتران المعجزة بدعوى النبي نازل منزلة التصديق بالقول، وذلك أنه متى عرف من سنة الله تعالى أنه لا يظهر أمرًا خارقا للعادة على يدي من يدعي الرسالة عند وقت التحدي والاستدعاء إلا لتصديقه فيما يجري به، واجتماع هذه الأركان انتهض قرينة قطعية دالة على صدق المدعي، فكان المعجزة بالفعل كالتصديق شفاه بالقول، ونحن نعلم قطعا في صورة من يدعي الرسالة من ملك حاضر محتجب بستر، والجماعة على كثرتها حضور ثم إن رجلا من عرض الناس إذا قام بين يدي ذلك الجمع الكثير من الخلق، وقال: أيها الناس إني رسول هذا الملك إليكم، وآية صدقي في دعواي أنه يحرك هذا الستر إذا استدعيت منه، ثم قال: أيها الملك إن كنت صادقا في دعواي الرسالة عنك فحرك هذا الستر، فحرك في الحال، علم قطعا ويقينا بقرينة الحال أنه أراد بذلك الفعل تصديق المدعي، ونزل التحريك منه على خلاف العادة منزلة التصديق بالقول، ولم يشك واحد من أهل الجمع أن الأمر على ما يجري به المدعي، فكذلك في صورة مسألتنا هذه، فإذا المرجح للصدق هي القرائن الحاصلة من اجتماع أمور كثيرة؛ منها الخارق للعادة ومنها كونه مقرونا بالدعوى، ومنها سلامته عن المعارضة، فانتهضت هذه القرائن بمجموعها دالة على صدق المدعي نازلة منزلة التصديق بالقول، وذلك مثل العلم الحاصل من سائر القرائن؛ أعني قرائن الحال وقرائن المقال.
The theologians have methods for establishing the determinant. One of them is that the conjunction of a miracle with the claim of a prophet occupies the station of verbal confirmation. That is because, whenever it is known from the established practice of God Almighty that He does not manifest a miraculous event at the hands of one who claims prophethood at the time of challenge and request except to confirm him in what he claims, and the combination of these conditions constitutes a definitive indication of the claimant’s truthfulness, then the miracle in act is like confirmation spoken with words. We know definitively in the case of someone claiming to be a messenger from a present king concealed behind a curtain, with a large group present, then a common man stands before that large crowd and says, ‘O people, I am the messenger of this king to you, and the sign of my truthfulness is that he will move this curtain when I ask him.’ Then he says, ‘O king, if I am truthful in my claim of being your messenger, move this curtain.’ Then the king moves it immediately. One knows definitively and with certainty by the circumstance that he intended by that act to confirm the claimant, and his moving contrary to custom accepts the station of verbal confirmation. No one in the gathering doubts the matter is as the claimant says. Similarly in our case, the determinant of truthfulness is the indications arising from the conjunction of many matters: the miracle, its being conjoined with the claim, and its being free from opposition. These indications collectively confirm the truthfulness of the claimant, holding the station of verbal confirmation, like the knowledge obtained from other indications, namely circumstantial and verbal indications.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several methods used by theologians to prove that a miracle confirms a prophetic claim. The key analogy is that of a king behind a curtain: when a claimant asks for a sign and it occurs, the audience knows the king intended to support him. Similarly, when a miracle occurs at the time of a prophetic challenge, it functions as God's endorsement. The author emphasizes that the miracle must be accompanied by the claim, be miraculous, and be free from any counter-challenge or opposition. The combination of these circumstances yields certainty. The author compares this to how we know things from circumstantial evidence (qarāʾin al-ḥāl) and verbal evidence (qarāʾin al-maqāl).

Key Terms
معجزة
تحدي
قرينة
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle alone proves prophethood.
Correction: The passage stresses that a miracle must be conjoined with a direct challenge and free from alternative explanations. Only then does it function as confirmation.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, what does the king's moving the curtain represent?
A) The miracle itself
B) The verbal confirmation of the claimant
C) The crowd's approval
D) The claimant's eloquence

Key Takeaway
A miracle functions as God's confirmation of a prophet when it is accompanied by a challenge and free from any counterevidence, just as a king's action confirms a messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the analogy of the king behind the curtain, do you personally feel the same level of certainty about the Prophet's (peace be upon him) truthfulness as the crowd felt about the messenger? If not, what is the barrier in your heart that prevents that certainty? What would it take for you to remove it?
?
The source says that the miracle is a 'definitive indication' of prophethood. Have you ever treated any of the Prophet's miracles (like the splitting of the moon) as less than a definitive, certain confirmation of his message? If so, what is at the root of that hesitation? Commit today to affirming it with full certainty.
?
Imagine you were among the crowd when the king moved the curtain. Would you have any doubt? Yet many doubt the Prophet's miracles. What does that say about the condition of your heart? What step can you take today to strengthen your certainty in the truth of his message?

Prophethood

Miracles as Evidence of Truthfulness
Presents a second proof: miracles indicate truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle indicates the prophet's truthfulness and why it cannot be granted to a liar.

Core Idea
A miracle confirms truth, not falsehood.

This lesson presents a second argument for prophethood. It explains that a miracle, performed in response to a prophet's prayer, indicates his special status with God. If a liar were given a miracle, the sign of truth would become evidence for falsehood, which is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author builds the argument from the miracle's attributes to its implication for truthfulness.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify how the miracle indicates power, will, knowledge, and then the prophet's truthfulness. Understand the role of 'response to prayer' as the key indicator.
3
Grasp the contradiction
Consider why a miracle for a liar would turn evidence of truth into evidence of falsehood. This is the core of the proof.
This shows the necessity of the prophet's truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر الآية الخارقة للعادة كما دلت بوقوعها على قدرة الفاعل وباختصاصها على إرادته وبأحكامها على علمه كذلك دلت بوقوعها مستجابة لدعاء الداعي لا لدعوى المدعي على أن له عند الله حالة صدق ومقالة حق، ومن كانت دعوته مستجابة عند الله يستحيل أن يكون في دعواه كاذبًا على الله تعالى وهذا هو حقيقة النبوة، وذلك أن استجابته في أمور لا يقدر الخلق على ذلك دليل على صدق هذا الداعي، ولو قدر الداعي كاذبًا في نفسه على الله أقبح كذب وهو الرسالة عنه بما لم يرسل انقلب دليل الصدق دليلا على الكذب وهو متناقض. ونحن لا ننكر أن يكون شخص من الأشخاص مستجاب الدعوة ثم ينقلب حاله إلى حالة الأشقياء، كما لا ننكر أن يظهر خارق للعادة على يد ساحر، لكن الشرط في الأمرين واحد؛ وهو أن يكون المدعي في حال ما يدعي مستجاب الدعوة.
Another aspect: the miraculous sign, just as it indicates by its occurrence the power of the Agent, by its specific character His will, and by its perfect order His knowledge, likewise it indicates by its occurrence in response to the prayer of the supplicant (not the claim of the claimant) that he has a state of truthfulness and a right saying with God. And whoever’s prayer is answered by God, it is impossible for him to be lying in his claim about God Almighty, and this is the reality of prophethood. That is because His answering him in matters that creatures cannot do is evidence of his truthfulness. If the supplicant were a liar against God with the worst lie, namely conveying a message from Him when He did not send him, then the evidence of truthfulness would turn into evidence for falsehood, which is contradictory. We do not deny that a person might have his prayers answered and then his state changes to that of the wretched, just as we do not deny that a miracle may appear at the hands of a sorcerer. However, the condition in both matters is one: that the claimant at the time of his claim is one whose prayers are answered.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents a second method: the miracle itself indicates the truthfulness of the prophet because it occurs in response to a specific prayer for confirmation. The author argues that just as a miracle shows God's power, will, and knowledge, it also shows that the person requesting it has a special status. It is impossible for God to grant such a sign to a liar, as that would lead to a contradiction (the sign of truth becoming evidence for falsehood). The passage then addresses an objection: what about a person who later becomes wretched or a sorcerer? The author replies that the condition is that at the time of the claim, the claimant's prayers must be answered. Later changes or sorcerers performing miracles do not invalidate the proof because the context and timing matter.

Key Terms
استجابة الدعاء
دعوى
متناقض
حالة صدق
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any miracle proves prophethood, regardless of context.
Correction: The passage specifies that the miracle must be in response to a prayer for confirmation, not just any supernatural event.

Checkpoint
Why would a miracle performed for a liar be contradictory?
A) Because liars cannot perform miracles.
B) Because it would turn the sign of truth into evidence of falsehood.
C) Because God never answers the prayers of sinners.
D) Because miracles only occur for prophets.

Key Takeaway
A miracle given in response to a prayer for confirmation proves the prophet's truthfulness, because its opposite would be a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken a personal experience or answered prayer as confirmation that you are on the right path, even though your intentions might have been mixed? How can you ensure that you are not using God's signs to justify your own desires?
?
This lesson teaches that God does not support a liar with a miracle. Reflect on your own speech: do you ever claim to speak on behalf of God (like saying 'God told me' or 'God wills this') without certainty? What accountability does that carry?
?
Imagine you are confronted with a situation where a sign seems to confirm a false claim. The lesson says this is impossible. How would you re-examine your assumptions if you found yourself doubting a recognized miracle? What would you need to surrender to God?

Addressing Objections to the Miracle Argument
Responds to objections about changing states and sorcerers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author addresses objections about later changes in a prophet's state and miracles performed by sorcerers.

Core Idea
Context and timing preserve the argument's validity.

This lesson follows up on the miracle argument by addressing potential objections. The author anticipates concerns about a prophet who later becomes wretched or a sorcerer who performs miracles. He clarifies that the key condition is the claimant's state at the time of the claim: his prayers must be answered then.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two examples: a person whose state changes and a sorcerer.
2
Understand the condition
The author states that the condition for the proof is that the claimant's prayers are answered at the time he makes the claim. Later changes or sorcerers do not invalidate the argument.
This shows the proof is not weakened by exceptions.
3
Apply the condition
Reflect on how this condition preserves the logical force of the original argument.

What is the condition that preserves the miracle argument despite later changes or sorcerers?
A) The miracle must be performed publicly.
B) The claimant's prayers must be answered at the time of the claim.
C) The claimant must never sin after the claim.
D) Sorcerers cannot perform real miracles.

Key Takeaway
The miracle argument remains valid as long as the claimant's prayer is answered at the moment of the claim, regardless of later changes or miracles by others.

?
When you see someone perform an impressive spiritual act—healing, prophecy, or answered prayer—do you automatically assume they are a true prophet or saint? This lesson warns that even a sorcerer can produce miracles. How will you test the source of such signs in your own life, especially in times when you desperately want to believe?

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood: Refuting the Skeptics
Refutes skeptics' objection that miracles do not prove prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conjunction of a miracle with a prophetic claim is a valid proof of truthfulness, using the analogy of divine signs and the examples of Adam and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Miracles prove prophethood just as signs prove God's existence—both are divine definitions.

Skeptics argue that a miracle attached to a claim does not prove truthfulness. In this passage, al-Shahrastani refutes this by drawing an analogy with how God proves His own divinity through signs. He explains that definition can be by speech or action, and gives examples from Adam and Muhammad. This lesson unpacks his argument and shows why the chain of prophets is internally consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first restates the skeptic's objection before providing his counter-argument.
The objection is that the miracle's conjunction with a claim does not entail truth.
2
Identify the key analogy
Look for the analogy between proving prophethood through miracles and proving God's divinity through signs. This is the core of the argument.
The author says: 'The way of God in making known the truthfulness of messengers through miracles is like the way of His making known His divinity through signs.'
3
Examine the modes of definition
The author distinguishes definition by speech (e.g., God informing the angels) and definition by action (e.g., Adam's knowledge of names and Muhammad's Quran). See how each functions as a proof.
Definition by action involves disabling the creation from opposing, as with Adam and the Quran.
4
Understand the chain of prophets
Once the first prophet (Adam) and the last prophet (Muhammad) are validated, all prophets in between are confirmed, forming a continuous chain of truth.
This shows the internal consistency of prophetic messages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجواب عن شبهات المنكرين أن نقول : قولكم: إن اقتران المعجزة بدعوى المدعي لا ينتهض دليلا على صدقه، فإن نفس الاقتران بالإضافة إلى دعواه أو إلى غيرها من الأفعال أو الأقوال بمثابة واحدة. فنقول : سبيل تعريف الله تعالى عباده صدق الرسل بالآيات الخارقة للعادة كسبيل تعريفه إياهم إلهيته بالآيات الدالة عليها، والتعريف قد يكون بالقول تارة، وقد يكون بالفعل أخرى والتعريف بالقول قد يكون إخبارًا عن صدقه ; كقوله تعالى للملائكة : ( إِنِّي جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةً ) وقد يكون تنبيها على صدقه بتعجيز الخلق عن معارضته كما علم آدم الأسماء كلها ثم عرضهم على الملائكة، وكما علم المصطفى القرآن وقال : ( فَأْتُواْ بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ ) فكما عجزت الملائكة عن معارضة آدم بالأسماء عجزت العرب والعجم عن معارضة المصطفى بآيات القرآن ودلت الأسماء والآيات على صدق النبي الأول والنبي الآخر، ولما ثبت صدق الأول كان مبشرًا بمن بعده إلى الآخر، ولما ثبت صدق الآخر كان هو مصدقا لمن قبله إلى الأول، فكانت الأقوال متواصلة، والعلوم متواترة، والكلمات متفقة والدعوات متحدة، والكتب والصحف متصادقة والسيوف على رقاب المنكرين قائمة.
Then the answer to the objections of the deniers is that we say: Your statement that the conjunction of the miracle with the claim of the claimant does not constitute a proof of his truthfulness—indeed, the conjunction itself, in relation to his claim or to other actions or sayings, is of the same status. So we say: The way of God—exalted is He—in making His servants know the truthfulness of the messengers through the customary-breaking signs is like the way of His making them know His divinity through the signs indicating it. And definition may be by speech sometimes, and by action at other times. And definition by speech may be an informing of his truthfulness, like His saying—exalted is He—to the angels: 'Indeed, I will make upon the earth a vicegerent'; and it may be a notification of his truthfulness by disabling the creation from opposing him, as He taught Adam all the names then presented them to the angels, and as He taught the Chosen One (Muhammad) the Quran and said: 'Then bring a surah like it.' So just as the angels were unable to oppose Adam regarding the names, the Arabs and non-Arabs were unable to oppose the Chosen One regarding the verses of the Quran. And the names and the verses indicated the truthfulness of the first prophet and the last prophet. And when the truthfulness of the first was established, he was a bringer of good tidings of those after him up to the last; and when the truthfulness of the last was established, he was a confirmer of those before him down to the first. Thus the statements became continuous, the knowledges transmitted successively, the words agreed, the calls unified, the books and scrolls mutually confirming, and the swords drawn upon the necks of the deniers.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a skeptic's objection that a miracle's conjunction with a prophetic claim does not prove the prophet's truthfulness. The author begins by restating the objection: the mere conjunction of a miracle with a claim does not entail truth, as the miracle could accompany a false claim just as it could accompany a true one. The author then presents the counter-argument: God's method of proving the truthfulness of prophets through miraculous signs is analogous to His method of proving His own divinity through signs. Definition (ta'rīf) can be by speech or by action. Speech includes direct informing, as when God told the angels that He would place a vicegerent on earth, and includes notification through disabling opposition, as when God taught Adam all the names and then presented them to the angels, and as when God taught Muhammad the Quran and challenged the Arabs to produce a similar surah. The inability of the angels to match Adam's knowledge of the names and the inability of the Arabs to match the Quran proved the truthfulness of both prophets. Once the truthfulness of the first prophet (Adam) was established, he could foretell later prophets up to the last (Muhammad); and once the truthfulness of the last was established, he confirmed all previous prophets. This creates a chain of continuous statements, successive knowledge, and unified calls, with the swords of the deniers at the necks of those who reject.

Key Terms
معجزة
اقتران
صدق
نبي
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that miracles alone prove prophethood without considering the claim, but the author argues that the conjunction of miracle and claim is crucial.
Correction: The author shows that the miracle's conjunction with the prophetic claim, not the miracle alone, functions as a proof, analogous to how signs of divinity are conjoined with the claim of God's existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the analogy used to defend the proof-value of miracles?
A) Miracles are like the Quran itself.
B) God's proof of prophethood through miracles is like His proof of His own divinity through signs.
C) Miracles are like the tongues of men.
D) Miracles are like the actions of the angels.

Key Takeaway
Miracles, when conjoined with a prophetic claim, serve as divine proof of truthfulness, analogous to the signs of God's existence, and the chain of prophets is validated through the first and last messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the skeptic's objection that miracles don't prove truth, do you feel any doubt in your heart? What does the author's analogy between divine signs and prophetic miracles reveal about the consistency of God's method? Are you willing to trust that method fully?
?
The author says that once Adam's truthfulness was established, he foretold later prophets, and once Muhammad's truthfulness is established, he confirms all previous prophets. Do you see your own faith as part of this unbroken chain? What does this continuity demand from you today in how you receive the message of all the prophets?
?
If you sincerely believe that the Quran is a miracle that proves Muhammad's prophethood, how should that change your relationship with every command and prohibition in the Quran? Are you living as if you are fully convinced, or do you hold back? Make a firm resolve to act on one teaching from the Quran this week.

The Prophet's Person as a Living Miracle
Sanusi argues that the prophet's entire being is a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prophet's entire being—his words, morals, and states—becomes a divine miracle that establishes his authority.

Core Idea
The prophet himself, in his total being, is a miracle beyond human imitation.

This lesson presents the first of two proofs for prophethood: the extraordinary nature of the prophet's person. Sanusi argues that God adorns His chosen messenger with such perfection that no human can oppose him, making his every movement a sign of divine selection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the beautiful metaphor of the 'garment of beauty' that God clothes the prophet with.
2
Understand the garment analogy
The prophet is clothed with perfection in words, morals, and states. This is not merely a metaphor but a real divine gift that makes his entire being a miracle.
The prophet's movements become as wondrous relative to people as people's movements are to animals.
3
Recognize the hierarchical miracle
Just as humans have mastery over animals through their actions, the prophet has mastery over humans through his miraculous state. This establishes his leadership and the authority of his message.
The Quranic verse is quoted to show that prophets are selected to leave no excuse for humanity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اصطفاه الله عز وجل لرسالته من عباده واجتباه لدعوته كساه ثوب جمال في ألفاظه وأخلاقه وأحواله ما يعجز الخلائق عن معارضته بشيء من ذلك ، فيصير جميع حركاته معجزة للناس كما صارت حركات الناس معجزة لمن دونهم من الحيوانات، ويكون مستتبعا جميع نوع الإنسان كما صار الإنسان مستسخرًا جميع أنواع الحيوان ( والله يصطفي من الملائكة رسلا ) ومن الناس رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين لئلا يكون للناس على الله ) حجة بعد الرسل، ونعيد ما أبديناه آنفًا. فنقول : أنتم معاشر الصابئة والبراهمة المعولون على مجرد العقول وقضاياها وافقتمونا ولم يحتج بالموافقة، بل قام الدليل الواضح على أن الله تعالى تصرفًا في عباده بالتكليف والأمر ، وعلى حركاتهم بالإحكام والحدود، ومن القضايا العقلية أن نوع الإنسان يحتاج إلى اجتماع على نظام وصلاح وأن ذلك الاجتماع لن يتحقق إلا بتعاون وتمانع، وأن ذلك التعاون والتمانع لن يتصور إلا بحدود وأحكام، وأن تلك الحدود والأحكام تجب أن تكون موافقة لحدود الله وأحكامه، وأن كل من دب ودرج ليس يتلقى من الله حدوده وأحكامه ، ولا له أن يضع من عند نفسه حدودًا وأحكامًا، فلزم العقل ضرورة أن يكون بين الناس شرع يفرضه شارع يتلقى
And whomever God—Honored and Exalted—chooses for His message from His servants and selects for His call, He clothes him with a garment of beauty in his words, morals, and states such that the creation are unable to oppose him in any of that. So all his movements become a miracle for people, just as the movements of people become a miracle for the animals below them. And he becomes the leader of the entire human species, just as the human has become the master of all animal species. (And God selects from the angels messengers) and from people messengers, bringers of good news and warners, so that mankind may have no argument against God after the messengers. And we repeat what we have shown earlier. So we say: O assembly of Sabians and Brahmins who rely solely on intellects and their premises, you have agreed with us—and agreement is not needed—rather the clear proof has been established that God—exalted—has disposal over His servants through obligation and command, and over their movements through rulings and limits. And from the rational premises is that the human species needs to gather upon a system and rectification, and that gathering will not be achieved except through cooperation and mutual restraint, and that cooperation and mutual restraint cannot be conceived except through limits and rulings, and that those limits and rulings must be in conformity with God's limits and rulings, and that no one who moves and crawls receives from God His limits and rulings, nor may he set forth from himself limits and rulings. So reason necessarily requires that there be among people a law imposed by a lawgiver who receives
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct proofs for prophethood. First, the author argues that God adorns His chosen prophet with such perfection in speech, character, and states that people are unable to match him—thus his entire being becomes a miracle. Just as humans are superior to animals by their actions, the prophet is superior to humans. This establishes his wonder-working authority. Then the author turns to address the Sabians and Brahmins who rely solely on reason. He argues that rational premises themselves demonstrate the necessity of divine revelation: humans need social organization, which requires cooperation and mutual restraint, which requires laws. These laws must conform to God's laws, but people cannot receive them directly from God nor can they invent them on their own. Therefore, reason demands a lawgiver (a prophet) who receives divine law. The argument concludes that a lawgiver is necessary, though the text cuts off before finishing the sentence.

A) The movements of angels.
B) The movements of people compared to animals.
C) The movements of the sun.
D) The movements of the prophets before him.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's entire being—his words, ethics, and states—is a divine miracle that authenticates his message and establishes his authority over humanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The prophet was given a 'garment of beauty' that made him unparalleled. When you look at your own character, are you satisfied with being 'ordinary', or do you feel the weight of following a leader who set a standard no human can rival? What specific traits of your personality do you need to surrender to his example today?
?
The passage says the prophet leads all humans just as humans lead animals. If you truly believed that following him is the only way to be on the right path, what comfort zone are you clinging to that prevents you from submitting fully to his guidance? What will you sacrifice this week to align your life with his?

Rational Necessity of a Divine Lawgiver
Sanusi argues from rational premises that a lawgiver is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that human society requires a lawgiver who receives divine revelation, contradicting those who rely solely on reason.

Core Idea
Reason itself proves the need for a prophet: without divine law, society cannot achieve order.

This lesson presents the second proof, addressed to the Sabians and Brahmins who trust only intellect. Sanusi builds a chain of rational premises leading to the conclusion that a lawgiver (prophet) is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that this is a series of logical 'if...then' statements that culminate in a necessary conclusion.
2
Identify the premises
The argument begins with the premise that humans need social organization, which requires cooperation and mutual restraint, which in turn requires laws. Trace each step.
These premises are presented as 'from the rational premises' (من القضايا العقلية).
3
Notice the key point: laws must be from God
The laws must conform to God's laws, and no human can receive them directly nor invent them. Therefore, a divine lawgiver is necessary.
The text cuts off but the conclusion is clear: a lawgiver who receives (divine law) is required.
