The Universality of Revelation and the Prophet's Miraculous Nature
Author asserts universal agreement of revelations and prophet's miraculous nature as proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all divine revelations are in agreement and how the prophet's entire being serves as a miracle that establishes his truthfulness.

Core Idea
The universality of prophecy is proven by the harmony of all revelations and the inimitability of the prophet.

This passage from a classical kalam text presents two intertwined arguments for the truth of prophethood. First, it points to the unanimous agreement of all revealed sciences, words, calls, and scriptures as a sign of their divine origin. Second, it describes the prophet as uniquely clothed with a beauty that no one can imitate, making his every movement a miracle analogous to humans' superiority over animals. Read on to see how these two points reinforce each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the rhythm and claims sink in.
2
Identify the universal consensus
Notice the series of affirmations: sciences are mutawatir, words agree, calls are united, books confirm each other. Swords on deniers is a metaphor for decisive proof.
This part establishes the harmony across all revelations.
3
Understand the prophetic garment
Focus on Allah clothing the chosen prophet with beauty in words, ethics, and states. This makes the prophet's every movement a miracle that people cannot oppose.
The analogy with animals highlights the prophet's elevated rank.
4
Connect the arguments
Ask yourself: How do the universal agreement of revelations and the prophet's inimitability together prove the truth of prophethood? The commentary will help.
These two points form a cumulative case for divine origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلوم متواترة، والكلمات متفقة والدعوات متحدة، والكتب والصحف متصادقة والسيوف على رقاب المنكرين قائمة. ومن اصطفاه الله عز وجل لرسالته من عباده واجتباه لدعوته كساه ثوب جمال في ألفاظه وأخلاقه وأحواله ما يعجز الخلائق عن معارضته بشيء من ذلك، فيصير جميع حركاته معجزة للناس كما صارت حركات الناس معجزة لمن دونهم من الحيوانات، ويكون مستتبعا جميع نوع الإنسان كما صار الإنسان مستسخرا جميع أنواع الحيوان، والله يصطفي من الملائكة رسلا ومن الناس رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل، ونعيد ما أبديناه آنفًا.
The sciences are transmitted successively, the words are in agreement, the calls are united, the books and scriptures are mutually truthful, and the swords are raised upon the necks of the deniers. And whoever Allah, Mighty and Majestic, chooses from His servants for His message and selects for His call, He clothes him with a garment of beauty in his words, ethics, and states, such that the creatures are incapable of opposing him in any of that. So all his movements become a miracle for people, just as the movements of people become a miracle for those below them from animals. And he becomes followed by all of the human species, just as man became making subservient all kinds of animals. And Allah selects from angels messengers and from people messengers as bearers of good tidings and warners, so that people should have no argument against Allah after the messengers. And we will repeat what we have previously stated.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section that establishes the universal character of prophethood and the miraculous nature of the prophet's entire persona. The author begins by stating the consensus of all sciences, words, calls, and scriptures—they agree and confirm each other, while deniers are met with 'swords' (a metaphor for decisive proof or punishment). Then the author describes how Allah distinguishes a prophet by granting him a 'garment of beauty' in his speech, character, and circumstances, making the entire creation unable to match him in any way. Thus, every movement of the prophet becomes a miracle for humans, just as human movements appear miraculous to animals. The prophet consequently commands the obedience of all humans, as humans have power over animals. The author quotes the Quranic verse (Al-Hajj 75) that Allah selects messengers from angels and from men to give glad tidings and warn, so that people have no excuse before Allah after the sending of messengers. The author then signals that he will restate his argument in what follows.

Key Terms
متواترة
معجزة
اصطفى
رسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'swords on the necks of deniers' is understood as advocating actual physical violence.
Correction: In the context of kalam argumentation, 'swords' is a metaphor for decisive rational or scriptural evidence that silences opponents, not for literal execution.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes all the prophet's movements a miracle?
A) Because he is a human like others.
B) Because Allah clothes him with a beauty that others cannot oppose.
C) Because people are forced to follow him.
D) Because he uses supernatural powers.

Key Takeaway
The truth of prophethood is established both by the universal agreement of all revelations and by the prophet's unique inimitability in every aspect of his being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the prophet's words, ethics, and states are so perfect that no one can oppose them. In what area of your life have you secretly thought you know better than the prophetic way? Why do you still resist being 'clothed' with that beauty?
?
The passage states that all scriptures are mutually truthful. If you are a Muslim, how does this affect your view of previous scriptures? Do you treat them with reverence or dismiss them? What would change if you took this seriously today?
?
The prophet's movements are miracles, just as our movements are miracles to animals. Do you live with the awareness that your every action can be a sign pointing to Allah? Pick one habit you do unconsciously and try to make it a conscious act of worship tomorrow.

Rational Necessity of a Prophet
Rational proof for the necessity of a prophet against Sabians and Brahmins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity of a divinely revealed law as argued against those who rely solely on reason.

Core Idea
Reason itself demands that law must come from God through a prophet.

This passage presents a rational proof aimed at the Sabians and Brahmins who claim to follow only intellect. The author builds a chain of unavoidable rational steps, showing that human society requires laws, and those laws must originate from Allah. The conclusion: a divinely appointed lawgiver (prophet) is rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let the argument's flow sink in.
2
Follow the logical chain
Identify each rational proposition that the author lists. Notice how each step builds on the previous one.
The chain starts with Allah's disposal and ends with the need for a lawgiver.
3
Understand the necessity
Ask yourself why each step is necessary. For example, why can't humans invent their own laws? The passage gives a clear reason.
This connects to the misconception that reason alone suffices.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The final conclusion is that a lawgiver (prophet) receiving revelation is rationally required. That is the key takeaway.
The author notes that the real issue is identifying the true lawgiver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: أنتم معاشر الصابئة والبراهمة المعولون على مجرد العقول وقضاياها وافقتمونا ولم يحتج بالموافقة، بل قام الدليل الواضح على أن الله تعالى تصرفًا في عباده بالتكليف والأمر، وعلى حركاتهم بالإحكام والحدود، ومن القضايا العقلية أن نوع الإنسان يحتاج إلى اجتماع على نظام وصلاح وأن ذلك الاجتماع لن يتحقق إلا بتعاون وتمانع، وأن ذلك التعاون والتمانع لن يتصور إلا بحدود وأحكام، وأن تلك الحدود والأحكام تجب أن تكون موافقة لحدود الله وأحكامه، وأن كل من دب ودرج ليس يتلقى من الله حدوده وأحكامه، ولا له أن يضع من عند نفسه حدودًا وأحكامًا، فلزم العقل ضرورة أن يكون بين الناس شرع يفرضه شارع يتلقى من الله وحيا أو ينزله تنزيلا على عباده، وهذه قضايا عقلية بيننا وبينكم، وإنما حقيقة القول إيل إلى تعيين الشارع.
We say: O community of the Sabians and Brahmins who rely on mere intellects and its propositions, you have agreed with us, but agreement is not sufficient. Rather the clear proof has been established that Allah, exalted, has disposal over His servants through obligation and command, and over their movements through judgments and limits. And among the rational propositions is that the human species needs to come together upon a system and rectification, and that such coming together will not be realized except through mutual cooperation and mutual prevention, and that such mutual cooperation and prevention will not be conceived except through limits and judgments, and that those limits and judgments must be in conformity with the limits and judgments of Allah, and that every creature that moves and walks does not receive from Allah His limits and judgments, nor does he have the authority to lay down from himself limits and judgments. So reason necessarily requires that there be among people a law imposed by a lawgiver who receives from Allah a revelation or sends it down as a revelation to His servants. And these are rational propositions between us and you. The reality of the statement ultimately reduces to the identification of the lawgiver.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument for the necessity of revelation, addressed specifically to the Sabians and Brahmins who claim to rely solely on reason. The author first notes that they agree on certain principles, but agreement is not proof; rather, clear demonstration exists. He then lists a chain of rational propositions: (1) Allah has control over people through obligation and command, and over their actions through rulings and limits. (2) Humans need social organization for order and goodness. (3) Such organization requires cooperation and competition (balance). (4) Cooperation and competition need laws and boundaries. (5) These laws must conform to Allah's laws. (6) No ordinary human can derive Allah's laws directly or invent them on his own. Therefore, reason itself necessitates a lawgiver who receives divine revelation and delivers it to people. The author concludes that these are shared rational premises, and the real issue is only identifying who the true lawgiver (prophet) is.

Key Terms
صابئة
براهمة
تكليف
تعاون
تمانع
حدود
أحكام
شارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that reason alone can determine the specific content of divine law.
Correction: The argument only proves that law must ultimately come from God through a prophet; it does not claim that reason can derive the specific rulings.

Checkpoint
What is the final rational conclusion the author draws for the Sabians and Brahmins?
A) Reason alone can govern human society.
B) A lawgiver must receive revelation from Allah.
C) Humans can invent their own laws.
D) Cooperation alone suffices without laws.

Key Takeaway
Reason itself compels the necessity of a divinely revealed law through a prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
How does your life reflect the reality that you cannot invent your own limits and judgments? Are there areas where you are living as though you have the authority to set your own rules apart from what Allah has revealed?
?
The proof shows that society needs cooperation and limits. In what ways are you contributing to the rectification of society by following the prophetic law, or are you part of the chaos by neglecting it?

The Necessity of a Clear Sign
Proof that God must provide a clear sign to verify the true prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical necessity for God to provide an unmistakable sign to verify the true prophet, and why failing to do so would lead to imposing an impossible burden.

Core Idea
Divine justice requires a clear sign for prophethood.

Why must God provide miracles to confirm prophets? This passage from al-Ash'ari or a later theologian argues that it is not a matter of external obligation but a logical necessity stemming from God's wisdom. Without a clear sign, people would be burdened with an impossible task, and could justly object to God on the Day of Judgment. The author distinguishes between speech and action as means of belief, showing that for humans, only action (a miracle) can serve as proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm of the argument. Then read the translation alongside it.
Notice the opening phrase 'لن يتحتم' — it sets up a distinction between obligation and necessity.
2
Distinguish between 'not incumbent' and 'necessity'
The author clarifies that providing a sign is not an obligation upon God (not incumbent), but a necessity (wujūb) for God's own consistency and justice. Why would failing to do so lead to 'taʿjīz' (inability) and 'taklif ma la yutaq' (burdening beyond capacity)?
3
Examine the speech/action dichotomy
The passage says belief (taṣdīq) is reached only through speech (qawl) or action (fiʿl). For humans, action is the only feasible way since we cannot hear divine speech directly. Consider why angels are different.
The author also mentions a hybrid: action that functions like speech (e.g., a miracle that 'speaks' truth) or speech that functions like action (e.g., the Qur'an as a living miracle).
4
Identify the three consequences of not providing a clear sign
The author lists three negative outcomes: (1) leads to incapacity in presenting proofs, (2) leads to denial concerning the news of succession, (3) allows people to use the Quranic argument in Surah Taha (20:134) against God. Find these in the text.
The third consequence is explicitly linked to the verse: 'Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger...'
5
Reflect on the Quranic proof
The passage ends with a hypothetical objection from humanity. Think about how this reinforces the necessity of a clear sign: God Himself acknowledges that people would have a valid argument if no messenger or sign was sent.
This connects the theological argument to scriptural authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: لن يتحتم على الله تعالى تعريف صدق من اصطفاه لرسالته وتعيين شخص من اجتباه لشريعته، فإنه يؤدي إلى التعجيز وإلى تكليف ما لا يطاق، وهو كما لو كلف عباده بمعرفته ثم يطمس عليهم وجوه أدلته، وإذ لا طريق إلى التصديق إلا بالقول والفعل ولا طريق إلى ذلك بالقول تعين الفعل، ولربما يعرف الملائكة بالقول، ويعرف الناس بالفعل، ولربما يعرف النوعين بهما جميعا؛ أعني تعريفهما بفعل نازل منزلة القول، أو بقول دال دلالة الفعل، وليس ذلك التعريف إيجابًا عليه تعالى وتقدس، بل وجوبًا له حتى لا يؤدي إلى التعجيز في نصب الأدلة، وحتى لا يفضي إلى التكذيب في خبر الاستخلاف، وحتى لا يكون على الله حجة بعد الرسل بتكليف ما لا يطاق، فيقال: رَبِّنَا لَوْلَا أَرْسَلْتَ إِلَيْنَا رَسُولاً لطه: ١٣٤، وإذا أرسلت فهلا بنيت لنا طريقا ودليلا يتوصل به إلى صدقه، ويتوصل بتصديقه إلى معرفتك وطاعتك.
We say: It is not incumbent upon Allah, exalted, to make known the truth of the one He has chosen for His message and to designate the person He has selected for His law, for that would lead to incapacity and to imposing what is beyond capacity. It is like if He charged His servants with knowing Him, then He obscured from them the faces of His proofs. And since there is no way to belief except through speech and action, and there is no way to that through speech, action is determined. Perhaps angels are known through speech, and people are known through action, and perhaps both types are known through both of them together; I mean, defining them by an action that takes the place of speech, or by a speech that indicates with the indication of an action. And that definition is not an obligation upon Him, exalted and sanctified, but rather a necessity for Him, so that it does not lead to incapacity in erecting the proofs, and so that it does not lead to denial concerning the news of succession, and so that there should be no argument against Allah after the messengers by imposing what is beyond capacity. Then it would be said: 'Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger?' (Taha 134) 'And when you sent, why did you not build for us a way and a proof through which we might attain to his truthfulness, and through believing him attain to knowledge of You and obedience to You?'
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must provide a clear and unmistakable sign to verify the prophet's truthfulness. The author begins by stating that it is not something that Allah is compelled to do by an external obligation, but rather it is a logical necessity (wujūb) stemming from His wisdom. If God were not to provide such a sign, it would lead to 'inability' (taʿjīz) and the imposition of an impossible burden, like asking people to know Him while obscuring all evidence. The author explains that there are two ways to belief (taṣdīq): speech (qawl) and action (fiʿl). For humans, action is the only possible way because we cannot perceive direct speech from God. Angels might be addressed directly by speech, but humans need visible actions (miracles). Sometimes, a combination of both is used: an action that functions like speech, or speech that functions like an action (e.g., the Qurʾan). The author then lists three consequences of not providing a clear sign: (1) it would lead to incapacity in presenting proofs; (2) it would lead to denial of the news of succession (perhaps referring to the appointment of a successor after the messenger); (3) it would allow people to argue against Allah after the sending of messengers, using the excuse from the Qurʾan (Surah Taha: 134) that they would say, 'Why did you not send a messenger? And if you did, why did you not provide a clear way and proof to his truthfulness?' Thus, the provision of a clear sign is a necessary implication of divine justice and wisdom.

Key Terms
يتحتم
تعجيز
تكليف ما لا يطاق
تصديق
قول
فعل
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God is obligated by an external force to provide miracles.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is not an external obligation (ijāb) but a necessity (wujūb) inherent to God's wisdom and justice, to avoid the charge of imposing an impossible burden.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that God must provide a clear sign to identify the prophet?
A) Because God is forced by an external obligation.
B) To avoid the charge of burdening people with the impossible.
C) Because the angels need an action.
D) To limit the number of prophets.

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom and justice necessitate a clear sign (miracle) to verify a prophet, otherwise humans would be burdened with an impossible task and would have a valid objection against God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage argues that without a clear sign, people would have a right to object on the Day of Judgment. If you stand before Allah today, will you be able to say that you truly followed the messenger He sent? Or are you acting as if you never received a clear sign?
?
When you read the verse 'Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger?' do you feel that this excuse is now closed for you? How will you change your life to reflect that you have indeed received a clear sign?

The Necessity of a Defining Miracle: Avoiding Absurdity
Proof that God must define the prophet's truthfulness to avoid logical absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must provide a miracle to authenticate a prophet to avoid logical absurdities like incapacitation and impossible obligation.

Core Idea
Without a miracle, God would be commanding the impossible.

This passage from al-Aqdam tackles a fundamental question: why does God provide miracles for prophets? The author argues that without a defining sign, people would be left without a way to know the truth, leading to a logical contradiction in divine justice. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical flow from the initial statement to the conclusion.
The argument is tight; pay attention to the analogy.
2
Identify the consequence of absence
What does the author say would happen if God did not define the truthfulness of the messenger? He mentions two key terms: ta'jiz and taklif ma la yutaq.
3
Understand the analogy
The author gives an analogy: commanding knowledge while obscuring proofs. Why is this analogous? Reflect on the injustice it implies.
This is a key point for the argument.
4
Analyze the means of belief
The author states that belief comes through word (qawl) or deed (fi'l). Why is word insufficient for humans, and why does deed become necessary?
Think about the nature of claims and verification.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
Consider why this argument is foundational in Islamic theology. How does it establish the necessity of miracles?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : لن يتحتم على الله تعالى تعريف صدق من اصطفاه لرسالته وتعيين شخص من اجتباه لشريعته، فإنه يؤدي إلى التعجيز وإلى تكليف ما لا يطاق، وهو كما لو كلف عباده بمعرفته ثم يطمس عليهم وجوه أدلته، وإذ لا طريق إلى التصديق إلا بالقول والفعل ولا طريق إلى ذلك بالقول تعين الفعل، ولربما يعرف الملائكة بالقول، ويعرف الناس بالفعل، ولربما يعرف النوعين بهما جميعا؛ أعني تعريفهما بفعل نازل منزلة القول، أو بقول دال دلالة الفعل، وليس ذلك التعريف إيجابًا عليه تعالى وتقدس، بل وجوبًا له حتى لا يؤدي إلى التعجيز في نصب الأدلة، وحتى لا يفضي إلى التكذيب في خبر الاستخلاف، وحتى لا يكون على الله حجة بعد الرسل بتكليف ما لا يطاق، فيقال : ( رَبِّنَا لَوْلَا أَرْسَلْتَ إِلَيْنَا رَسُولاً لطه : ١٣٤ ، وإذا أرسلت فهلا بنيت لنا طريقا ودليلا يتوصل به إلى صدقه، ويتوصل بتصديقه إلى معرفتك وطاعتك.
We say: It is not incumbent upon God to define the truthfulness of one whom He has chosen for His message and to appoint a person whom He has selected for His law, for that would lead to incapacitation and to obligating what cannot be borne. It is similar to if He obligated His servants to know Him then obscured from them the faces of His proofs. And since there is no way to belief except through word and deed, and no way to that through word, the deed becomes determined. Perhaps the angels are known through word, and people are known through deed, and perhaps both types are known through both together; I mean their definition through a deed that takes the place of word, or through a word that indicates the indication of a deed. That definition is not an obligation upon Him—exalted and sanctified is He—but rather a necessity for Him, so that it does not lead to incapacitation in the erection of proofs, and so that it does not lead to denial in the report of succession, and so that there is no argument against God after the messengers by obligating what cannot be borne, so that it would be said: 'Our Lord, if only You had sent us a messenger' (Ta-Ha: 134), and 'When You sent, why did You not build for us a way and a proof by which we could arrive at his truthfulness and by believing him arrive at knowing You and obeying You?'
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God providing a defining sign (mu'jiza) to authenticate a prophet. The author begins by stating the opponent's implied position: that God is not obligated to define the truthfulness of the messenger. He then argues that if God did not do so, it would lead to 'ta'jiz' (rendering people incapable) and 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligating the impossible). This is analogous to commanding people to know Him while obscuring all proofs—a manifest injustice. The author then breaks down the means of belief: word (qawl) or deed (fi'l). For humans, the way is through deed, i.e., miracles, since words alone are insufficient without corroboration. Angels may be known through word (direct revelation), but people require visible acts. The definition itself is not something God is 'obligated' to do as a duty imposed upon Him, but rather a logical necessity (wujub lahu) to avoid the absurd consequences: failure to provide proofs, denial of the prophecy's claim, and giving people a valid excuse on the Day of Judgment. The passage ends by quoting the hypothetical complaint of people if God did not send a messenger with clear signs.

Key Terms
تعريف الصدق
تعجيز
تكليف ما لا يطاق
قول
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God is legally obligated to provide miracles, as a duty upon Him.
Correction: The author is not arguing that God has a legal obligation, but rather that the absence of a sign leads to logical absurdity, making miracle provision a rational necessity.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would result if God did not define the truthfulness of the prophet?
A) People would still believe.
B) It would lead to incapacitation and impossible obligation.
C) The angels would reveal the truth.
D) It would be a test of faith.

Key Takeaway
God must provide a miracle for a prophet because without it, people would be unable to distinguish truth from falsehood, leading to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard so many claims of spiritual authority in your life—from preachers, scholars, or even your own heart. But this passage tells you that without a clear sign from Allah, you are not obligated to believe. Where in your life have you given unquestioning trust to someone’s religious speech without demanding the evidence that a true messenger brings? Will you now withhold allegiance until you see the deed?
?
Allah has blessed you with the greatest miracle—the Qur’an—and the clear proofs of prophethood. Yet how often do you treat His commands as if they are optional or impossible? The passage says God does not obligate the unbearable. So when you claim you cannot pray, cannot lower your gaze, cannot give charity, are you really being honest, or have you simply hidden from the proofs He has placed before you? What is one command you have been avoiding, and what practical step will you take today to obey it?
?
The analogy in this passage is piercing: God commanding you to know Him but then hiding the proofs. Have you ever felt that Allah is distant or that His signs are vague? But the passage insists that He has not hidden them. The proof is clear in the miracle of the revelation and the life of the Prophet. So why do you still hesitate? Is it because you prefer the comfort of doubt over the accountability of certainty? Choose one sign of prophethood—the Qur’an’s challenge, the prophecies, or the miracles—and commit to studying it tonight until your doubt turns to submission.

The Nature of Divine Definition: Logical Necessity, Not Obligation
Clarification that the definition is a logical necessity and explanation of different modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between a logical necessity for God and a legal obligation, and understand the different ways prophets are authenticated.

Core Idea
God's definition of a prophet is a logical necessity, not a duty imposed on Him.

Having established that a defining sign is necessary, the author now clarifies its nature. He explains that the definition is not an obligation upon God but rather a necessity that flows from His wisdom. He also notes that different creation may be defined differently: angels through word, humans through deed. Let's delve into these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the shift from the previous argument to a clarification.
Pay attention to the terms 'ijab' and 'wujub'.
2
Identify the different modes of definition
The author mentions that angels may be known through word, and people through deed. Why this difference?
3
Understand the key distinction
The author explicitly says the definition is not an obligation (ijab) upon God, but a necessity (wujub) for Him. What is the difference?
This is crucial for avoiding a false understanding of divine compulsion.
4
Learn the consequences of absence
The author lists three consequences of not providing a sign: incapacitation in proofs, denial of the prophecy, and a valid excuse on Judgment Day.
These echo the earlier argument.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
The passage ends with a verse (Ta-Ha 134) that shows the complaint of people if no messenger was sent with signs. How does this support the argument?

The author begins by stating the opponent's implied position: that God is not obligated to define the truthfulness of the messenger. The Prophet's Referral to God's Will as Proof of Truth
Refutation of an objection about prophetic referral of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet's referral of miracles to God's will is actually a strong evidence of his truthfulness, and how this relates to the concept of essential distinction and infallibility.

Core Idea
The prophet's inability to produce miracles independently proves he is a true messenger.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection: opponents claim that when a prophet says the miracle occurs only by God's will, it shows weakness. The author turns this on its head, explaining that this very referral is the strongest proof of the prophet's sincerity. By attributing all power to God and showing his own incapacity, the prophet demonstrates that he is not acting on his own but delivering a divine message. This lesson explores that argument and its connection to the prophet's essential distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author starts with an objection and then gives the response.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the objection and the counter-argument
The objection is that referral shows weakness. The counter-argument is that it shows truthfulness. Find where the author states this directly.
3
Understand the concept of essential distinction
The author introduces 'fasl dhati' (essential distinction) for the prophet. Reflect on what it means for the prophet to never be independent of God.
This is linked to infallibility (isma).
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 53:3-4 to support the argument. See how the verse reinforces the idea that the prophet does not speak from desire.
The ending apologetic phrase is a rhetorical device.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم : إن الرسول يحيل نزول الآية إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، قلنا : تلك الحوالة من أدل الأدلة على صدق المقالة ؛ إذ لو ادعى الاستقلال بإظهار الآيات ما كان مخبرا الله تعالى بأمره ، ولا داعيًا إلى الله بإذنه، فهو في كل حال من الأحوال يثبت حق موكله، ويظهر العجز من نفسه ويحيل الحول والقوة إلى مرسله وكان الفصل الذاتي لنفس النبي عليه السلام أن لا يكون موكولا لنفسه طرفة عين، فلا ينطق عن الهوى، ولا يتحرك إلا على متن الهدى، وذلك هو العصمة الإلهية المقيمة لنفسه المقومة لذاته، ولئن كان النطق فصلا ذاتيا لنوع الإنسان ( وما ينطق عن الهوى إن هو إلا وحي يوحى ) النجم : ٣، ٤] فصل ذاتي لنفس النبي عليه السلام اعرف الإشارة ولا تؤاخذني بالعبارة.
And your saying: 'The prophet refers the coming of the sign to the will of God Almighty.' We say: That referral is among the strongest evidences of the truthfulness of the claim; for if he had claimed independence in displaying the signs, he would not be informing of God's command, nor calling to God by His permission. Rather, in every circumstance he affirms the right of his principal, shows incapacity from himself, and refers power and strength to his sender. And the essential distinction of the person of the prophet—peace be upon him—is that he is not delegated to himself for the twinkling of an eye. He does not speak from whim, nor does he move except upon the path of guidance. And that is the divine infallibility that establishes his person and stabilizes his essence. And if speech is an essential distinction for the human species—'He does not speak from whim; it is only revelation revealed' (An-Najm: 3-4)—it is an essential distinction for the person of the prophet upon him peace. Understand the allusion and do not hold me to the letter.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection: opponents claim that a prophet who says 'the miracle occurs only by God's will' is actually showing weakness. The author turns this around, arguing the opposite: the prophet's referral (ihala) of signs to God's will is actually the strongest proof of his truthfulness. Had he claimed to produce miracles independently, his message would be self-serving, not a true report from God. Instead, the prophet constantly attributes power to God and shows his own incapacity. This is then linked to the essential distinction (fasl dhati) of the prophet: he is never independent for even a moment. He does not speak from desire (hawa) but only from divine guidance. This is 'isma (infallibility). The author cites the Quranic verse about the Prophet Muhammad not speaking from whim, and then says that this is the essential distinction of the prophet. He ends with an apologetic phrase asking the reader to grasp the intended meaning, not quibble over exact wording.

Key Terms
حوالة
استقلال
عجز
فصل ذاتي
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prophet's referral of miracles to God's will is a sign of weakness.
Correction: It is actually a strong evidence of truthfulness because it shows the prophet is not independent but a true messenger relying entirely on God.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the prophet's referral of miracles to God's will?
A) It is a sign of weakness.
B) It is a strong evidence of truthfulness.
C) It is a neutral statement.
D) It disproves prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's referral of miracles to God's will is not a sign of weakness but the strongest evidence of his truthfulness, as it shows his complete reliance on God and his role as a mere messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act, even for a moment, as if you are independent of God? How does the prophet's essential distinction challenge your daily reliance on Him?
?
When you succeed in something, do you attribute it to your own abilities or to God? The prophet showed incapacity—what would it look like for you to do the same?
?
You claim to follow the prophet, but do you still speak from whim? Read the verse 'He does not speak from whim'—is that true of your words today? What will you change?

Refuting the Sabians: Inconsistency in Accepting Some Prophets
Argument against Sabians who accept certain prophets but deny Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the logical inconsistency in the Sabian position regarding prophethood.

Core Idea
The Sabians' selective acceptance of prophets is logically indefensible.

This passage from Shahrastani's al-Milal wa al-Nihal presents a sharp critique of the Sabians, a group that accepted certain ancient prophets but rejected Muhammad. The author demonstrates that their criteria for accepting prophets are arbitrary and self-defeating. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's direct questioning style.
2
Identify the Sabian position
The author states that the Sabians accept Seth (Adhimun) and Idris (Hermes) as prophets. Identify this and the subsequent challenge.
3
Trace the logical progression
Follow the author's questions: How did they verify these prophets? If by proof, it applies to others. If they object to human messengers, these were human too. If they claim sages, why follow their laws?
This reveals the inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the Sabians have no consistent criterion, making their position irrational. Consider how this argument defends the prophethood of Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : أنتم معاشر الصابئة سلمتم بنبوة عاذيمون وهرمس وهما شيث وإدريس عليهما السلام، فبم عرفتم صدقهما، أبمجرد الدعوى والخبر أم بدليل ونظر؟ وكل ما قدرتموه دليلا في حق شخص واحد ثبتت به نبوته، أو على الجملة صدقه في جميع أقواله فهو دليل المخبر الثاني والثالث، وإن أحلتم الرسالة في الصورة البشرية فهما بشر مثلكم وأنتم مخبرون عنهما بشر مثلنا. فإن قلتم : إنهما كانا حكيمين عالمين لا نبيين مرسلين، قيل : وبم وجب عليكم اتباعهما والمحافظة على حدودهما وأحكامهما وانتهاج مناهجهما في الدعوات والصلوات والصيام والزكوات، وقد تساوت أقدامكم في العقول والأنفس، وتشابهت صوركم في البشرية والإنسانية؟
And we say: You, company of Sabians, have accepted the prophethood of Adhimun and Hermes, who are Seth and Idris—peace be upon them. How then did you recognize their truthfulness? Was it by mere claim and report, or by proof and consideration? And everything that you consider a proof for a single person through which his prophethood is established, or in general his truthfulness in all his statements, is the proof for the second and third informant. And if you deem impossible the message in human form, they are humans like you, and you are informants about them, humans like us. If you say: 'They were wise and knowledgeable, not prophets and messengers,' it is said: And why is it obligatory upon you to follow them, preserve their boundaries and judgments, and pursue their methods in supplications, prayers, fasting, and alms? And your feet are equal in intellects and souls, and your forms resemble each other in humanity and human-ness?
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sabians (a group who accepted some prophets from the past but rejected the Prophet Muhammad). The author points out their inconsistency: they accept the prophethood of Adhimun (Seth) and Hermes (Idris). The author then asks how they determined the truthfulness of these figures—was it by mere claim or by proof? If by proof, then the same method would apply to later prophets. Furthermore, if they object to prophethood because of human form, those earlier prophets were human too. If they claim those figures were merely wise men, not prophets, then why do they follow their laws and rituals? The argument is that the Sabians have no consistent criterion, and thus their position is irrational.

The Paradox of Denying Prophethood
Logical paradox: denial of prophethood implies affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical paradox that denying prophethood implicitly affirms the concept of prophethood.

Core Idea
Denying prophethood is self-refuting: it requires reporting about God, which is the essence of prophethood.

This lesson presents a fascinating logical paradox found in classical kalam. The author argues that those who deny prophethood cannot avoid affirming it in the very act of denial. As you read the passage, consider how the definition of prophethood as a report from God plays a key role in this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the paradox and the definition given.
The text is cut off at the end due to page break.
2
Identify the paradoxical structure
The passage states that denial implies affirmation. Find how the definition of prophethood ('report from God') makes this work.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Consider why this refutes the denier: they must issue a report about God, which is precisely what prophethood is.
This shows the self-refuting nature of the denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عجب ما يلزم منكري النبوة أن من أنكر النبوة فقد أقر بها من حيث أنكرها ، فإن النبوة لا معنى لها إلا الخبر عن الله تعالى بأنه أرسل رسولاً، ومن أنكر
And among the amazing things necessitated against the deniers of prophethood is that whoever denies prophethood has affirmed it in the very act of denying it. For prophethood has no meaning except the report from God Almighty that He sent a messenger. And whoever denies...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents a striking logical paradox: those who deny prophethood actually affirm it implicitly. The author argues that prophethood (nubuwwa) is defined as a report from God that He sent a messenger. By denying prophethood, a person is making a claim about God—either that God did not send a messenger or that such a report is false. But in doing so, they are talking about God and issuing a report about Him, which is itself a form of 'prophethood' (news from God) in a broader sense. The text here is cut off, but the intended implication is that the denial is self-refuting because it uses the very concept it denies. The paradox is meant to show that prophetic reports cannot be rejected without falling into contradiction.

Key Terms
نبوة
خبر
إنكار
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author claims deniers actually believe in prophethood, but the point is logical: the act of denial presupposes the conceptual framework of prophethood.
Correction: The paradox is about logical implication, not psychological belief. The denier uses the concept of a report about God, which is the essence of prophethood, so their denial relies on the very concept they reject.

Checkpoint
What is the paradox the author presents regarding deniers of prophethood?
A) They prove prophethood by their denial.
B) They are unaware of their own inconsistency.
C) They affirm prophethood in the very act of denying it because denial involves a report about God.
D) They are not rational.

Key Takeaway
Denying prophethood is self-refuting because the act of denial itself uses the concept of a report from God, which is the very essence of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If denying prophethood logically affirms it, what does that say about your own moments of doubt? Can you honestly point to a time you wrestled with doubt and resolved it through evidence, or are you still holding onto a comfortable skepticism? What would it take for you to move from denial to submission?

The Sabians' Inconsistent Acceptance of Prophets
Addressing the Sabians who accept some prophets but deny others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the logical inconsistency in the Sabians' selective acceptance of prophets.

Core Idea
If evidence proves one prophet, it proves them all.

The Sabians accepted only Seth and Enoch as prophets, rejecting others. In this passage, the author challenges them: what proof did you use? Whatever it was, it must apply equally to other claimants. Moreover, if you object to prophethood in human form, Seth and Enoch were human too. This forces the Sabians into a logical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the direct address to the Sabians.
2
Identify the Sabians' position
The Sabians accept the prophethood of 'Adhimun (Seth) and Hermes (Enoch) but deny others. Note that the author identifies these figures with known prophets.
3
Analyze the author's challenge
The author asks by what criterion the Sabians recognized the truthfulness of these two prophets. Was it by blind acceptance or by evidence? He argues that whatever evidence they used should apply to any other prophet who comes with similar claims.
This is the core consistency argument.
4
Consider the objection about human form
The Sabians might reject prophethood because it comes in human form. The author counters that the prophets they accept are also human, so that objection is self-defeating.
5
Reflect on the logical dilemma
The Sabians must either accept all prophets who come with similar evidence, or reject their own accepted prophets. This dilemma shows the inconsistency of their selective acceptance.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : أنتم معاشر الصابئة سلمتم بنبوة عاذيمون وهرمس وهما شيث وإدريس عليهما السلام، فبم عرفتم صدقهما، أبمجرد الدعوى والخبر أم بدليل ونظر؟ وكل ما قدرتموه دليلا في حق شخص واحد ثبتت به نبوته، أو على الجملة صدقه في جميع أقواله فهو دليل المخبر الثاني والثالث، وإن أحلتم الرسالة في الصورة البشرية فهما بشر مثلكم وأنتم مخبرون عنهما بشر مثلنا.

So by what did you recognize their truthfulness? Is it by mere claim and report, or by proof and consideration? Commentary
This passage directly addresses the Sabians, who accept the prophethood of two figures identified as Shith (Seth) and Idris (Enoch) but deny other prophets. The author asks by what method they recognized their truthfulness — was it based on mere claims or on evidence? He argues that whatever evidence they used to establish the truthfulness of those first prophets would equally apply to other prophets. Further, if the Sabians reject prophethood in human form, then these prophets were humans like them, so consistency demands they either accept all human prophets or reject these as well.

The Sabian Objection and Prophetic Authority
Author rebuts Sabian claim by highlighting inconsistency in their practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation to follow Shith and Idris implies their prophethood, and how the author refutes the Sabian claim that they were merely wise men.

Core Idea
If they were just wise like you, why must you follow them?

The Sabians argued that Shith and Idris were wise men, not prophets. But if that were true, why would they be bound to follow their teachings? The author exposes this contradiction, showing that the very act of following them requires prophetic authority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sabian objection
The Sabians claim that Shith and Idris were merely wise men, not prophets. What is their reasoning for denying prophethood?
2
Read the author's reply
The author responds with a rhetorical question that exposes a contradiction. Read the passage and identify the key point.
Notice how the author uses the Sabians' own practice against them.
3
Analyze the implication
If following these men is obligatory only if they are prophets, then the Sabians have implicitly accepted their prophethood. Why else would they follow them?
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلتم : إنهما كانا حكيمين عالمين لا نبيين مرسلين، قيل : وبم وجب عليكم اتباعهما والمحافظة على حدودهما وأحكامهما وانتهاج مناهجهما في الدعوات والصلوات والصيام والزكوات، وقد تساوت أقدامكم في العقول والأنفس، وتشابهت صوركم في البشرية والإنسانية؟

This passage continues the debate with the Sabians. They object that Shith and Idris were merely wise men, not prophets. The author responds by pointing out the inconsistency: if they are merely wise men like you, then why do you follow their teachings and practices? There would be no obligation to follow them, as you are equal in rationality. Thus, the only justification for following them is their prophethood, which you have implicitly accepted.

The Paradox of Denying Prophethood
Showing that denying prophethood is self-refuting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying prophethood is logically self-refuting and implies an affirmation of messengerhood.

Core Idea
Denying prophethood is self-refuting because it claims prophetic knowledge.

This passage presents a clever logical paradox: those who deny that God sends messengers are themselves making a claim about God's actions. In doing so, they implicitly assume the role of a messenger, thus contradicting their own denial. Let's see how the author unpacks this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the paradox.
The argument hinges on the definition of prophethood.
2
Identify the logical steps
Notice how the author defines prophethood as 'news from God that He sent a messenger.' Then see how the denier's claim fits that definition.
The denier claims to know that God did not send a messenger, which is itself a form of informing about God.
3
Understand the shift to human messengers
After establishing the paradox, the author moves to the real dispute: can a messenger be human? He cites a Quranic verse and concludes that humanity does not negate messengerhood.
This part addresses a common objection.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument is compelling. The denier cannot avoid self-contradiction without admitting the possibility of prophethood.
The passage ends by affirming the possibility of sending human messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عجب ما يلزم منكري النبوة أن من أنكر النبوة فقد أقر بها من حيث أنكرها، فإن النبوة لا معنى لها إلا الخبر عن الله تعالى بأنه أرسل رسولاً، ومن أنكر النبوة فقد ادعى أنه مخبر عن الله أنه لم يرسل رسولاً فقد ادعى الرسالة لنفسه، فكان إنكاره إقرارًا وعاد إنكاره تسليما، ومن سلم أن الله على عباده تكليفا وأمرا فقد سلم أنه يرسل رسولاً، وإنما النزاع وقع في أن الرسالة هل تتصور في الصورة البشرية ( وَمَا مَنَعَ النَّاسَ أَن يُؤْمِنُوا إِذْ جَاءَهُمُ الْهُدَى إِلَّا أَن قَالُوا أَبَعَثَ اللَّهُ بَشَرًا رَّسُولاً ) [الإسراء : ٩٤] وإذا بينا أن البشرية لا تنافي الرسالة، فقد أثبتنا جواز انبعاث الرسل.
And among the remarkable things that follow for deniers of prophethood is that whoever denies prophethood has affirmed it from the very perspective of his denial. For prophethood has no meaning except the news from God Almighty that He sent a messenger. And whoever denies prophethood has claimed that he is informing about God that He did not send a messenger, so he has claimed messengerhood for himself. Thus his denial becomes an affirmation, and his denial returns to an acceptance. And whoever affirms that God has imposed obligation and command upon His servants has affirmed that He sends a messenger. The dispute only occurred regarding whether messengerhood is conceivable in human form – 'And nothing prevented people from believing when guidance came to them except that they said: Has God sent a human as a messenger?' [Al-Isra' 17:94] – and when we have shown that humanity does not negate messengerhood, we have established the possibility of sending messengers.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents a logical paradox: the denier of prophethood claims to know that God did not send a messenger, but this claim is itself a form of informing about God — which is precisely what a prophet does. Therefore, the denier implicitly claims prophetic authority for himself. The author then argues that anyone who acknowledges God's command and obligation upon humans must admit that God sends messengers. The real dispute is about whether a messenger can be human. Citing the Qur'anic verse that records the objection of the disbelievers, the author concludes that since humanity does not prevent messengerhood, the possibility of sending messengers is established.

Key Terms
الإنكار
الإقرار
الرسالة
الصورة البشرية
جواز البعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The denier must claim knowledge about God, which is a prophetic claim, so the denial is self-refuting.

Checkpoint

A) It leads to atheism
B) The denier himself claims knowledge about God, which is a form of prophethood
C) It contradicts the Qur'an
D) It is irrational

Key Takeaway
Denying prophethood is inconsistent because it relies on the same divine claim it rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sabians and Hanifas: Contrasting Views on Prophethood and Worship
Comparing Sabian and Hanifa views on prophecy and worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sabians and Hanifas differed in their understanding of prophethood and worship, and why the Hanifa position preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Hanifas worship God alone; Sabians worship through idols to spiritual beings.

In early theological debates, two contrasting positions emerged regarding how to approach the divine. The Sabians directed worship to spiritual beings through physical idols, while the Hanifas revered human prophets without deifying them. This passage from al-Ash'ari clarifies these differences and grounds the Hanifa view in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main contrast between the two groups.
Notice the key terms: الصابئة, الحنفاء, الروحانيات, الجسمانيات.
2
Identify the beliefs of each group
List the specific beliefs of the Sabians and Hanifas regarding prophecy, worship, and idols.
Sabians: turn to planets and their temples; Hanifas: turn to human prophets without idolatry.
3
Analyze the dual aspect of prophets
Examine the two aspects of humanity and messengerhood, and understand why this distinction prevents deification.
The Quranic verse (17:93) is key: 'Say: Glory be to my Lord! Was I anything but a human messenger?'
4
Reflect on Abraham's declaration
Consider how Abraham's statement in the passage exemplifies the Hanifa position and is upheld by the Seal of the Prophets.
This shows the consistency of monotheism from Abraham to Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصابئة سلموا الرسالة للروحانيات وأوجبوا التوجه إلى هياكلها من الكواكب السبعة وعملوا الأشخاص على صورة الهياكل فتقربوا إليها، وذلك عود إلى الصورة والأصنام والحنفاء سلموا الرسالة للجسمانيات وأوجبوا التوجه إلى أشخاصهم ولم يتخذوا أصناما آلهة ولا اعتقدوا النبيين أربابا، لكنهم قالوا : لهم طرفان بشرية ورسالة ( قُلْ سُبْحَانَ رَبِّي هَلْ كُنتُ إِلَّا بَشَرًا رَّسُولاً ) [الإسراء : ٩٣] فبطرف البشرية يشاكل نوع الإنسان ويشاركه فيأكل ويشرب وينام ويستيقظ ويحيى ويموت، وبطرف الرسالة يشاكل نوع الملائكة ويشاركه فيسبح ويقدس ويبيت عند ربه فيطعمه ويسقيه وتنام عيناه ولا ينام قلبه ويموت قالبه ولا يموت روحه، والمناظرة بين الفريقين في أول الزمان كانت قائمة وقد أظهرها الخليل صلى الله عليه وسلم حيث قال : ( إني بريء مما تشركون إني وجهت وجهي للذي فطر السماوات والأرض حنيفا وما أنا من المشركين ) [الأنعام 78، 79] وبذلك أمر خاتم النبيين عليه السلام.
And the Sabians affirmed messengerhood for spiritual beings and made obligatory turning towards their temples from the seven planets, and they made human-shaped forms on the image of those temples to draw near to them. That is a return to form and idols. And the Hanifas affirmed messengerhood for physical beings and made obligatory turning towards their persons, and they did not take idols as gods, nor did they believe the prophets to be lords. Rather, they said: They have two aspects: humanity and messengerhood – 'Say: Glory be to my Lord! Was I anything but a human messenger?' [Al-Isra' 17:93]. By the aspect of humanity, he resembles the human species and shares with them: he eats, drinks, sleeps, wakes, lives, and dies. By the aspect of messengerhood, he resembles the angelic species and shares with them: he glorifies, sanctifies, passes the night with his Lord who feeds him and gives him drink, his eyes sleep but his heart does not sleep, his body dies but his soul does not die. And the debate between the two groups existed in the beginning of time, and it was demonstrated by the Friend (Ibrahim), peace be upon him, when he said: 'Indeed, I am free from what you associate. Indeed, I have turned my face toward Him who created the heavens and the earth, hanifan, and I am not of the polytheists.' [Al-An'am 6:78-79]. And with this did the Seal of the Prophets (peace be upon him) command.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Sabians and the Hanifas (monotheists) regarding their understanding of prophethood and worship. The Sabians accept messengerhood for spiritual beings (angels) and direct worship to physical structures representing the seven planets, thus falling into idolatry. The Hanifas accept messengerhood for human beings and worship only God, not the messengers themselves. They affirm that prophets have two aspects: humanity and messengerhood. As a human, the prophet shares in all human attributes. As a messenger, he shares in angelic worship and closeness to God, but is not divine. The author then notes that this debate was present from early times, exemplified by Abraham's declaration of monotheism, and this is the command of the Seal of the Prophets (Muhammad).

Key Terms
الصابئة
الحنفاء
الروحانيات
الجسمانيات
الهياكل
الأصنام
طرفان: بشرية ورسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Hanifa position elevates prophets to divine status, similar to the Christian view of Christ.
Correction: The Hanifas explicitly deny that prophets are lords, affirming only two aspects: humanity and messengerhood. Prophets are fully human in one aspect and divinely appointed messengers in another, but never objects of worship.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between Sabians and Hanifas regarding prophecy?
A) Sabians accept prophets while Hanifas reject them
B) Sabians worship spiritual beings through idols, Hanifas worship God and honor human prophets
C) Both worship idols
D) Hanifas consider prophets as gods

Key Takeaway
The Hanifas maintain divine transcendence by worshipping God alone and honoring prophets as human messengers, avoiding idolatry and deification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sabians crafted idols in the likeness of temples to draw near to spiritual beings. In what areas of your life do you find yourself creating 'likenesses' or intermediaries that subtly distract your heart from direct, pure worship of God alone?
?
The Hanifas honor prophets as having both humanity and messengerhood. Have you ever fallen into either extreme—either diminishing the Prophet's honor by focusing only on his humanity, or exalting him to a level only for God? Which side do you lean toward, and what one concrete change can you make today to correct that balance?
?
Abraham declared, 'I am free from what you associate.' Is there any association—whether a person, a status, a desire, or a comfort—that you are not fully free from? What would declaring innocence from it cost you, and are you ready to pay that price today?

Denial as Acknowledgment: The Paradox of Prophethood
Argument that denying a messenger affirms the possibility of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how denying a specific messenger actually affirms the general possibility of prophethood.

Core Idea
Denial of a prophet is itself a claim about God's actions.

This passage presents a counterintuitive argument: those who reject a particular messenger are not denying the concept of prophecy; rather, they are implicitly asserting that they know what God did or did not do. Before we can argue about the specifics of a prophet, we must first establish that sending messengers is rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the paradox
Reflect on how denying a specific messenger could be considered an admission of the possibility of messengers in general. This is the central twist of the argument.
The key is that the denier claims knowledge of God's actions.
2
Read the passage and identify the two logical steps
First, the denial paradox: claiming God did not send a messenger is itself a claim to knowledge. Second, the obligation argument: anyone who accepts that God imposes duties must accept that He sends messengers.
Look for the phrases 'فقد ادعى' and 'فمن سلم'.
3
Connect the Qur'anic verse to the argument
The verse (17:94) shows that the real objection of the disbelievers was not the concept of a messenger, but the messenger being a human. This leads to the final conclusion.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Once we show that humanity does not contradict prophethood, the possibility of sending messengers is established. The denier's position collapses.
This conclusion is the immediate payoff of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ادعى أنه مخبر عن الله أنه لم يرسل رسولاً فقد ادعى الرسالة لنفسه، فكان إنكاره إقرارًا وعاد إنكاره تسليما، ومن سلم أن الله على عباده تكليفا وأمرًا فقد سلم أنه يرسل رسولاً، وإنما النزاع وقع في أن الرسالة هل تتصور في الصورة البشرية ( وَمَا مَنَعَ النَّاسَ أَن يُؤْمِنُوا إِذْ جَاءَهُمُ الْهُدَى إِلَّا أَن قَالُوا أَبَعَثَ اللَّهُ بَشَرًا رَّسُولاً [الإسراء : ٩٤] وإذا بينا أن البشرية لا تنافي الرسالة، فقد أثبتنا جواز انبعاث الرسل.
For he who claims that he is informed about God that He did not send a messenger has claimed the message for himself, so his denial becomes an acknowledgment and his denial becomes an admission. And whoever concedes that God imposes obligations and commands upon His servants has conceded that He sends a messenger. The dispute is only about whether the messenger can be conceived in human form. (And nothing prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them except that they said: 'Has God sent a human as a messenger?' [Al-Isra 17:94].) And when we have demonstrated that humanity does not contradict the message, we have established the possibility of sending messengers.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about the nature of rejection of prophets. The author argues that the denier of a specific messenger implicitly affirms the possibility of sending messengers because he claims to know that God did not send one, thus he must have some knowledge of God's actions. Moreover, anyone who accepts that God imposes obligations on His servants must also accept that God can send messengers to convey those obligations. The real point of contention, the author notes, is not whether messengers can be sent, but whether they can be human. The Qur'anic verse (17:94) is cited to show that the disbelievers' objection was precisely that the messenger was a human. The author concludes that once it is shown that humanity does not preclude prophethood, the possibility of sending messengers is proven.

Key Terms
الرسالة
الرسول
جواز انبعاث الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying a particular prophet is a rejection of prophethood entirely.
Correction: The author shows that such denial actually implies acceptance of the general concept, because the denier claims knowledge about God's actions.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the denier of a messenger?
A) He is a disbeliever in all messengers.
B) His denial implies acknowledgment of the possibility of sending messengers.
C) He must be shown a miracle.
D) He is excused for his ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The denial of a specific messenger confirms rather than refutes the principle of prophethood, because it presumes knowledge of God's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you found yourself denying or avoiding a specific command of God because it came through a human messenger like you? Does this lesson reveal a hidden pride in your heart? How will you humbly accept God's choice of human prophets today?
?
The deniers claimed to know that God would not send a human messenger. Is there an area of your life where you assume you know what God would or would not do, thereby limiting His sovereignty? Will you repent from that presumption right now?
?
This passage shows that accepting God's commands implies accepting His messengers. Do you truly accept all the commands brought by the Prophet Muhammad, or do you pick and choose based on your own desires? What is one command you have been ignoring, and what practical step will you take to submit to it starting today?

Sabians and Their View on Messengers
Sabian acceptance of spiritual messengers and idolatrous practices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian doctrine of prophethood and why it led to idolatry.

Core Idea
Sabians accepted only spiritual messengers but fell into polytheism.

The Sabians are one of the ancient sects discussed by al-Shahrastani. Their understanding of prophethood was limited to spiritual beings, which led them to create physical representations for worship. This lesson examines their view and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sabian passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note how the Sabians understood 'risala' (message) and what they directed worship toward.
The passage associates the Sabians with the seven planets.
2
Identify the core elements
List: (1) They accepted messengers only from spiritual beings (ruhaniyyat). (2) They obligated orientation toward planetary temples. (3) They made physical figures resembling those temples. (4) The author equates this with idolatry.
3
Contrast with true monotheism
Consider why the author calls this a 'return to images and idols.' How does this differ from the Hanifite view? This contrast will be developed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصابئة سلموا الرسالة للروحانيات وأوجبوا التوجه إلى هياكلها من الكواكب السبعة وعملوا الأشخاص على صورة الهياكل فتقربوا إليها، وذلك عود إلى الصورة والأصنام والحنفاء سلموا الرسالة للجسمانيات وأوجبوا التوجه إلى أشخاصهم ولم يتخذوا أصناما آلهة ولا اعتقدوا النبيين أربابا، لكنهم قالوا: لهم طرفان بشرية ورسالة ( قُلْ سُبْحَانَ رَبِّي هَلْ كُنتُ إِلَّا بَشَرًا رَّسُولاً ) [الإسراء : ٩٣] فبطرف البشرية يشاكل نوع الإنسان ويشاركه فيأكل ويشرب وينام ويستيقظ ويحيى ويموت، وبطرف الرسالة يشاكل نوع الملائكة ويشاركه فيسبح ويقدس ويبيت عند ربه فيطعمه ويسقيه وتنام عيناه ولا ينام قلبه ويموت قالبه ولا يموت روحه، والمناظرة بين الفريقين في أول الزمان كانت قائمة وقد أظهرها الخليل صلى الله عليه وسلم حيث قال : ( إني بريء مما تشركون إني وجهت وجهي للذي فطر السماوات والأرض حنيفا وما أنا من المشركين ) 78، 179، وبذلك أمر خاتم النبيين عليه السلام قال : على ملة أبيكم إبراهيم، وقال : إني أمرت أن أكون أول من أسلم [الأنعام: ١٤] وقال : ( إني وجهت وجهي ) الآية وغيرها من الآيات، وفيه فصول كثيرة ذكرناها في كتاب الملل والنحل.
The Sabians accepted the message for spiritual beings and obligated orientation towards their temples from the seven planets, and they made figures in the form of temples to draw near to them, and that is a return to images and idols. The Hanifites accepted the message for physical beings and obligated orientation towards their persons, and they did not take idols as gods nor did they believe the prophets to be lords. Rather, they said: They have two aspects: humanity and message. (Say: 'Exalted is my Lord! Was I ever but a human messenger?' [Al-Isra 17:93].) By the aspect of humanity, he resembles the human type and shares with it: he eats, drinks, sleeps, wakes, lives, and dies. By the aspect of message, he resembles the angelic type and shares with it: he glorifies, sanctifies, spends the night with his Lord Who feeds him and gives him to drink, his eyes sleep but his heart does not sleep, his body dies but his spirit does not die. The debate between the two groups was ongoing in early times, and it was manifested by al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him, when he said: 'Indeed I am free from what you associate. Indeed I have turned my face toward He who created the heavens and the earth, hanifan, and I am not of the polytheists.' (78:179?) And by that the Seal of the Prophets was commanded, peace be upon him: he said: 'On the religion of your father Abraham,' and said: 'I have been commanded to be the first of those who submit' (Al-An'am 6:14), and said: 'Indeed I have turned my face' etc., and other verses. And there are many chapters on this which we have mentioned in the book Al-Milal wa al-Nihal.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage classifies two groups: the Sabians and the Hanifites, regarding their views on prophethood. The Sabians accepted messengers only among spiritual beings (angels or celestial spirits) and directed worship towards the temples of the seven planets, making physical representations to draw near. The author equates this with idolatry. The Hanifites (monotheists) accepted messengers as physical human beings and directed worship towards them (as prophets), but they did not deify the prophets. They distinguished two aspects of the prophet: a human aspect shared with mankind, involving physical needs, and a prophetic aspect shared with angels, involving spiritual acts and closeness to God. The author cites the Qur'anic verse (17:93) where the Prophet Muhammad declares his humanity. Then he mentions that Abraham (al-Khalil) already demonstrated this correct understanding by declaring dissociation from polytheism and turning to the Creator. The final prophet was also commanded to follow Abraham's religion. The author notes that he has discussed this topic extensively in his book Al-Milal wa al-Nihal.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الروحانيات
هياكل
الكواكب السبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all pre-Islamic groups accepted human prophets; the Sabians did not.
Correction: The Sabians only accepted spiritual messengers, not human ones, and their practices devolved into idolatry.

Checkpoint
What did the Sabians accept as messengers?
A) Human beings
B) Spiritual beings (ruhaniyyat)
C) Both humans and spirits
D) No messengers at all

Key Takeaway
The Sabian view of prophethood, though acknowledging divine communication, led to idolatry by focusing worship on planetary temples and physical representations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a practice in your worship that resembles the Sabians' reasoning—thinking it brings you closer to Allah when it actually distances you? What step will you take today to identify and remove such practices?
?
The Sabians believed they were on the right path but ended in shirk. What aspects of your own religious life have you accepted from culture or tradition without verifying against the Quran and authentic Sunnah? Will you examine them now?
?
If you had been a Sabian, would you have recognized the subtle shift from spiritual devotion to idolatry? How can you ensure that your own focus on sacred places, objects, or personalities does not cross into forbidden imitation of the polytheists?

Hanifite View: Prophets as Human and Angelic
Hanifite view of human messengers with two aspects and Abrahamic foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanifite balanced view of prophets as having both human and angelic aspects, and how this aligns with the Abrahamic tradition.

Core Idea
Prophets are human in body, angelic in spirit.

The Hanifites (monotheists) held a nuanced view of prophets: they are fully human yet also partake in angelic qualities through their prophetic office. This lesson explains the two-aspect theory and its scriptural foundation, culminating in the example of Abraham and the final prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hanifite passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Pay attention to the two aspects (tarafan) and how each is described.
The verse 17:93 is cited: 'Say: Exalted is my Lord! Was I ever but a human messenger?'
2
Understand the two aspects
Humanity (bishariyya): eating, drinking, sleeping, living, dying. Message (risala): glorifying, sanctifying, special closeness to God, eyes sleep heart awake, body dies spirit lives.
3
Trace the Abrahamic connection
Note that Abraham (al-Khalil) exemplified this correct view by dissociating from polytheism and turning to the Creator. The final prophet was commanded to follow Abraham's religion.
This shows continuity of the hanifiyya.

Source Text

Why Prophets Do Not Grant Respite
Refutation of the claim that prophets must grant respite to doubters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophets do not need to grant respite to doubters and how they present immediate rational proof.

Core Idea
Prophets do not delay; they present an undeniable proof from the start.

This lesson addresses a key objection against prophethood: the claim that a prophet can be refuted if he demands immediate belief without granting time for reflection. The author distinguishes between the Mu'tazilite position and the correct Sunni view, showing that the prophet's call is inherently accompanied by a compelling rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note the Mu'tazilite view that prophets must grant respite, and the opposing view that they do not.
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite claim.
2
Understand the prophet's response
The prophet says: 'I have come to remove the respite and guide you by pointing to your need for a Creator.' This is an immediate proof.
The proof is based on your own existence.
3
Connect to the Qur'anic verse
The author quotes 'O people, worship your Lord...' to show that the call begins with tawhid, not with demanding a delay.
All prophets start with tawhid.
4
Reflect on the condition for respite
Respite would only be needed if the call lacked proof. But the prophet's call is never without a compelling proof.
The proof is both rational and scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن مسألة إمهال النظر وإفحام الأنبياء، فإنما يلزم الإفحام على من قال بالمهلة، ويجب على النبي إمهال النظر وذلك مذهب المعتزلة، وأما قال بأنه لا يمهله ولا يجب عليه أن يمهله، بل يجيب بأني جئت لأرفع المهلة وأرشدك إلى معرفة المرسل بأن أخبر عنك بما لا يمكنك إنكاره وهو احتياجك إلى صانع فاطر حكيم، إذ كنت تعرف ضرورة أنك لم تكن فكنت، وما كنت بنفسك بل يكون غيرك، وهذه هي ابتداء الدعوة بـ ( يا أيها الناس اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم والذين من قبلكم لعلكم تتقون ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۱] ، فكيف يمهله ويكله إلى نفسه وهو مأمور بدعوة الناس إلى التوحيد والمعرفة حالة فحالة وشخصا فشخصا، والاستمهال إنما يتوجه إذا خلت الدعوة عن الحجة الملزمة والدلالة المفحمة، فإن من تحققت رسالته عنه في نفسها وتصدى لدعوة الناس إلى وحدانية الله تعالى لا يخلى دعوته عن الدلالة على التوحيد أولا، ثم يتحدى بالرسالة عنه ثانيا اعتبر حال جميع الأنبياء عليهم السلام في دعوتهم.
As for the answer to the issue of granting respite for reflection and the refutation of prophets, such refutation only applies to those who assert the respite, and that the prophet must grant respite—that is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila. But as for the one who says that he does not grant him respite and it is not obligatory upon him to grant him respite, rather he replies: 'I have come to remove the respite and guide you to knowledge of the Sender by informing you of what you cannot deny, namely your need for a Wise Creator, since you know necessarily that you were not and then you were, and you were not by yourself, but rather another brought you into being.' This is the beginning of the call: 'O people, worship your Lord who created you and those before you, that you may become righteous' (Al-Baqara 2:21). So how can he grant him respite and leave him to himself when he is commanded to call people to tawhid (monotheism) and knowledge repeatedly and individually? Desiring respite only arises when the call is devoid of a compelling proof and an overwhelming indication. For he whose message is verified in itself and he devotes himself to calling people to the oneness of God Almighty does not leave his call devoid of evidence for tawhid first, then he challenges with the message second. Consider the situation of all the prophets, peace be upon them, in their call.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: that a prophet can be refuted if he is asked for time to think (imhal al-nazar). The author distinguishes between two positions. The Mu'tazilite view holds that the prophet must grant such respite. The correct view, according to the author, is that the prophet does not grant respite and is not obliged to. Instead, he immediately presents an undeniable rational proof: the opponent's own existence as a contingent being requiring a Creator. This is the initial call of all prophets, as exemplified in the Qur'anic verse 'O people, worship your Lord...'. The author argues that a prophet's call is always accompanied by a compelling proof from the outset, so there is no need for respite. He first establishes tawhid through rational argument, then challenges with the proof of prophethood. The prophets' example shows they did not delay.

Key Terms
إمهال النظر
إفحام
المعتزلة
التوحيد
الحجة الملزمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets must grant people time to reflect before demanding belief, otherwise they can be refuted.
Correction: Prophets do not need to grant such respite because they present immediate, undeniable rational proof, such as the argument from contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the prophet not need to grant respite?
A) Because the prophet is impatient.
B) Because the prophet's call comes with an irrefutable proof from the start.
C) Because people already believe.
D) Because the prophet does not need to convince anyone.

Key Takeaway
Prophets do not need to grant respite because their call is always accompanied by an immediate, compelling rational proof that leaves no room for doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a clear truth from the Quran that challenges your lifestyle, do you ask for more time to think, or do you respond immediately? What does this passage say about that?
?
If you are calling others to Allah, do you present undeniable proofs from the start, or do you delay out of fear of their reaction? How can you follow the prophetic method described here?
?
This passage says the prophetic call begins with an undeniable proof. Is your own faith built on such a proof, or is it based on something else? Ask yourself: Have you truly accepted the contingency argument—that you did not create yourself and need a Creator—and let it transform your life?

Prophethood
intermediate
