Necessity of a Prophet and the Immediate Proof of Truthfulness
The author argues for the necessity of a chosen prophet and the requirement of an immediate miracle as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet must be chosen by God and why his claim must be accompanied by an immediate miracle as proof of truthfulness.

Core Idea
A prophet's claim requires an immediate miracle because the duty to believe is instantaneous.

Having established God's sovereignty, the author now argues that since ordinary people cannot receive divine revelation directly, God must choose a prophet. The prophetic claim is a rational possibility, but it becomes binding only when accompanied by an immediate proof of truthfulness—a miracle. This proof cannot be delayed because the hearer is obligated to believe immediately.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the logical sequence from the necessity of a prophet to the timing of the proof.
Notice the use of the Quranic verse.
2
Understand the rational possibility
The author states that the claim of prophethood is among the rationally possible things (jā'izāt 'aqliyya). Why is that important?
It means it is neither necessary nor impossible.
3
Trace the argument for immediate proof
The author argues that the proof must not be delayed from the claim because the obligated person must believe immediately. The capacity to believe is sufficient, and that capacity is realized when intellect and proof exist.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider why delaying the miracle would undermine the obligation to believe. How does this relate to the concept of taklīf?

The Necessity of a Prophet and the Proof of Truthfulness
Arguments for necessity of prophet and immediate proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophetic claim must be accompanied by an immediate proof of truthfulness, and how this proof obligates belief.

Core Idea
A prophet's proof of truthfulness must be simultaneous with his claim.

This passage from al-Shahrastani establishes a fundamental principle in prophethood: once we accept that God communicates through prophets, we must also accept that the prophet's claim is accompanied by an undeniable sign. The author argues that this sign cannot be delayed; it must be present at the moment of the claim because the listener is immediately obligated to believe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from the necessity of a prophet to the requirement of an accompanying proof.
2
Identify the premises
The argument starts by assuming agreement on previous stages (that God is a sovereign and ordinary people cannot receive revelation). This leads to the necessity of a chosen prophet. Identify these premises in the text.
The Qur'anic verse is cited as support.
3
Understand the key argument
The prophet's claim is a rational possibility, but when a proof of truthfulness accompanies it, belief becomes obligatory. Note that the proof must be immediate—it cannot be delayed.
The author emphasizes that ability to believe is only realized through complete intellect and the presence of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا حصل وفاقنا على هذه المراتب وعلمنا أن كل واحد من آحاد الناس لا يتأهل لقبول أمر الله تعالى وحيا له، تعين بالضرورة واحدا اصطفاه من عباده (وربك يخلق ما يشاء ويختار ما كان لهم الخيرة) [القصص: 68]، فدعواه نفسها ما الجائزات العقلية، وإذا كانت الدعوى مقرونة بدليل الصدق وجب التصديق، وأن وجب لا يتراخى دليل الصدق عن نفس الخبر، فإن المخاطب مكلف بالتصديق في الحال، ولا يتراخى الوجوب عليه إلا أن يعرف الصدق، بل التمكن كاف كما بينا ولكن التمكن إنما تحقق من جانب المكلف بوجود العقل التام والعقل إنما يدرك إذا وجد دليل الصدق، فكما لا يتراخى الوجوب عن وقت التكليف، لا يتراخى أيضًا دليل الصدق عن نفس الدعوى، فليبحث هاهنا كل البحث أن الدليل المقرون بالخبر والدعوى ماذا، بحيث لا يلزم إفحام، ولا ينفع إحجام.
And when we have agreement on these stages and we know that every single ordinary person is not qualified to accept the command of God Almighty as a revelation to him, it is necessarily determined that He select one from His servants, and your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice [Qasas 68]. So his claim itself is among the intellectual possibilities. And if the claim is accompanied by a proof of truthfulness, then belief is obligatory. And it is necessary that the proof of truthfulness is not delayed from the report itself, for the addressee is obligated to believe immediately, and the obligation is not delayed for him except that he knows the truthfulness. Rather, ability is sufficient as we have explained, but ability is only realized from the side of the obligated person by the existence of complete intellect, and the intellect only perceives when a proof of truthfulness exists. So just as the obligation is not delayed from the time of obligation, likewise the proof of truthfulness is not delayed from the claim itself. So let everyone search here: what is the proof that is conjoined with the report and the claim, such that no confusion (ifham) is necessary, and no withholding (ihjam) benefits.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam proof for the necessity of prophethood and the requirement that a prophetic claim be accompanied by a conclusive sign. The author begins by stating that once we agree that God is a sovereign king who can command and forbid, and that ordinary humans cannot receive revelation directly, it becomes necessary that God appoint a prophet from among His servants, citing the Qur'anic verse that God chooses whom He wills. The prophet's claim is itself a rational possibility (ja'izat 'aqliyya). The key point is that if the claim is accompanied by a proof of truthfulness (dalil al-sidq), then belief becomes obligatory. Furthermore, the proof must be simultaneous with the claim—it cannot be delayed—because the addressee is obligated to believe immediately upon hearing the claim. The obligation to believe is not postponed until the person verifies the truthfulness; rather, the mere presence of the proof suffices, provided the person has complete intellect. The intellect can only recognize truth when the proof is present. Hence, just as the obligation is immediate, the proof must also be immediate. The passage concludes by challenging the reader to identify what that proof is, such that it leaves no room for confusion or evasion.

الجائزات العقلية
التكليف

Misconception: One might think that the proof of prophecy can be delayed or verified later.
Correction: The author insists that the proof must be simultaneous with the claim because the listener is obligated to believe immediately.

Key Takeaway
A prophetic claim must be accompanied by an immediate proof of truthfulness to obligate belief.

Definition of Naskh and Its Role in Prophethood
Two definitions of naskh and its connection to final prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of naskh (abrogation) and how it pertains to the finality of prophethood.

Core Idea
Naskh clarifies the end of a ruling's duration, not a change of divine mind.

The passage begins by linking naskh to the topic of prophethood, stating that the Shari'ah of Muhammad (SAW) abrogates all previous laws and he is the seal of prophets. Two definitions of naskh are then given by scholars, both emphasizing that naskh is not arbitrary but a clarification of time limits. We will explore these definitions to set the stage for understanding the Jewish objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the author's rhythm.
2
Identify the two definitions of naskh.
Find the phrase 'قال بعض العلماء' and note the two definitions: lifting a ruling after establishment vs. clarifying the end of its duration.
The second definition likens naskh to specification by time.
3
Consider why naskh does not imply change in Allah's knowledge.
Reflect on the second definition: if naskh only clarifies a pre-set time limit, then no new knowledge appears for Allah. This will be important for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتصل بهذا الموضع الكلام في النسخ، وأن هذه الشريعة ناسخة للشرائع كلها وهي أتمها وأكملها، وأن محمدًا المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتم الأنبياء، وبه نختم الكتاب.
قال بعض العلماء: النسخ رفع الحكم بعد ثبوته، وقال بعضهم : النسخ تبيين انتهاء مدة الحكم وكأنه تخصيص بزمان وهو بظاهره كان شاملا لكل زمان، وبالنسخ يتبين أنه لم يشمل الأوقات كلها.
وقالت اليهود: النسخ رفع تكليف بعد توجهه على العباد وذلك لا يجوز في حق الباري تعالى؛ فإنه يؤدي ذلك إلى البدا والندم على ما قال، ولو أن واحدا منا أمر غلامه على كل حال أن يفعل فعلا ثم ذلك؛ إما في الحال أو في ثاني الحال، دل ذلك على أنه بدا له ذلك؛ أي ظهر له أمر بخلاف ما تصور في الأول، أو ندم بالقلب على تكليفه الأول، وهاتان القضيتان مستحيلتان في حق من لا تخفى عليه ذرة في الأرض ولا في السماء، ومن له الملك والملك والتصرف في عباده بما يشاء.
And connected to this topic is the discussion on naskh (abrogation), and that this Shari'ah abrogates all the Shari'ahs entirely, and it is the most complete and perfect of them, and that Muhammad al-Mustafa (SAW) is the seal of the prophets, and with it we conclude the book. Some scholars said: Naskh is the lifting of a ruling after its establishment. And some said: Naskh is the clarification of the end of the period of the ruling, and it is like a specification by time, while in its apparent sense it was inclusive of all times, and by naskh it becomes clear that it did not encompass all times. And the Jews said: Naskh is the lifting of a duty after it has been directed upon the servants, and that is not permissible with respect to the Exalted Creator, for it leads to badā' (change of mind) and nadam (regret) as they said. And if one of us commanded his servant in every case to do an action, then [changed] that, either immediately or later, that would indicate that he had a badā' (i.e., something appeared to him contrary to what he first conceived) or that he regretted in his heart his initial command. These two matters are impossible with respect to Him from Whom not a speck is hidden in the earth or in the heavens, and to Whom belongs sovereignty and dominion and disposal over His servants as He wills.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of naskh (abrogation) and presents the Jewish objection against it. The passage first states the Islamic position: the Shari'ah of Muhammad (SAW) abrogates all previous laws and is the most complete, and he is the seal of prophets, concluding the book. Then it provides two definitions of naskh: one as lifting a ruling after establishment, and another as clarifying the end of a ruling's duration, akin to specification by time. The core of the passage is the Jewish objection: they argue that naskh is impossible for Allah because it would imply badā' (a new appearance or change of mind) and nadam (regret). They illustrate with a human example: if a master commands a servant to do something and then changes the command, it indicates either a new consideration (badā') or regret. These are impossible for Allah, who has perfect foreknowledge and sovereignty. The objection sets up the refutation in subsequent passages.

Key Terms
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naskh means that Allah changes His mind or discovers something new.
Correction: Naskh is merely a clarification of the preordained duration of a ruling, not a change in divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of naskh avoids the implication that Allah's knowledge changes?
A) Naskh as the lifting of a ruling after its establishment.
B) Naskh as the clarification of the end of a ruling's duration.
C) Naskh as the abrogation of all previous laws.
D) Both definitions equally avoid implying change.

Key Takeaway
Naskh is not a divine afterthought but a clarification of the temporal scope of a ruling, preserving Allah's perfect knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the Qur'an and Sunnah as the final authority in your life, or do you sometimes give equal weight to other sources of guidance? How does the concept of naskh challenge you to submit completely to the final Shari'ah?
?
When you learn that a certain ruling was abrogated, do you ever feel a sense of inconsistency in Allah's commands? How does the definition of naskh as 'clarification of time' help you trust Allah's perfect knowledge and wisdom in your own life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's law is the most complete and perfect, what habits in your daily life would change? Name one thing you would start doing or stop doing to align more with the final Shari'ah.

The Jewish Objection to Naskh: Badā' and Nadam
Jewish objection that naskh implies badā' and nadam, and the refutation of it being impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish argument that naskh implies badā' (change of mind) and nadam (regret) and why this is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Naskh does not imply divine change; it is a clarification of an eternal decree.

After presenting the definitions, the author reports the Jewish objection that naskh would lead to badā' and nadam in Allah. They illustrate with a human master-servant example. This objection is critical to refute, as it challenges the coherence of abrogation. We will examine the objection and the reason it is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the human example.
Read the passage where the Jews argue that naskh would be like a master changing orders, indicating badā' or nadam.
Note the analogy: a master commands a servant, then changes the command.
2
Understand why badā' and nadam are impossible for Allah.
The passage states that nothing is hidden from Allah; He has perfect foreknowledge and sovereignty. Therefore, badā' and nadam cannot apply to Him.
Badā' means a new matter appears; nadam means regret.
3
Link to the previous lesson's definition.
Recall the second definition of naskh as clarifying duration. That definition already avoids the implication of change, providing a response to the Jewish objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتصل بهذا الموضع الكلام في النسخ، وأن هذه الشريعة ناسخة للشرائع كلها وهي أتمها وأكملها، وأن محمدًا المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتم الأنبياء، وبه نختم الكتاب.
قال بعض العلماء: النسخ رفع الحكم بعد ثبوته، وقال بعضهم : النسخ تبيين انتهاء مدة الحكم وكأنه تخصيص بزمان وهو بظاهره كان شاملا لكل زمان، وبالنسخ يتبين أنه لم يشمل الأوقات كلها.
وقالت اليهود: النسخ رفع تكليف بعد توجهه على العباد وذلك لا يجوز في حق الباري تعالى؛ فإنه يؤدي ذلك إلى البدا والندم على ما قال، ولو أن واحدا منا أمر غلامه على كل حال أن يفعل فعلا ثم ذلك؛ إما في الحال أو في ثاني الحال، دل ذلك على أنه بدا له ذلك؛ أي ظهر له أمر بخلاف ما تصور في الأول، أو ندم بالقلب على تكليفه الأول، وهاتان القضيتان مستحيلتان في حق من لا تخفى عليه ذرة في الأرض ولا في السماء، ومن له الملك والملك والتصرف في عباده بما يشاء.
And connected to this topic is the discussion on naskh (abrogation), and that this Shari'ah abrogates all the Shari'ahs entirely, and it is the most complete and perfect of them, and that Muhammad al-Mustafa (SAW) is the seal of the prophets, and with it we conclude the book. Some scholars said: Naskh is the lifting of a ruling after its establishment. And some said: Naskh is the clarification of the end of the period of the ruling, and it is like a specification by time, while in its apparent sense it was inclusive of all times, and by naskh it becomes clear that it did not encompass all times. And the Jews said: Naskh is the lifting of a duty after it has been directed upon the servants, and that is not permissible with respect to the Exalted Creator, for it leads to badā' (change of mind) and nadam (regret) as they said. And if one of us commanded his servant in every case to do an action, then [changed] that, either immediately or later, that would indicate that he had a badā' (i.e., something appeared to him contrary to what he first conceived) or that he regretted in his heart his initial command. These two matters are impossible with respect to Him from Whom not a speck is hidden in the earth or in the heavens, and to Whom belongs sovereignty and dominion and disposal over His servants as He wills.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of naskh (abrogation) and presents the Jewish objection against it. The passage first states the Islamic position: the Shari'ah of Muhammad (SAW) abrogates all previous laws and is the most complete, and he is the seal of prophets, concluding the book. Then it provides two definitions of naskh: one as lifting a ruling after establishment, and another as clarifying the end of a ruling's duration, akin to specification by time. The core of the passage is the Jewish objection: they argue that naskh is impossible for Allah because it would imply badā' (a new appearance or change of mind) and nadam (regret). They illustrate with a human example: if a master commands a servant to do something and then changes the command, it indicates either a new consideration (badā') or regret. These are impossible for Allah, who has perfect foreknowledge and sovereignty. The objection sets up the refutation in subsequent passages.

Key Terms
نسخ
بداء
ندم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naskh implies that Allah changes His mind or regrets, like a human master changing orders.
Correction: Allah's knowledge is perfect and eternal; naskh only clarifies a preordained duration, not a change of plan.

Checkpoint
Why do the Jews consider naskh impossible for Allah?
A) Because it would imply that previous prophets were wrong.
B) Because it would imply badā' and nadam, which are impossible for Allah.
C) Because the Quran forbids abrogation.
D) Because naskh only applies to human laws.

Key Takeaway
The Jewish objection fails because naskh does not involve a change in divine knowledge; it is a manifestation of Allah's eternal decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's truth — that naskh is a manifestation of eternal knowledge — challenge that feeling? What would it take for you to fully trust that Allah's decrees are not reactions but perfect wisdom?
?
The Jews compared Allah to a human master who changes his mind out of ignorance or regret. Are there areas of your life where you unconsciously compare Allah to humans — expecting Him to think, feel, or act like people? Name one such area and commit to replacing that mental image with the understanding that ‘not a speck is hidden from Him.’
?
Badā' and nadam are impossible for Allah because His knowledge is complete. Yet, when your plans fail or life takes an unexpected turn, do you feel as if Allah 'changed His mind' about you? Reflect on a specific disappointment and ask: How might this 'change' actually be part of an eternal plan you cannot see? What would it look like to respond with submission rather than confusion?

The Prophetic Argument for the Possibility of Naskh
Poses a historical dichotomy to argue for naskh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the historical sequence of prophets demonstrates the necessity of naskh (abrogation) in divine law.

Core Idea
If Musa brought nothing new, he wouldn't be a lawgiver—so naskh must be possible.

The author now turns to a historical argument rooted in the succession of prophets. He notes that Musa (AS) came after many earlier prophets, and then poses a crucial question: was the law he brought entirely the same as theirs, or partially new? This simple dichotomy forces us to accept that naskh must have occurred, otherwise Musa's role as a lawgiving prophet would be undermined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is implied on the next page.
2
Identify the factual premise
Notice that the author begins by stating a historical fact: Musa (AS) existed later than many prophets. This is the foundation of the argument.
The list includes Adam, Nuh, Ibrahim, etc.
3
Understand the dichotomy
The author asks: was all of Musa's law already legislated for earlier prophets, or only part of it? Consider the implication of each answer.
If all of it was already for them, then Musa brought no new Shari'ah, which contradicts his role as a lawgiving prophet.
4
Draw the missing conclusion
Since the option that Musa brought no new law is unacceptable, the only remaining possibility is that some of his law was new, implying abrogation (naskh) of earlier laws.
The argument shows naskh is a necessary feature of prophetic history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال فنقول : لا شك أن موسى عليه السلام تأخر وجودًا عن آدم ونوح وهود وصالح وإبراهيم وإسماعيل ويعقوب، وجماعة من الأنبياء والأسباط عليهم السلام، أَهَلْ كان جميع ما ورد به موسى مشروعًا لهم أو منها ما كان مشروعًا، ومنها ما لم يكن مشروعًا، فإن كان كله مشروعًا لهم فلم يرد موسى بشريعة
Then he said: We say: There is no doubt that Musa (AS) existed later than Adam, Nuh, Hud, Salih, Ibrahim, Isma'il, Ya'qub, and a group of prophets and tribes (AS). Was all that Musa brought legislated for them, or some of it was legislated and some was not? If all of it was legislated for them, then Musa did not bring a Shari'ah
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage provides a historical argument to support the possibility of naskh. The author notes that Musa (AS) came after many prophets, including Adam, Nuh, Ibrahim, etc. He then poses a dichotomy: either the entire law brought by Musa was already legislated for those earlier prophets, or only part of it was. If all of it was already their law, then Musa did not actually bring a new Shari'ah (which contradicts his role as a lawgiving prophet). The missing conclusion would likely be that naskh must have occurred – some laws from earlier prophets were abrogated and replaced by Musa's law. The argument demonstrates that naskh is a necessary feature of prophetic history.

Key Terms
شريعة
أنبياء
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All prophets had identical laws.
Correction: The historical sequence of prophets like Musa and those before him shows that later prophets brought new laws that were not applicable to earlier communities, implying abrogation (naskh).

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the argument about Musa and earlier prophets?
A) Musa was the greatest prophet.
B) All prophets had the same law.
C) Musa's law could not have been entirely applicable to earlier prophets, implying naskh.
D) Earlier prophets did not have any law.

Key Takeaway
The existence of naskh is logically necessary from the historical order of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that if Musa brought nothing new, he wouldn't be a lawgiver, does that challenge your view of prophecy? Do you accept that God sends prophets with specific times and laws, and that following a later prophet may require leaving some practices of the earlier one? How does this affect your trust in the finality of the Prophet Muhammad's message?
?
The argument forces a logical choice: either all of Musa's law was already for earlier people (making him redundant) or some was new (implying abrogation). Which side do you find more convincing, and does it change how you view the continuity of God's guidance across history?
?
If naskh were impossible, what would that mean for the necessity of later prophets? Consider your own adherence to Islamic law—do you accept that God's commands can be tailored to different eras, and how does that shape your commitment to the Shari'ah today?

The Logical Foundation for Abrogation from Prophetic History
The logical dilemma establishing abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that proves abrogation must have occurred between Moses and earlier prophets.

Core Idea
If Moses brought any new ruling, abrogation is proven.

The author begins with a powerful logical argument: either all of Moses' law was already given to earlier prophets, or some of it was new. If all was pre-existing, Moses brought no new law, contradicting his prophethood. As soon as one new ruling is found, abrogation is established. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic dilemma
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the structure: a premise followed by a question and two possible answers.
The passage uses a logical 'either/or' construction.
2
Understand the two horns
Identify the two possibilities: either all of Moses' law was already legislated, or some of it was new. Consider the implications of each.
The first horn would make Moses a mere confirmer of previous laws.
3
See why any new ruling proves abrogation
If even one new ruling exists, it must have abrogated a previous ruling on the same matter. This establishes abrogation as a reality.
The author uses the term 'raf'' (lifting) to describe abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فنقول : لا شك أن موسى عليه السلام تأخر وجودًا عن آدم ونوح وهود وصالح وإبراهيم وإسماعيل ويعقوب، وجماعة من الأنبياء والأسباط عليهم السلام، أَهَلْ كان جميع ما ورد به موسى مشروعًا لهم أو منها ما كان مشروعًا ، ومنها ما لم يكن مشروعًا ، فإن كان كله مشروعًا لهم فلم يرد موسى بشريعة أصلا، بل قرر الشرائع الماضية، وإن ورد بحكم واحد غير ما ثبت في شرعهم فقد تحقق أن الحكم الأول مرفوع بذلك الحكم، أو قد انتهى مدة الحكم الأول وتجدد هذا الحكم فثبت النسخ وإن أنكروا ذلك فنكاح الأخوات في زمن آدم عليه السلام حتى قيل : إنه استمر ذلك الحكم إلى زمن نوح مما يجب الاعتراف به ليتحقق النسل والذرية، وتوبة عبدة العجل بقتل أنفسهم كان حكمًا لم يكن قبل ذلك وصار مرفوعًا عن غيرهم في التوراة. والختان في اليوم السابع من الولادة حكم لم يكن لنوح وغيره، بل ترك الختان كان مشروعًا لهم، وأمر بذلك إبراهيم عليه السلام على الكبر، وأطلق له أن لا يختتن إسماعيل في طفولته حتى يصير مراهقا وحظر على موسى ترك الختان فوق سبعة أيام والتمسك بالسبت ما كان واجبًا على الأنبياء قبل موسى عليه السلام، ثم صار واجبًا على موسى، فما المستحيل أن يعود إلى ما كان مباحا ، وكذلك الصيد في يوم السبت تو جميع الأفعال الدنياوية ) كان مباحا قبل موسى عليه السلام، فصار محظورًا على موسى، والمواعدة من أدل الدلائل على النسخ، إذ واعده الميقات ثلاثين ليلة ثم أتمها بعشر فتم ميقات ربه أربعين ليلة ، وكذلك تلك التكاليف الشديدة على بني إسرائيل ما كانت واجبة على من قبلهم.
Then we say: There is no doubt that Moses, peace be upon him, came later in existence than Adam, Noah, Hud, Salih, Abraham, Ishmael, Jacob, and a group of prophets and tribes, peace be upon them. Was all that Moses brought legislated for them, or were some of it legislated and some not? If all of it was legislated for them, then Moses did not bring a new law at all, but rather confirmed the previous laws. But if he brought a single ruling different from what was established in their law, then it is confirmed that the first ruling is lifted by that ruling, or the period of the first ruling ended and this ruling renewed, so abrogation is established. And if they deny that, then the marriage of sisters in the time of Adam, peace be upon him, until it is said that this ruling continued until the time of Noah, is something that must be acknowledged for the realization of progeny and offspring. And the repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling that did not exist before and became lifted from others in the Torah. And circumcision on the seventh day after birth was a ruling that did not exist for Noah and others; rather, leaving circumcision was legislated for them, and Abraham was commanded with it in old age, and he was permitted not to circumcise Ishmael in his childhood until he became a youth, and Moses was prohibited from leaving circumcision after seven days. And adherence to the Sabbath was not obligatory upon the prophets before Moses, peace be upon him, then it became obligatory upon Moses. So what is impossible about its returning to being permissible? Likewise, hunting on the Sabbath and all worldly actions were permissible before Moses, then became prohibited for Moses. And the appointment [at the mountain] is among the clearest proofs of abrogation, for He appointed with him a meeting of thirty nights, then completed them with ten, so the meeting with his Lord became forty nights. And likewise those severe obligations upon the Children of Israel were not obligatory upon those before them.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an empirical/historical argument for the occurrence of abrogation (naskh). The author presents a series of examples from the history of prophets to demonstrate that laws changed over time, proving abrogation is a reality. The reasoning begins with a logical dilemma: either all of Moses' law was already legislated for previous prophets (making Moses a mere confirmer) or at least one ruling was new. If it was all pre-existing, then Moses brought no new law, which contradicts his status as a law-giving prophet. Thus, there must be at least one new ruling, which abrogates the previous one. Then the author lists specific examples: (1) Marriage of sisters in the time of Adam (to enable reproduction) continued until Noah; this ruling was later abrogated. (2) The repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling specific to that incident and later lifted. (3) Circumcision on the seventh day was not required for Noah but was commanded for Abraham, and later Moses forbade delaying beyond seven days. (4) The Sabbath was not obligatory before Moses, then became obligatory for him, and could later return to being permissible. (5) Hunting and worldly activities on the Sabbath were permissible before Moses, then prohibited for him. (6) The appointment at the mountain: initially thirty nights, then completed to forty. (7) The severe obligations on the Children of Israel were not required of earlier communities. All these examples show that divine laws change over time, which is the essence of abrogation. The author concludes that abrogation is not only rationally possible but historically attested.

Key Terms
النسخ
شريعة
موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think abrogation is impossible because God's knowledge is eternal and His decrees cannot change.
Correction: The author shows that abrogation is proven by historical fact, not merely rational possibility. The examples from prophets demonstrate that laws did change over time.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if Moses brought even one new ruling?
A) Moses confirmed previous laws
B) Abrogation is established
C) The earlier ruling was invalid
D) Moses was not a prophet

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is historically proven through the logical necessity that Moses must have introduced at least one new ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof forces you to choose: either Moses was a mere repeater or he introduced something new. Have you ever treated a prophet's message as just repeating what came before? How does this dilemma change how you see the unique role of each prophet?
?
Abrogation means Allah's rulings change with time and wisdom. When you struggle to follow a difficult command in the Quran, do you trust that its wisdom is perfect for your time? What specific ruling do you need to submit to today, knowing it abrogates previous human customs?
?
The proof hinges on the sequence of prophets. Do you live as if the final revelation has truly abrogated all previous laws and norms? Is there an area of your life where you still follow a 'previous law' (culture, habit, personal preference) over the Quran? What step will you take this week to align fully with the final sharia?

Historical Examples of Abrogation: Early Prophets
Historical examples from Adam, Noah, and Abraham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite specific examples from Adam, Noah, and Abraham that demonstrate changes in divine law.

Core Idea
Examples from Adam to Abraham show that divine laws changed over time.

After establishing the logical principle, the author provides concrete historical examples. These examples from the lives of early prophets serve as empirical evidence that abrogation actually occurred. This lesson examines the examples of the marriage of sisters, the repentance of the calf-worshippers, and circumcision.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. There are three distinct examples. Note the specific prophets and the changes described.
The examples are: marriage of sisters, repentance of calf-worshippers, and circumcision.
2
Understand each change
For each example, identify what the ruling was before and what it became after. How does this show abrogation?
Note that the marriage of sisters was allowed for Adam but later prohibited.
3
See how they prove abrogation
Each example illustrates a clear change in a specific ruling. This directly demonstrates that divine laws can and do change.
These are not just hypotheticals; they are historical events accepted by Jews and Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فنقول : لا شك أن موسى عليه السلام تأخر وجودًا عن آدم ونوح وهود وصالح وإبراهيم وإسماعيل ويعقوب، وجماعة من الأنبياء والأسباط عليهم السلام، أَهَلْ كان جميع ما ورد به موسى مشروعًا لهم أو منها ما كان مشروعًا ، ومنها ما لم يكن مشروعًا ، فإن كان كله مشروعًا لهم فلم يرد موسى بشريعة أصلا، بل قرر الشرائع الماضية، وإن ورد بحكم واحد غير ما ثبت في شرعهم فقد تحقق أن الحكم الأول مرفوع بذلك الحكم، أو قد انتهى مدة الحكم الأول وتجدد هذا الحكم فثبت النسخ وإن أنكروا ذلك فنكاح الأخوات في زمن آدم عليه السلام حتى قيل : إنه استمر ذلك الحكم إلى زمن نوح مما يجب الاعتراف به ليتحقق النسل والذرية، وتوبة عبدة العجل بقتل أنفسهم كان حكمًا لم يكن قبل ذلك وصار مرفوعًا عن غيرهم في التوراة. والختان في اليوم السابع من الولادة حكم لم يكن لنوح وغيره، بل ترك الختان كان مشروعًا لهم، وأمر بذلك إبراهيم عليه السلام على الكبر، وأطلق له أن لا يختتن إسماعيل في طفولته حتى يصير مراهقا وحظر على موسى ترك الختان فوق سبعة أيام والتمسك بالسبت ما كان واجبًا على الأنبياء قبل موسى عليه السلام، ثم صار واجبًا على موسى، فما المستحيل أن يعود إلى ما كان مباحا ، وكذلك الصيد في يوم السبت تو جميع الأفعال الدنياوية ) كان مباحا قبل موسى عليه السلام، فصار محظورًا على موسى، والمواعدة من أدل الدلائل على النسخ، إذ واعده الميقات ثلاثين ليلة ثم أتمها بعشر فتم ميقات ربه أربعين ليلة ، وكذلك تلك التكاليف الشديدة على بني إسرائيل ما كانت واجبة على من قبلهم.
Then we say: There is no doubt that Moses, peace be upon him, came later in existence than Adam, Noah, Hud, Salih, Abraham, Ishmael, Jacob, and a group of prophets and tribes, peace be upon them. Was all that Moses brought legislated for them, or were some of it legislated and some not? If all of it was legislated for them, then Moses did not bring a new law at all, but rather confirmed the previous laws. But if he brought a single ruling different from what was established in their law, then it is confirmed that the first ruling is lifted by that ruling, or the period of the first ruling ended and this ruling renewed, so abrogation is established. And the repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling that did not exist before and became lifted from others in the Torah. And circumcision on the seventh day after birth was a ruling that did not exist for Noah and others; rather, leaving circumcision was legislated for them, and Abraham was commanded with it in old age, and he was permitted not to circumcise Ishmael in his childhood until he became a youth, and Moses was prohibited from leaving circumcision after seven days. And adherence to the Sabbath was not obligatory upon the prophets before Moses, peace be upon him, then it became obligatory upon Moses. So what is impossible about its returning to being permissible? Likewise, hunting on the Sabbath and all worldly actions were permissible before Moses, then became prohibited for Moses. And likewise those severe obligations upon the Children of Israel were not obligatory upon those before them.

The author presents a series of examples from the history of prophets to demonstrate that laws changed over time, proving abrogation is a reality. The reasoning begins with a logical dilemma: either all of Moses' law was already legislated for previous prophets (making Moses a mere confirmer) or at least one ruling was new. If it was all pre-existing, then Moses brought no new law, which contradicts his status as a law-giving prophet. Thus, there must be at least one new ruling, which abrogates the previous one. Then the author lists specific examples: (1) Marriage of sisters in the time of Adam (to enable reproduction) continued until Noah; this ruling was later abrogated. (2) The repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling specific to that incident and later lifted. (3) Circumcision on the seventh day was not required for Noah but was commanded for Abraham, and later Moses forbade delaying beyond seven days. (4) The Sabbath was not obligatory before Moses, then became obligatory for him, and could later return to being permissible. (5) Hunting and worldly activities on the Sabbath were permissible before Moses, then prohibited for him. (6) The appointment at the mountain: initially thirty nights, then completed to forty. (7) The severe obligations on the Children of Israel were not required of earlier communities. All these examples show that divine laws change over time, which is the essence of abrogation. The author concludes that abrogation is not only rationally possible but historically attested.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all prophets followed the exact same set of laws.
Correction: These examples show that different prophets had different laws, proving that divine law can change.

Checkpoint
Which example shows a law that was allowed for Adam but later prohibited?
A) Marriage of sisters
B) Circumcision
C) Sabbath
D) Repentance of calf-worshippers

Key Takeaway
Historical examples from early prophets confirm that divine laws changed over time, proving abrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah allowed something in the past for a wise purpose and later prohibited it, are you willing to submit fully to His changing commands, even if they contradict your desires or cultural norms? What specific command do you find hardest to accept, and why?
?
The calf-worshippers had to sacrifice their lives for repentance. Is there a sin in your life that you cling to so tightly that you would rather face its consequences than truly repent? Are you ready to 'kill' that attachment today?
?
Abraham obeyed the command of circumcision in old age, despite the difficulty. What command of Allah have you been delaying because of your age or circumstances? What would it take for you to obey immediately?

Examples of Abrogation from Moses' Law
Examples from Moses' law showing abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how changes in Sabbath obligations, hunting, and the mountain appointment demonstrate abrogation during Moses' time.

Core Idea
The Sabbath and other laws were not always obligatory; they changed with Moses.

The author continues with examples from Moses' own law, showing that even within his prophethood, changes occurred. The Sabbath, hunting, and the appointment at the mountain all illustrate that God's laws are not static but can be superseded. This lesson examines these examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples about Sabbath and hunting
Read the passage. Note that the Sabbath was not obligatory before Moses, then became so. Also hunting on Sabbath was permissible then prohibited.
The author asks rhetorically: why can't it return to being permissible?
2
Understand the mountain appointment
The appointment was initially thirty nights, then extended to forty. This shows a change in God's own decree.
This is presented as one of the clearest proofs.
3
Connect to severe obligations on Israelites
The severe obligations placed on the Children of Israel were not required of earlier communities, showing a change in the level of requirement.
This reinforces the pattern of changing laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فنقول : لا شك أن موسى عليه السلام تأخر وجودًا عن آدم ونوح وهود وصالح وإبراهيم وإسماعيل ويعقوب، وجماعة من الأنبياء والأسباط عليهم السلام، أَهَلْ كان جميع ما ورد به موسى مشروعًا لهم أو منها ما كان مشروعًا ، ومنها ما لم يكن مشروعًا ، فإن كان كله مشروعًا لهم فلم يرد موسى بشريعة أصلا، بل قرر الشرائع الماضية، وإن ورد بحكم واحد غير ما ثبت في شرعهم فقد تحقق أن الحكم الأول مرفوع بذلك الحكم، أو قد انتهى مدة الحكم الأول وتجدد هذا الحكم فثبت النسخ وإن أنكروا ذلك فنكاح الأخوات في زمن آدم عليه السلام حتى قيل : إنه استمر ذلك الحكم إلى زمن نوح مما يجب الاعتراف به ليتحقق النسل والذرية، وتوبة عبدة العجل بقتل أنفسهم كان حكمًا لم يكن قبل ذلك وصار مرفوعًا عن غيرهم في التوراة. والختان في اليوم السابع من الولادة حكم لم يكن لنوح وغيره، بل ترك الختان كان مشروعًا لهم، وأمر بذلك إبراهيم عليه السلام على الكبر، وأطلق له أن لا يختتن إسماعيل في طفولته حتى يصير مراهقا وحظر على موسى ترك الختان فوق سبعة أيام والتمسك بالسبت ما كان واجبًا على الأنبياء قبل موسى عليه السلام، ثم صار واجبًا على موسى، فما المستحيل أن يعود إلى ما كان مباحا ، وكذلك الصيد في يوم السبت تو جميع الأفعال الدنياوية ) كان مباحا قبل موسى عليه السلام، فصار محظورًا على موسى، والمواعدة من أدل الدلائل على النسخ، إذ واعده الميقات ثلاثين ليلة ثم أتمها بعشر فتم ميقات ربه أربعين ليلة ، وكذلك تلك التكاليف الشديدة على بني إسرائيل ما كانت واجبة على من قبلهم.
Then we say: There is no doubt that Moses, peace be upon him, came later in existence than Adam, Noah, Hud, Salih, Abraham, Ishmael, Jacob, and a group of prophets and tribes, peace be upon them. Was all that Moses brought legislated for them, or were some of it legislated and some not? If all of it was legislated for them, then Moses did not bring a new law at all, but rather confirmed the previous laws. But if he brought a single ruling different from what was established in their law, then it is confirmed that the first ruling is lifted by that ruling, or the period of the first ruling ended and this ruling renewed, so abrogation is established. And if they deny that, then the marriage of sisters in the time of Adam, peace be upon him, until it is said that this ruling continued until the time of Noah, is something that must be acknowledged for the realization of progeny and offspring. And the repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling that did not exist before and became lifted from others in the Torah. And circumcision on the seventh day after birth was a ruling that did not exist for Noah and others; rather, leaving circumcision was legislated for them, and Abraham was commanded with it in old age, and he was permitted not to circumcise Ishmael in his childhood until he became a youth, and Moses was prohibited from leaving circumcision after seven days. And adherence to the Sabbath was not obligatory upon the prophets before Moses, peace be upon him, then it became obligatory upon Moses. So what is impossible about its returning to being permissible? Likewise, hunting on the Sabbath and all worldly actions were permissible before Moses, then became prohibited for Moses. And the appointment [at the mountain] is among the clearest proofs of abrogation, for He appointed with him a meeting of thirty nights, then completed them with ten, so the meeting with his Lord became forty nights. And likewise those severe obligations upon the Children of Israel were not obligatory upon those before them.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an empirical/historical argument for the occurrence of abrogation (naskh). The author presents a series of examples from the history of prophets to demonstrate that laws changed over time, proving abrogation is a reality. The reasoning begins with a logical dilemma: either all of Moses' law was already legislated for previous prophets (making Moses a mere confirmer) or at least one ruling was new. If it was all pre-existing, then Moses brought no new law, which contradicts his status as a law-giving prophet. Thus, there must be at least one new ruling, which abrogates the previous one. Then the author lists specific examples: (1) Marriage of sisters in the time of Adam (to enable reproduction) continued until Noah; this ruling was later abrogated. (2) The repentance of the calf-worshippers by killing themselves was a ruling specific to that incident and later lifted. (3) Circumcision on the seventh day was not required for Noah but was commanded for Abraham, and later Moses forbade delaying beyond seven days. (4) The Sabbath was not obligatory before Moses, then became obligatory for him, and could later return to being permissible. (5) Hunting and worldly activities on the Sabbath were permissible before Moses, then prohibited for him. (6) The appointment at the mountain: initially thirty nights, then completed to forty. (7) The severe obligations on the Children of Israel were not required of earlier communities. All these examples show that divine laws change over time, which is the essence of abrogation. The author concludes that abrogation is not only rationally possible but historically attested.

السبت
الصيد
الميقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that once a law is given, it cannot be changed or abrogated.
Correction: The example of the mountain appointment shows that even a divinely set time can be extended, demonstrating that God can change His rulings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is presented as a proof of abrogation from Moses' own time?
A) The extension of the mountain appointment from 30 to 40 nights
B) The prohibition of adultery
C) The obligation of fasting
D) The sacrifice of animals

Key Takeaway
Changes in Sabbath, hunting, and the mountain appointment confirm that abrogation occurred within Moses' prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah can change His commands even for a single prophet, are you holding onto any religious practice or interpretation as if it were eternal, while forgetting that Allah's will supersedes all? How will you cultivate readiness to submit to whatever He prescribes, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
The duration of Musa's appointment changed from 30 to 40 nights without protest. When your own plans—set by Allah—are extended or altered without explanation, do you accept it as His absolute wisdom, or do you resent the change? What can you learn from Musa's silence?
?
Consider a command from Allah that you find difficult to follow today. If He were to abrogate it tomorrow, would your obedience have been worthless? Or does each command carry its own reward? How does this lesson reshape your motivation to obey in the present moment?
